About This Guidebook

Why This Guidebook Exists

This guidebook was written for people outside government who want to get inside.

Other BARMM guidebooks – on Complete Staff Work, supervision, bill drafting, budget briefing – are written for people already inside the Bangsamoro government. Those guidebooks assume you know what a memorandum looks like, what “CSW” means, and how the approval chain works. They coach you as a peer.

This guidebook assumes none of that.

This guidebook exists because the Bangsamoro government needs you – and you deserve a clear, honest guide to help you decide whether to join, how to apply, and how to succeed once you are inside.

The Bangsamoro Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao has 50,467 authorized positions across 15 ministries and 13 executive offices (as reported in BDP Chapter 5 on Governance, November 2022). Thousands remain vacant. The region has the largest land area in the Philippines, the youngest population, and the widest development gaps. It needs engineers, teachers, nurses, planners, budget analysts, lawyers, IT specialists, social workers, and administrative professionals.

It needs you.

Who This Guidebook Is For

Primary Audience: Fresh Graduates

You have completed college but have not yet been employed, or you are in your first job search. You have academic knowledge – a thesis, a capstone project, leadership experience in student organizations – but zero government experience. You may not know what “BTA” stands for, what a plantilla position is, or how the Comparative Assessment works.

This guidebook will teach you all of that.

Secondary Audience: Experienced Professionals

You have worked in the private sector, NGOs, international organizations, or other government agencies, and you want to join or transfer to the Bangsamoro government. You have work experience but may not know BARMM-specific systems – the Bangsamoro Civil Service Code (BAA 17), the Bangsamoro Administrative Code (BAA 13), or the 12 Pillars of Moral Governance.

This guidebook will bridge that gap.

AI Declaration

This guidebook was produced with AI assistance. The author used AI tools (Claude Code, Anthropic) for drafting institutional analyses, structuring career pathway frameworks, formatting legal references to the Bangsamoro Civil Service Code, and organizing application process guides.

AI did not design the methodology. The institutional analysis of the Bangsamoro government, the career track frameworks, the Complete Staff Work methodology, the competency models, and the ethical foundations of Bangsamoro public service come from the author’s professional experience in Bangsamoro governance – not from AI-generated suggestions. AI accelerated the writing. It did not direct the thinking.

Every legal citation, institutional fact, and governance claim in this guidebook was verified by the author against the Bangsamoro Organic Law (RA 11054), enacted Bangsamoro Autonomy Acts (including BAA 13 and BAA 17), the Bangsamoro Development Plan 2023-2028, and official government records. The author bears full responsibility for the accuracy and completeness of the published content.

For the author’s full professional standards on AI-augmented governance, legislation, and development work, see How to Use AI in Governance, Legislation, and Development Work in the Bangsamoro (Saidamen R. Mambayao, 2026).

How to Use This Guidebook

This guidebook is organized into four parts. You can read it cover to cover, or you can go directly to the part that matches where you are in your journey.

Part I: Understanding the Bangsamoro Government (Chapters 1-6)

Read this first. Before you apply, understand what you are joining. These six chapters give you the foundation: why the government needs you, the Bangsamoro story, how the government is organized, your employment framework under BAA 17, the Bangsamoro Development Plan, and the four career tracks you can choose from.

If you are a fresh graduate with no government background, Part I is essential. Do not skip it.

Part II: Getting the Job (Chapters 7-9)

Read this when you are ready to apply. These three chapters are the most practical in the guidebook. Chapter 7 tells you where to find job openings. Chapter 8 walks you through the application process step by step. Chapter 9 prepares you for the interview and assessment. A fresh graduate should be able to read Part II, follow the steps, and submit a competitive application without outside help.

Part III: Succeeding in Government (Chapters 10-16)

Read this when you get the job. These seven chapters prepare you for your first day and beyond. You will learn Complete Staff Work (the methodology every government worker uses), the competencies that determine your career trajectory, how to work with your supervisor, how to write government documents, the employee lifecycle from onboarding to promotion, career growth and development pathways, and the ethical foundations of Bangsamoro public service.

Part IV: Reference (Chapter 17, Glossary, and Appendices)

Use this anytime. Chapter 17 answers the 33 most frequently asked questions – from “Do I need Civil Service eligibility?” to “I am not Muslim – can I still work in BARMM government?” The Glossary defines every acronym, legal term, and institutional term used in this guidebook. The Appendices provide detachable tools: checklists, worksheets, templates, and quick reference cards you can use throughout your career.

Conventions Used in This Guidebook

Throughout this guidebook, you will encounter the following:

  • Bold terms indicate key vocabulary you should learn. Most are defined in the Glossary.
  • Tables and checklists are designed for practical use. Print them, fill them in, and bring them to your interviews.
  • “Actionable Tool” sections at the end of each chapter provide worksheets, self-assessments, and reference cards you can detach and use.
  • Cross-references (e.g., “See Chapter 8, Section 8.3”) point you to related content elsewhere in this guidebook.
  • Legal citations (e.g., BAA 17, RA 6713) refer to specific laws. You do not need to read these laws before starting – the guidebook explains what you need to know.
  • Islamic terms (e.g., amanah, shura) are governance values rooted in Islamic tradition. Each is defined where it first appears and in the Glossary. These values apply to all staff regardless of personal faith.

About the Author

Saidamen “Sayeed” R. Mambayao has worked in governance, legislation, and cooperative development across the Bangsamoro region for over a decade.

Before the establishment of the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region, he served as a Consultant of the Cooperative Development Authority under the former Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao, where he led programs on cooperative development, Islamic finance, and executive leadership. He also served as Secretariat Head and Program Director of the Bangsamoro Executives and Leaders League (BELL), Inc., and held leadership roles in the ARMM Islamic Finance Sub-Cluster and the Bangsamoro Coalition on Social Enterprise.

When the Bangsamoro Transition Authority was established in 2019, he led the development and implementation of two BARMM flagship programs: the Access to Higher and Modern Education (AHME) Scholarship Program under the Ministry of Basic, Higher, and Technical Education, and the Engaging the SK, Empowering the Youth (ESKEY) Program under the Ministry of the Interior and Local Government. He later served as Chief of Staff at the Office of a Member of Parliament, where he assisted on legislative portfolios covering women, youth, children, persons with disabilities, cooperatives, social enterprises, and halal industries. He drafted and reviewed legislative measures on scholarships, cultural heritage preservation, assistance to Other Bangsamoro Communities, and cooperative and social enterprise development. He also served as OIC-Legislative Affairs at the Office of MP Sittie Fahanie S. Uy-Oyod.

He is the Founder of MoroTech and MoroAcademy, social enterprises empowering Bangsamoro organizations and communities through knowledge and technology, and the Founding Chairperson of the Bangsamoro Scholars Association. He also founded the SEED Initiative, a consultancy and training firm focused on cooperative and social enterprise development. He is an Active Citizen and graduate of the British Council’s CSO-SEED Business and Investment Readiness Program.

Introduction

Why the Bangsamoro Government Needs You

The Bangsamoro government has 50,467 authorized positions1 — and it needs more. It governs the largest land area in the Philippines, serves one of the youngest populations in the country, and faces the widest development gaps of any region. If you want to build something from the ground up, this is the last frontier.

This chapter is not about history or organizational charts. Those come later. This chapter is about a simple question: Why should a fresh graduate or job seeker choose the Bangsamoro government?

The answer is in the numbers, in the staffing gaps, and in the kind of work you will do here that you cannot do anywhere else.

1.1 The Development Gap — In Numbers

The gap between where BARMM is today and where the 2nd Bangsamoro Development Plan (BDP) 2023-2028 says it should be is the reason you are needed. These numbers are not abstract. Each one represents communities waiting for services, families without clean water, children leaving school too early.

Indicator BARMM (Current) National Average BDP Target (2028)
Poverty incidence 29.80% (2021) ~16% 20-25%
Functional literacy rate 71.6% 91.6% Near national
Dropout rate (G1-G6) 8.36% 3.76% Below national
WASH access (safe water) Lowest in PH — Universal
Maternal mortality rate 73.83 per 100,000 live births (2020) 70.66 per 100,000 Below national
GRDP contribution to national 1.4% — Proportional
Underemployment rate 9.0% (2020) — 4-7%

Sources: All indicators from the 2nd BDP 2023-2028, Chapter 4.2 Staffing data from Chapter 5.1

Look at these numbers together. BARMM’s functional literacy rate trails the national average by 20 percentage points. The dropout rate is more than double the national figure. Safe water access ranks 17th out of 17 regions — dead last. Maternal mortality sits above the national average. And despite having the largest land area in the country, BARMM contributes only 1.4% to national GDP — the lowest of any region.

Education

The scale of the education gap is staggering. Out of 2,590 barangays (BDP 2023-2028 figure; excludes adjustments from Sulu’s exclusion from BARMM per SC ruling), 104 remain unserved or underserved — meaning children in those communities have no nearby school or an incomplete school. Only 7% of 1,679 madrasahs (Islamic schools) are accredited. There are not enough teachers for Grades 1 through 12. The completion rate from Grade 1 to Grade 6 sits at 63.98% — the lowest in the Philippines.3

What this means for you: if you have a degree in education, curriculum development, or any social science, the Ministry of Basic, Higher, and Technical Education (MBHTE) needs you. The gap is not just about buildings. It is about people — teachers, planners, curriculum developers, and education administrators.

Health

BARMM has the highest rates of stunting, wasting, and underweight children in the Philippines. There are not enough doctors, nurses, or midwives to serve the population. Psychosocial and mental health services barely exist at the community level. Provincial and district hospitals operate below capacity — not because of low demand, but because of insufficient personnel and equipment.

The maternal mortality rate sits at 73.83 per 100,000 live births (2020) — above the national average of 70.66.6 Behind that number are mothers in island barangays of Tawi-Tawi and Basilan who give birth without a skilled attendant. Behind it are health stations that have buildings but no midwife assigned.

What this means for you: if you have a degree in nursing, midwifery, public health, nutrition, or any allied health field, the Ministry of Health (MOH) needs you — at provincial hospitals, district health offices, and community health stations across all five provinces and Cotabato City.

Infrastructure

Basic water and sanitation access ranks last nationally across all indicators. BARMM’s WASH (water, sanitation, and hygiene) situation is the worst in the country — ranked 17th out of 17 regions. Roads in island provinces remain underdeveloped. Connectivity between mainland BARMM (Lanao del Sur, Maguindanao del Norte, Maguindanao del Sur) and island provinces (Basilan, Tawi-Tawi) depends on sea routes and limited air service.

The infrastructure backlog is not just about convenience. A road that washes out during rainy season means children cannot reach school. A water system that breaks means families drink unsafe water. A seaport that cannot handle cargo means food prices in island municipalities are double the mainland rate.

The Ministry of Public Works (MPW) and the Ministry of Transportation and Communications (MOTC) need engineers, project managers, construction inspectors, and infrastructure planners. If you have a degree in civil engineering, architecture, geodetic engineering, or project management, the infrastructure gap is where your skills translate directly into community impact.

Economy

Here is the paradox: BARMM is the number one producer of seaweed (48.42% national share) and cassava (41.70% national share).7 It has vast agricultural land, marine resources, a strategic position in the BIMP-EAGA (Brunei-Indonesia-Malaysia-Philippines East ASEAN Growth Area) corridor, and a young labor force. Yet its contribution to national GDP is the lowest of any region at 1.4%.

The gap is not in resources. It is in people — the market analysts, the agricultural economists, the trade specialists, the investment officers, the tourism planners who can turn raw resources into economic value. The Ministry of Trade, Investments, and Tourism (MTIT), the Ministry of Agriculture, Fisheries, and Agrarian Reform (MAFAR), and the Bangsamoro Board of Investments (BBOI) all need professionals who can help close this gap.

Social Welfare

The Ministry of Social Services and Development (MSSD) manages social protection programs for the most vulnerable communities — persons with disabilities, solo parents, displaced families, senior citizens, and children in need of special protection. BARMM’s poverty incidence at 29.80% means nearly one in three families lives below the poverty line. MSSD implements livelihood programs, humanitarian assistance, and social welfare services across the region, but its staff-to-beneficiary ratio remains stretched thin.

If you have a degree in social work, community development, psychology, or any social science, MSSD offers a career path where your work directly reaches the most underserved communities in the Philippines.

1.2 The Staffing Reality

The numbers tell the staffing story clearly:

  • 50,467 authorized positions across 15 ministries and 13 executive offices1
  • 35,812 positions filled — meaning 14,655 positions remain vacant1
  • 600,000+ job applications received through the Bangsamoro Job Portal1 — but many applicants lack required qualifications or eligibility
  • 15 line ministries need staff across all functional areas: policy, planning, budget, administration, IT, legal, and technical
  • 10 attached agencies under the Office of the Chief Minister (BPDA, BBOI, BIO, BICTO, OSC, OOBC, BPA, DAB, BDI, CSEA) — many are newly created and still building teams
  • 4 commissions (BWC, BYC, BSC, BCPCH) need specialized staff in gender, youth, sports, and cultural heritage
  • 116 municipalities and 2,590 barangays (BDP 2023-2028 figures; exclude adjustments from Sulu’s exclusion from BARMM per SC ruling) need frontline service delivery workers

The gap between 600,000+ applicants and 14,655 vacancies is misleading. Many applicants lack Civil Service eligibility, do not meet qualification standards, or apply for positions that do not match their credentials. The government does not need just warm bodies. It needs qualified, eligible, motivated people who understand the mission.

What does “qualified” mean in practice? Chapter 4 covers this in detail, but the short version: for professional positions (SG 11 and above), you typically need a bachelor’s degree and Career Service Professional eligibility (obtained by passing the CSC exam or through equivalent eligibility for licensed professionals). For some technical positions, you need a degree in a specific field — engineering for MPW, agriculture for MAFAR, nursing for MOH.

The eligibility requirement alone eliminates many applicants. If you have a college degree and CSC eligibility (or a professional license that grants equivalent eligibility), you are already ahead of most of the 600,000+ applicants. If you do not yet have eligibility, Chapter 4 explains how to get it.

The Coterminous and COS Reality

Not all government positions are permanent. A significant number of staff in BARMM work under coterminous appointments (tied to the appointing authority’s term) or Contracts of Service (COS). These arrangements offer less job security but provide a way into the system. Many permanent employees started as COS workers, proved their competence, and were later appointed to plantilla positions when vacancies opened.

Understanding this landscape prepares you for realistic expectations. You may not land a permanent position on your first try. But getting into the system — even through COS or a temporary appointment — gives you insider knowledge, professional networks, and demonstrated experience that strengthens future applications.

Where Staff Are Needed Most Urgently

Office/Ministry Why Example Positions
MBHTE (Education) Lowest literacy, dropout, and completion rates nationally Teachers, curriculum developers, education planners
MOH (Health) Maternal mortality above national average, highest stunting rates Nurses, midwives, health program officers, nutritionists
MAFAR (Agriculture) Largest land area, food security gaps, 110,513 ha of undistributed land16 Agricultural technicians, agrarian reform officers
BPDA (Planning) Coordinates the entire BDP — needs analysts and M&E staff Development planners, statisticians, M&E officers
MFBM (Finance) Manages PHP 80B+ annual block grant17 — needs financial controls Budget analysts, accountants, procurement officers
MPW (Public Works) Infrastructure backlog across 5 provinces + Cotabato City Engineers, project managers, construction inspectors
Parliament Secretariat Supports 77 MPs across all committees Legislative staff officers, political affairs officers, media officers

This table is your starting map. Find the office whose mission matches your degree and your motivation. That is where you begin.

The Demand for Generalists

The table above highlights technical roles, but the government also needs generalists — people with degrees in public administration, political science, management, communications, information technology, or the liberal arts. Every ministry needs administrative officers, planning staff, IT support, records managers, and communications personnel. If your degree is not technical, you are not shut out. You simply enter through a different door: the administrative, planning, or communications track within any ministry or office.

Generalists are especially valued in the Parliament Secretariat (where legislative staff officers analyze policy across all subject areas), at BPDA (where planners coordinate across ministries), and in the commissions (BWC, BYC, BSC, BCPCH), where small teams handle diverse functions.

1.3 What Makes BARMM Different from Any Other Government Job

Working for the Bangsamoro government is not the same as working for a national government agency or a regional field office. Five things set it apart.

Autonomous Governance

BARMM is the only region in the Philippines with its own parliament, its own civil service code (BAA 17),8 its own administrative code (BAA 13),9 its own budget system (BAA 84),10 and its own education code (BAA 18).11 You are not joining a field office that follows Manila’s instructions. You are joining a government that makes its own laws.

“The Bangsamoro Autonomous Region is an integral, indivisible, and inseparable part of the territory of the Republic of the Philippines.”12 — Republic Act 11054, Article IV, Section 1

This means your work has direct legislative backing from the Bangsamoro Parliament — not just administrative orders from a department secretary in Manila.

Moral Governance

Every government claims to value ethics. What makes BARMM different is that its ethical framework is not borrowed from a generic good governance manual. It is rooted in the Bangsamoro people’s own values — informed by Islamic principles, shaped by centuries of governance tradition, and codified as official policy.

The 12 Pillars of Moral Governance4 are the ethical foundation of everything BARMM does. They are not aspirational slogans on a wall poster. They are cited in performance evaluations, referenced in legislation, and expected in daily conduct. The 12 pillars are:

  1. Integrity — avoid conflicts of interest
  2. Accountability — be open about decisions and actions
  3. Transparency and Honesty — declare private interests, resolve conflicts protecting the public
  4. Sense of Patriotism — invest in the Bangsamoro’s reputation
  5. Striving for Excellence — deliver the highest quality
  6. Cultural Understanding and Tolerance — appreciate diversity within the Bangsamoro
  7. Unwavering Loyalty to God — governance guided by devotion
  8. Justice — serve without prejudice
  9. Inclusivity — foster environments free from discrimination
  10. Objectivity — make merit-based decisions
  11. Dialogue and Meaningful Engagement — seek community opinion
  12. Balance Between Practical Life and Moral Side — never deviate from the ethical dimension

If you come from the private sector, this will feel unfamiliar. Government work anywhere has ethical standards, but BARMM’s moral governance framework is explicit, faith-informed, and culturally grounded in ways that are unique in Philippine governance.

Parliamentary System

BARMM is the only region in the Philippines with a parliamentary system. The Chief Minister leads the executive. The Parliament legislates and exercises oversight. This means legislative staff and executive staff work differently here than anywhere else in Philippine government. If you join the Parliament, you will draft bills, prepare legislative briefers, and support committee hearings. If you join a ministry, you will implement laws that the Parliament enacts. The two branches work in closer coordination than in a presidential system.

The Peace Process

You are not joining a regular bureaucracy. You are implementing the Comprehensive Agreement on the Bangsamoro (CAB)20 — a peace agreement that ended decades of armed conflict. Every program you run, every policy you draft, every barangay you serve is a peace dividend. The government’s legitimacy rests not just on elections or appointments, but on delivering the promises made in that agreement.

Cultural Diversity

BARMM is home to 13 ethnolinguistic groups: Meranaw, Maguindanaon, Tausug, Sama, Yakan, Iranun, Kalibugan, Palawanon, Molbog, Jama Mapun, Badjao, Sangil, and Teduray. There are also settler communities, Other Bangsamoro Communities, and indigenous peoples. You will work with colleagues and communities whose languages, customs, and perspectives differ from yours — sometimes dramatically.

Cultural sensitivity is not optional. It is a job requirement. A program that works in Maguindanao may fail in Tawi-Tawi because the community dynamics are different. A communication style that is respected in Lanao del Sur may be perceived differently in Basilan. Understanding these differences — or at least being humble enough to ask about them before assuming — is a core competency in BARMM government work.

The government institutionalizes this commitment to diversity through specific offices: the Office for Settler Communities (OSC), the Office for Other Bangsamoro Communities (OOBC), and the Ministry of Indigenous Peoples’ Affairs (MIPA). These offices exist because the Bangsamoro government recognizes that “Bangsamoro” is not a monolithic identity — it is a diverse community of communities, and governance must reflect that diversity.

1.4 What “Founder” Means

In a mature government, you inherit systems. Manuals are written. Templates exist. Precedents guide you. In BARMM, you build the systems yourself. The Bangsamoro government is barely seven years old. Here is what “founder” looks like in practice:

You may be the first. Your position may have existed on paper for years but never been filled. Your job description may be a paragraph, not a manual. You will define the role as you fill it.

You are the systems designer. Many offices do not yet have Manuals of Procedure (MOPs). You may be asked to create the filing system, the tracking sheet, the approval workflow, the template that everyone after you will use.

You set the standard. Your output quality becomes the benchmark. If you write excellent memos, your successors will follow your format. If you write poor ones, they will too. The first person to do something defines how it gets done.

You are the bridge. The BTA is transitional — appointed by the President. The first elected Bangsamoro government is coming. The systems you build during transition become the inheritance of that elected government. Your filing system, your tracking tool, your template — those survive you.

A real example: When the Bangsamoro Parliament began operations in 2019, there were no committee staffing templates, no standard legislative briefer format, no standardized bill-drafting manual. Staff who joined early created these tools from scratch — and those templates are still in use today. That is what “founder” means.

This is not unique to the Parliament. When MAFAR began implementing agrarian reform programs, staff had to design the application forms, the beneficiary screening criteria, and the distribution tracking systems. When BPDA launched the first Bangsamoro Development Plan, planners had to create the monitoring framework, the data collection templates, and the coordination mechanisms from scratch. Every ministry has a version of this story.

The founder advantage is real: if you join now and build well, you shape the institution. But the founder burden is also real: if you need someone to tell you what to do step by step, you will struggle. Government work in BARMM rewards initiative, resourcefulness, and a willingness to figure things out.

What “Founder” Does NOT Mean

Being a founder does not mean you work alone or without guidance. You will have supervisors, colleagues, and institutional frameworks. BAA 1318 defines your office’s mandate. BAA 1719 defines your employment terms. The BDP defines your targets. What is missing — and what you will help create — are the operational details: the workflows, the templates, the checklists, the standard operating procedures that turn mandates into daily practice.

It also does not mean that everything is broken. BARMM inherited some functioning systems from ARMM. Many national government processes (procurement, auditing, financial reporting) apply in BARMM. The challenge is adapting national systems to autonomous governance and building new systems where none existed.

1.5 The 12-Point Priority Agenda — Where You Fit

The Enhanced 12-Point Priority Agenda5 is the Chief Minister’s implementation framework within the BDP. Each priority maps to ministries and offices that need staff. Use this table to find where your skills and interests align.

# Priority Focus Offices Needing Staff
1 Bureaucracy Strengthen regional bureaucracy All MOAs — HR, admin, planning
2 Priority Codes Enact priority codes (5 enacted: Administrative BAA 13, Civil Service BAA 17, Education BAA 18, Electoral BAA 35,14 Local Governance BAA 49;15 remaining: Revenue, Investment) Parliament (legislative staff for drafting), Legal offices
3 Revenue Generation Increase revenue and economic advantages MFBM, BBOI, MTIT
4 Social Services Education, health, social welfare MBHTE, MOH, MSSD
5 Infrastructure Strategic infrastructure development MPW, MOTC
6 Economic Development Inclusive economic growth MTIT, MAFAR, MOLE
7 Environment Natural resources protection MENRE
8 Disaster Resilience Community resilience MILG, BRDRRMO
9 Peace and Normalization Peace agreement provisions OCM, MPOS
10 Marawi Rehabilitation Marawi City recovery Task Force Bangon Marawi, MHSD
11 Peace and Security Sustain peace, justice, security MPOS, BHRC
12 Cultural Heritage Preserve Bangsamoro culture BCPCH, MIPA

Notice how every priority connects to specific offices. If you care about education, Priority 4 leads you to MBHTE. If you care about the environment, Priority 7 leads you to MENRE. If you care about Marawi’s recovery, Priority 10 leads you to MHSD and Task Force Bangon Marawi. Your passion has an address in this government.

How to Use This Table

Start with the column that matches your motivation. If you wake up thinking about food security, start at Priority 6 (Economic Development) and look at MAFAR. If you wake up thinking about cultural preservation, start at Priority 12 and look at BCPCH and MIPA. Then move to Chapter 3, where you will learn the detailed organizational structure of each office, and Chapter 7, where you will learn how to find actual job openings in those offices.

The 12-Point Priority Agenda is also a useful interview tool. When an interviewer asks, “Why do you want to work here?” being able to say, “Your ministry supports Priority 4 of the Enhanced 12-Point Priority Agenda, and I want to contribute to closing the education gap” demonstrates that you understand the government’s strategic framework — not just the job listing.

1.7 The Youth Advantage

BARMM has one of the youngest populations in the Philippines. The youth bulge — a disproportionately large share of the population between ages 15 and 35 — is both a development challenge and an opportunity.

The challenge: Young people need jobs, education, and social services. If the government fails to provide these, the youth bulge becomes a source of instability rather than growth.

The opportunity: A young population means a growing workforce, fresh perspectives, and energy for institution-building. The Bangsamoro government needs young professionals who understand digital tools, data analysis, social media, and modern management practices — skills that older bureaucracies often lack.

As a fresh graduate, you bring exactly what this young government needs for its young population. You are not a liability because of your lack of experience. You are an asset because of your proximity to the communities, technologies, and ideas that the government must reach.

The Bangsamoro Youth Commission (BYC), created by BAA 10,13 exists specifically to advance youth development and participation. But every ministry needs young staff — not just the BYC. Your age is not a disadvantage here. It is a strategic fit.

1.8 The Geographic Opportunity

BARMM is not Cotabato City. The seat of government is in the mainland, but the region spans a vast and diverse territory:

  • Lanao del Sur — 39 municipalities surrounding Lake Lanao, including Marawi City
  • Maguindanao del Norte and del Sur — the agricultural heartland, home to the Bangsamoro Government Center
  • Basilan — an island province south of Zamboanga, with agriculture-based communities
  • Tawi-Tawi — the southernmost province of the Philippines, gateway to Southeast Asian neighbors
  • Cotabato City — the commercial hub and seat of government
  • 8 SGA municipalities — the newest municipalities, still establishing basic governance structures

Each location offers different work environments. Cotabato City has the most developed infrastructure and the highest concentration of government offices. Island provinces offer fewer amenities but more direct community impact. The SGA municipalities are literally being built from scratch — if you want the purest founder experience, that is where to find it.

Your willingness to work outside Cotabato City — especially in island provinces or SGA municipalities — dramatically increases your chances of getting hired. Many applicants prefer the comfort of the mainland. Offices in Basilan, Tawi-Tawi, and remote Lanao del Sur municipalities struggle to attract qualified staff. Supply and demand work in your favor if you are willing to go where the need is greatest.

1.6 Actionable Tool: “Is Government Work Right for Me?” Self-Assessment

Before you invest weeks preparing applications, take 10 minutes to check your fit. This is not a test — there are no right or wrong answers. It helps you understand whether Bangsamoro government work matches your current motivations, tolerance for ambiguity, and career expectations.

Instructions: Rate each statement from 1 (Strongly Disagree) to 5 (Strongly Agree). Be honest.

# Statement Score (1-5)
1 I care about the Bangsamoro people’s aspirations for self-governance and development — this is not just any job for me. ___
2 I am willing to work in areas outside Metro Manila or my hometown, including island provinces and conflict-affected communities. ___
3 I can work in a diverse environment with colleagues from different ethnolinguistic groups, religious backgrounds, and political perspectives. ___
4 I am comfortable with ambiguity — I can function even when there is no manual, no template, and no precedent. ___
5 I can accept that government salaries are lower than private sector for the same level of education and skill. ___
6 I am willing to learn government writing (memos, briefers, policy papers) even though it is very different from academic writing. ___
7 I can handle bureaucratic processes (approvals, endorsements, transmittals, compliance requirements) without frustration. ___
8 I am interested in policy, governance, public administration, or community development — not just any available job. ___
9 I understand that working in BARMM means implementing a peace agreement — and I take that responsibility seriously. ___
10 I am willing to start at an entry-level position (SG 11-15, approximately PHP 27,000-38,000/month) and build my career over years. ___
11 I can respect Islamic governance principles (moral governance, amanah, shura) even if my own background is different. ___
12 I am willing to develop new competencies on the job — Complete Staff Work, policy analysis, stakeholder engagement — through training and practice. ___

Scoring Guide

48-60 — Strong fit. You have the mindset and motivation for Bangsamoro public service. Read on — the rest of this guidebook will show you how to get there.

36-47 — Good potential. Some areas need reflection. Read Chapters 2 through 5 to build your understanding of the Bangsamoro context before applying. Pay attention to the areas where you scored below 3 — those are your growth edges.

24-35 — Mixed signals. Government work may not be what you expect. Read the entire Part I before deciding. The “Day-in-the-Life” profiles in Chapter 6 will give you a realistic picture of what government work actually looks like.

12-23 — Consider other paths. Government work — especially in BARMM — requires a level of commitment, adaptability, and willingness to accept trade-offs that may not match your current preferences. That is OK. Not every contribution to the Bangsamoro happens inside the government. The private sector, civil society, and development organizations also need talented people.

How to Use Your Score

Your total score is less telling than your pattern of low scores. If you scored 5 on most items but 1 on “I can accept government salaries,” that is a specific, solvable concern — you may decide the trade-off is worth it, or you may not. If you scored 2 or below on items 1, 8, and 9 (mission, interest in governance, and understanding of the peace process), the issue is motivation, not capability. No amount of preparation fixes a misaligned motivation.

Keep your self-assessment results. You will revisit them in Chapter 6 when you choose your career track, and in Chapter 8 when you prepare for interviews.

A Note About Item 5: Government Salaries

The salary question deserves honest discussion. A fresh graduate entering at SG 11 earns approximately PHP 27,000 per month in base pay. With allowances (COLA, PERA) and bonuses (mid-year, year-end, cash gift), the annualized compensation is better than the base number suggests. But it is still lower than what many private sector employers offer for graduates with the same degree.

The trade-offs are real: security of tenure (you cannot be fired without due process), retirement benefits through GSIS (which accumulate over decades), leave credits that monetize upon retirement, and the non-monetary value of public service. Some people find these trade-offs worthwhile. Others do not. There is no shame in either answer.

What you should not do is accept a government job resenting the salary. Resentment erodes performance, and poor performance in a founding government has consequences that outlast your tenure. If salary is a dealbreaker, the private sector, NGOs, and international development organizations operating in BARMM offer alternative paths to contribute — often at higher pay.

A Note About Item 4: Comfort with Ambiguity

This item deserves emphasis because it separates people who thrive in BARMM from people who struggle. In a mature bureaucracy — the Department of Finance in Manila, for example — every process has a manual, every form has a template, every question has a precedent. In BARMM, you will regularly face situations where no manual exists, no template has been created, and no one has done what you are about to do before.

If that prospect excites you — if you see it as a chance to create something — you will do well. If it terrifies you — if you need step-by-step instructions to function — BARMM government work will be frustrating. Neither reaction is wrong. But knowing your reaction before you apply saves time.

What Comes Next

Now you know the gap, the staffing reality, and what makes this government different. You have a self-assessment score that tells you how well your motivations align with the demands of Bangsamoro public service. You have a table showing where staff are needed most urgently. You have a framework — the 12-Point Priority Agenda — that connects your interests to specific offices.

The next chapter tells you how this government came to exist — the Bangsamoro story from struggle to self-governance. That history is not decoration. It shapes every policy, every institution, and every interaction you will have as a government worker. Colleagues will reference the peace process in casual conversation. Community members will connect their expectations to promises made during negotiations. Decision-makers will frame policy choices in terms of the BOL’s vision.

Understanding that history is not optional. It is the foundation on which everything else in this guidebook builds.

02
The Bangsamoro Story: From Struggle to Self-Governance

The Bangsamoro Story: From Struggle to Self-Governance

You are joining a government that was born from a peace agreement. That history shapes everything — every institution, every policy, every relationship between the government and its people. You do not need to become a historian, but you do need to understand why the Bangsamoro government exists, what it replaced, and what it promises.

This chapter gives you that foundation. It covers the story in three acts: the centuries-long struggle for self-determination, the peace process that led to the Bangsamoro Organic Law, and the transition period you are entering.

2.1 The Bangsamoro Struggle — A Brief, Necessary History

Before the Colonizers

Long before Spain arrived in the Philippines, the Bangsamoro homeland was organized into independent sultanates and political systems. These were not tribal chieftaincies. They were structured governments with written laws, diplomatic relations, and trade networks that stretched across Southeast Asia.

The major political entities included:

  • Sultanate of Sulu — established in 1457, the oldest Islamic institution in the Philippines. It maintained trade and diplomatic ties with Borneo, China, and the Malay world.
  • Sultanate of Maguindanao — a powerful sultanate that controlled much of Mindanao and maintained its own legal and administrative systems.
  • Sultanate of Buayan — a sister sultanate of Maguindanao.
  • Sultanate of Kabuntalan — a confederation in the Cotabato region.
  • Royal House of Ranao — the Pat a Pangampong (Four Lake Kingdoms) of Lanao, centered around Lake Lanao.
  • Iranun Confederation — a maritime power known for naval strength.

These sultanates governed through Islamic legal traditions (Shari’ah), maintained independent political systems, conducted trade with Southeast Asian neighbors, and preserved distinct cultural identities. This is the foundation of the Bangsamoro identity: a people who governed themselves, practiced their faith, and defended their homeland for centuries before the Philippine state existed.

Colonial Resistance: 300+ Years

Spain arrived in the Philippines in 1521 and spent over 300 years trying to conquer the Bangsamoro. They called the Muslim inhabitants “Moros” — from their experience fighting the “Moors” in the Iberian Peninsula. The term was meant as a slur. The Bangsamoro reclaimed it as a badge of resistance.

The key fact: The Bangsamoro were never fully conquered by Spain. Despite aggressive military campaigns and anti-Muslim policies, the sultanates maintained effective self-governance throughout the colonial period. This is not a minor footnote. It is the historical basis for the Bangsamoro claim to self-determination — they were independent before colonization and never surrendered that independence by force.

The American Period and Forced Integration

When Spain ceded the Philippines to the United States in 1898 through the Treaty of Paris, Mindanao was administered separately as the “Moro Province.” The Americans recognized that the Bangsamoro were distinct from the rest of the Philippines.

But in 1935, the Bangsamoro homeland was integrated into the Philippine Commonwealth under the new Constitution — over what the Bangsamoro Organic Law itself describes as the “vehement resistance of the Bangsamoro.”1 This forced integration is the root of the modern conflict. The Bangsamoro did not consent to becoming part of the Philippine state on the terms that were imposed.

Post-Independence: The Modern Struggle

After Philippine independence in 1946, the Bangsamoro struggle for self-determination continued through three channels:

  • Political advocacy — legal and democratic efforts to secure autonomy
  • Armed resistance movements — the Moro National Liberation Front (MNLF, founded 1969) and later the Moro Islamic Liberation Front (MILF, founded 1977)
  • Peace negotiations — decades of talks between the Philippine government and Bangsamoro fronts

These three channels — politics, arms, and negotiation — ran in parallel for decades. The government you are joining is the product of the third channel: peace negotiations that succeeded.

What This History Means for You

You may think: “I am applying for a budget analyst position — why do I need to know about sultanates and colonial resistance?” Because the Bangsamoro identity is not academic. Your colleagues carry it. The communities you serve remember it. The government’s foundational documents reference it.

When the BOL’s preamble speaks of the “Bangsamoro people” as a people with a “distinct historical and cultural identity,” it is drawing on these centuries. When the 12 Pillars of Moral Governance include “Sense of Patriotism” and “Cultural Understanding,” they are not borrowing abstract values — they are codifying what the Bangsamoro have practiced for generations.

You do not need to become a scholar of Bangsamoro history. But you need to know enough to respect it, understand references to it, and avoid the common mistake of treating the Bangsamoro struggle as mere insurgency. It was, and remains, a movement for self-determination rooted in a pre-colonial political identity.

2.2 Why ARMM Failed

Before BARMM, there was ARMM — the Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao, created in 1989 through Republic Act 6734 (later amended by RA 9054). ARMM was later expanded through RA 9054 following the 1996 Final Peace Agreement between the Philippine government and the MNLF.

ARMM lasted 30 years (1989-2019). It failed.2 Understanding why it failed helps you understand why BARMM was designed differently.

Limited autonomy. ARMM followed a unitary system — the regional governor and departments were essentially extensions of the national government. The region had minimal control over its own budget, legislation, or institutional design.

Weak fiscal powers. ARMM did not have genuine fiscal autonomy. Its budget depended almost entirely on national government allocations, with limited ability to generate its own revenue.

Governance problems. ARMM’s 25 regional departments were often poorly staffed, poorly managed, and plagued by patronage. Accountability mechanisms were weak.

Institutional design. ARMM replicated the national government’s presidential structure at the regional level. The regional governor held executive power. There was no parliament. Legislative functions were limited.

The Bangsamoro Organic Law was designed to fix every one of these failures. Where ARMM had a governor, BARMM has a Chief Minister accountable to Parliament. Where ARMM had departments, BARMM has 15 line ministries with clear mandates under BAA 13.3 Where ARMM had limited fiscal powers, BARMM has a constitutionally guaranteed block grant equal to 5% of net national internal revenue, plus the power to create its own revenue sources.4

Why This Matters for You

When you join BARMM, you will hear people say “we are not ARMM.” This is not a throwaway line. It is a statement of institutional identity. The entire organizational design of BARMM — the parliamentary system, the civil service code, the ministry structure, the moral governance framework — was built to prevent the failures of ARMM from repeating.

Understanding ARMM’s shortcomings helps you appreciate why certain BARMM systems work the way they do. The merit and fitness principle in BAA 17 exists because ARMM’s hiring was notoriously patronage-driven.5 The SPMS performance management system exists because ARMM had weak accountability mechanisms. The parliamentary oversight function exists because ARMM’s executive branch operated with insufficient checks.

You are joining a government that was designed as a corrective. That design is intentional and well-documented. When you encounter a process that seems cumbersome — a multi-step approval chain, a qualification standard that seems demanding, a reporting requirement that takes time — ask yourself: “What problem was this designed to solve?” The answer is usually in ARMM’s history.

2.3 The Peace Process: From Conflict to Agreement

The Framework Agreement on the Bangsamoro (2012)

In 2012, the Government of the Philippines (GPH) and the Moro Islamic Liberation Front (MILF) signed the Framework Agreement on the Bangsamoro (FAB).6 This agreement established the framework for a new autonomous entity to replace ARMM. It was signed under the Aquino III administration and represented a breakthrough after years of negotiations — and setbacks, including the failed Memorandum of Agreement on Ancestral Domain (MOA-AD) in 2008.

The Comprehensive Agreement on the Bangsamoro (2014)

Two years later, the GPH and MILF signed the Comprehensive Agreement on the Bangsamoro (CAB) — the comprehensive peace agreement that became the blueprint for the BOL.7 The CAB has four annexes, and each one shapes the government you are joining:

Annex on Transitional Arrangements and Modalities — This annex created the transition mechanism from ARMM to BARMM. It established the Bangsamoro Transition Authority (BTA) as the interim government. The BTA you may work under today exists because of this annex.

Annex on Revenue Generation and Wealth-Sharing — This annex established the block grant mechanism, natural resource sharing arrangements, and the Bangsamoro government’s tax powers. When you work with the budget, you are implementing provisions from this annex.

Annex on Power-Sharing — This annex defines which powers belong to the national government (reserved), which belong exclusively to the Bangsamoro government, and which are shared (concurrent). Understanding this annex helps you know what BARMM can and cannot do.

Annex on Normalization — This annex covers the decommissioning of MILF forces, socioeconomic development programs for former combatants, and transitional justice and reconciliation. This is the annex that makes BARMM different from any other government job — you are implementing a peace agreement.

Why This History Matters for Your Work

You may wonder: why does a fresh graduate applying for a budget analyst position need to know about the CAB? Because the CAB is not ancient history. It is the legal and political foundation of the government you are joining.

When your office prepares its annual budget proposal, the block grant formula comes from the CAB’s wealth-sharing annex. When your ministry coordinates with a national government agency, the power-sharing annex determines who has jurisdiction. When your MP files a bill, the bill’s constitutional basis often traces back to the powers enumerated in the CAB and codified in the BOL.

Colleagues, supervisors, and community members will reference the peace process casually — “that’s a CAB commitment,” “this is part of normalization,” “the BDP targets flow from the peace agreement.” If you do not know what they mean, you will be lost. If you do, you will understand not just what you are doing, but why.

2.4 The Bangsamoro Organic Law (Republic Act 11054)

What the BOL Is

The Bangsamoro Organic Law is Republic Act 11054, signed by President Rodrigo Duterte on July 27, 2018, and ratified through a plebiscite in January-February 2019.8 It replaced RA 6734 (as amended by RA 9054), which created the ARMM.9

The BOL is the foundational law of the Bangsamoro government. Everything — every ministry, every commission, every budget allocation, every BAA that the Parliament enacts — derives its authority from the BOL. Think of the BOL as the Bangsamoro’s constitution within the Philippine Constitution.

What the BOL Established

The BOL created an autonomous government with real powers — not a symbolic autonomy, but a government with its own parliament, its own civil service, its own budget, and its own legal system. Here are the key provisions, organized by article:

Article Subject What You Need to Know
I Name and Purpose Establishes the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao
II Bangsamoro Identity Defines who the Bangsamoro are — a people with a distinct identity
III Territory Defines the geographic scope (5 provinces + Cotabato City + SGA)10
IV General Principles Guiding principles of governance
V Powers of Government 55 enumerated areas of competence (Section 2)11
VI Intergovernmental Relations How BARMM coordinates with the national government
VII Bangsamoro Government The Parliament, Chief Minister, and Cabinet
VIII Wali Ceremonial head — moral and cultural authority
IX Basic Rights Rights of all people in BARMM
X Justice System Shari’ah courts and the justice system
XI National Defense and Security Defense, public order and safety, coast guard services
XII Fiscal Autonomy Block grant (Sec. 15-16), revenue powers, 75% tax share (Sec. 10)
XIII Regional Economy and Patrimony Social justice, economic system, natural resources, cooperatives
XIV Rehabilitation and Development Special Development Fund (Sec. 2): PHP 5B/year for 10 years
XV Plebiscite Ratification process for the Organic Law
XVI BTA / Transition The BTA and the transition period
XVII Amendments Process for amending the Organic Law
XVIII Final Provisions Separability, repealing clause, effectivity

The 2019 Plebiscite: The People Chose This

The BOL did not take effect automatically. It was submitted to the people through a plebiscite held in two stages: January 21 and February 6, 2019. The residents of the affected areas voted on whether to be part of the new Bangsamoro region.

The results:

  • The former ARMM provinces voted overwhelmingly in favor
  • Cotabato City voted to join BARMM
  • 63 barangays in North Cotabato (from Carmen, Kabacan, Midsayap, Pigkawayan, and Pikit) voted to join — these became the Special Geographic Area (SGA)12
  • Isabela City (Basilan) voted not to join — it remains outside BARMM
  • Sulu voted against the BOL in the plebiscite — rejecting it by 54.3% — and the Supreme Court later confirmed that Sulu could not be part of BARMM because the province voted against it13

This plebiscite matters because it gives the Bangsamoro government democratic legitimacy. The people chose this government. When you serve in BARMM, you serve a government that the people voted for.

Territory: Five Provinces and More

The Bangsamoro territory today consists of:

  • Lanao del Sur — capital: Marawi City. Home to Lake Lanao, the Royal House of Ranao heritage, and the ongoing Marawi rehabilitation.
  • Maguindanao del Norte — split from the former Maguindanao province via RA 11550, ratified in 2022.14
  • Maguindanao del Sur — the other half of the former Maguindanao. Home to the Bangsamoro Government Center.
  • Basilan — an island province south of Zamboanga. Capital: Lamitan City. Isabela City is not part of BARMM.
  • Tawi-Tawi — the southernmost province of the Philippines. Capital: Bongao. Known as the seaweed capital.
  • Cotabato City — the commercial hub and current administrative center. Joined BARMM by plebiscite. (Note: BAA 37 designates Parang, Maguindanao del Norte as the permanent seat of government.)
  • Special Geographic Area (SGA) — 63 barangays in North Cotabato that voted to join. These have been organized into 8 new municipalities (Pahamuddin, Kadayangan, Nabalawag, Tugunan, Malidegao, Ligawasan, Kapalawan, and Old Kaabakan) through BAAs 41-48.15

Sulu is not part of BARMM. In the case of Province of Sulu v. Medialdea (G.R. Nos. 242255, 243246, 243693), the Supreme Court ruled on September 9, 2024 (finalized November 26, 2024) that Sulu could not be part of BARMM because the province voted against the BOL — rejecting it by 54.3%.16 This violated Article X, Section 18 of the Constitution, which provides that only areas voting favorably in the plebiscite can join an autonomous region.17 Executive Order 91 (July 30, 2025) realigned Sulu under Region IX.18

The SC ruling applied the doctrine of operative fact — meaning that actions taken during the period when Sulu was considered part of BARMM (including BTA appointments of members from Sulu) remain valid. The ruling did not invalidate those prior acts.

2.5 Key BOL Provisions Every Government Worker Should Know

You do not need to memorize the entire BOL. But you need to know these provisions well enough to recognize them when they come up in your work.

Article V, Section 2 — The 55 Enumerated Powers

This is the most operationally significant section of the BOL. Section 2 of Article V lists 55 areas of competence — from (a) administration of justice to (ccc) water supply. These are the subjects over which the Bangsamoro government has authority. They include:

  • Administrative organization
  • Creation of sources of revenue
  • Ancestral domain and natural resources management
  • Social services
  • Education
  • Environmental protection
  • Labor and employment
  • Disaster risk reduction
  • Culture and language preservation
  • Cooperatives
  • Public works
  • Tourism

When you draft a policy, prepare a budget proposal, or write a legislative briefer, your first question should always be: “Does BARMM have competence over this subject?” The answer is in Article V, Section 2.

“The Bangsamoro Government shall exercise its authority over the following matters… (n) Creation of sources of revenues.” — Republic Act 11054, Article V, Section 2(n)

Article XII — Fiscal Autonomy

This article gives BARMM genuine financial independence:

  • Annual Block Grant (Art. XII, Sec. 15-16) — equal to 5% of the net national internal revenue of the BIR and Bureau of Customs19
  • Special Development Fund (Art. XIV, Sec. 2) — PHP 5 billion per year for 10 years (PHP 50 billion total)20
  • Revenue generation — power to create own revenue sources (taxes, fees, charges)
  • 75% tax share (Art. XII, Sec. 10) — of national taxes collected within BARMM21

When you see budget discussions, block grant allocations, or fiscal reports, these provisions are the legal basis.

Article VII — The Bangsamoro Government

This article establishes the parliamentary system:

  • Bangsamoro Parliament — the legislative body, with 80 Members of Parliament under the BOL (Art. VII, Sec. 6)22
  • Chief Minister — head of government, elected by Parliament from among its members
  • Cabinet — ministers appointed by the Chief Minister with Parliament consent
  • Wali — the ceremonial head, representing the moral and cultural authority of the Bangsamoro people

Currently, 77 MPs are sworn in (as of March 2025).23 The Parliament is led by the Speaker, supported by the Secretary General and the Secretariat.

Article X — The Shari’ah Justice System

BARMM has its own justice system for matters governed by Islamic law:

  • Shari’ah courts with jurisdiction over personal law (marriage, divorce, inheritance, custody), property relations among Muslims, and cases involving Muslims within BARMM
  • District and Circuit Shari’ah Courts
  • Shari’ah High Court as the highest Shari’ah court in BARMM

This is unique to BARMM. No other region in the Philippines has its own Shari’ah court system. If your work touches family law, personal status, inheritance, or community disputes among Muslims, you will encounter this system.

Article IX — Basic Rights

This article guarantees rights for all people in BARMM — not just Muslims, not just Bangsamoro, but everyone. Key provisions include:

  • Right to life, liberty, and property
  • Freedom of religion, expression, and assembly
  • Right to education (Section 16) — integrated system of quality education
  • Right to culture and identity (Section 24) — preserve and promote Bangsamoro culture
  • Women’s rights and protection from violence
  • Indigenous peoples’ rights
  • Right to adequate social services

Section 24 — the cultural preservation mandate — is especially significant. It is the legal basis for many programs you may work on, from cultural heritage preservation to mother tongue-based education.

Article VI — Intergovernmental Relations

Because BARMM is autonomous but still part of the Philippines, it must coordinate with the national government. Article VI establishes mechanisms for this coordination — including the Intergovernmental Relations Body (IGRB), which resolves issues between the Bangsamoro and national governments.

Why this matters for you: If you work in any ministry, you will encounter situations where BARMM’s authority overlaps with a national agency. MAFAR coordinates with the Department of Agriculture. MOH coordinates with the Department of Health. MBHTE coordinates with the Department of Education. Article VI defines the rules for these interactions. The power-sharing arrangement specifies which powers are exclusive to the Bangsamoro, which are reserved to the national government, and which are concurrent (shared).

When you are unsure whether your office has jurisdiction over a matter, Article V (enumerated powers) and Article VI (intergovernmental relations) are where you look first.

2.6 The Transition Period — Where You Are Entering

What “Transition” Means

The BTA — Bangsamoro Transition Authority — is the interim government. It holds both legislative and executive powers. Its members are appointed by the President, not elected. The BTA was originally supposed to serve until 2022, but the transition has been extended:

  • RA 11593 (2021) — extended the BTA mandate, resetting elections to 202524
  • RA 12064 (2024) — further extended the transition to 202825

The current BTA (appointed by President Marcos under RA 12064) has been in place since March 2025. Regular elections for the Bangsamoro Parliament are scheduled for 2028.

Three BTA Compositions

The BTA has been reconstituted three times:

  1. First BTA (February 2019 - September 2022) — appointed by President Duterte
  2. Second BTA (September 2022 - February 2025) — reconstituted by President Marcos under RA 11593
  3. Current BTA (March 2025 - present) — appointed by President Marcos under RA 12064

Each reconstitution brought new members but maintained institutional continuity. Bills filed during one BTA composition were not archived — they continued to be deliberated by the succeeding composition without refiling.26

Why Transition Creates Opportunity

The transition period is both urgent and opportunity-rich for new staff:

Urgency. The government must build institutions, enact priority codes, deliver services, and prepare for elections — all within a defined timeline. There is more work than there are people to do it.

Opportunity. Because the government is still forming, entry-level staff can have outsized impact. You are not joining a 100-year-old bureaucracy with rigid hierarchies and filled positions. You are joining a government that is still deciding how to organize itself.

Priority codes. The BTA is tasked with enacting seven priority codes during the transition. As of 2026, five have been enacted:

Code BAA Status
Bangsamoro Administrative Code BAA 13 (October 2020) Enacted
Bangsamoro Civil Service Code BAA 17 (February 2021) Enacted
Bangsamoro Education Code BAA 18 (May 2021)27 Enacted
Bangsamoro Electoral Code BAA 35 (2023)28 Enacted
Bangsamoro Revenue Code — Pending
Bangsamoro Investment Code — Pending
Bangsamoro Local Governance Code BAA 4929 Enacted

Each code creates new institutions, new positions, and new functions — which means new staff are needed to implement them.

The Shift from Appointed to Elected

The most significant event ahead is the transition from an appointed BTA to an elected Bangsamoro Parliament. When elections happen, the government will change from a transitional body to a permanent one. Coterminous staff (those whose appointments are tied to the appointing authority) may be affected. Permanent staff (those with plantilla positions and security of tenure) will continue.

This is why Chapter 4 covers appointment types in detail. Understanding whether your position is permanent, coterminous, or contractual determines how the transition affects you.

2.7 Actionable Tool: BOL Quick Reference Card

Keep this reference card accessible. These are the 10 BOL provisions you will encounter most frequently in your work.

# Provision Article/Section What It Means for Your Work
1 Enumerated Powers Art. V, Sec. 2 Check this list to confirm BARMM has authority over your subject area
2 Annual Block Grant Art. XII, Sec. 15-16 The primary funding source — 5% of net national internal revenue
3 Special Development Fund Art. XIV, Sec. 2 PHP 5B/year for 10 years — funds development projects
4 Parliament composition Art. VII, Sec. 6 80 MPs — the body that enacts BAAs and approves budgets
5 Chief Minister Art. VII, Sec. 7 Head of government — elected by Parliament from among its members
6 Cultural preservation Art. IX, Sec. 24 Legal mandate for all cultural heritage programs
7 Right to education Art. IX, Sec. 16 Integrated system of quality education — basis for MBHTE programs
8 Shari’ah courts Art. X Jurisdiction over personal law among Muslims
9 Territory definition Art. III, Sec. 2 5 provinces + Cotabato City + SGA (Sulu excluded per SC ruling)
10 Transition Art. XVI BTA as interim government, extended to 2028 via RA 12064

How to use this card: When someone references a BOL provision — in a meeting, a memo, or a policy discussion — find it on this card first. If you need the full text, read the relevant article in RA 11054. The full BOL is publicly available through the BARMM Official Gazette and Congress’s official website.

Common BOL Misconceptions

As a newcomer, you may encounter these misunderstandings. Knowing the correct answers helps you avoid repeating them:

“BARMM is a separate country.” No. BARMM is an autonomous region within the Republic of the Philippines. The BOL explicitly states that “The Bangsamoro Autonomous Region is an integral, indivisible, and inseparable part of the territory of the Republic of the Philippines.”30

“BARMM can do anything — it has full autonomy.” Not quite. BARMM has 55 enumerated powers (Art. V, Sec. 2), but powers not granted to BARMM remain with the national government (Art. V, Sec. 1 — the residual clause).31 Defense, foreign affairs, coinage, and customs are examples of powers that remain national.

“Sulu is part of BARMM.” No longer. The Supreme Court ruled in Province of Sulu v. Medialdea (finalized November 2024) that Sulu is excluded because the province voted against the BOL.32 Sulu was realigned under Region IX via EO 91 (July 2025).

“The BTA is the permanent government.” No. The BTA is a transitional body. Its members are appointed by the President. The permanent Bangsamoro government will be established through elections, currently scheduled for 2028.

“Only Muslims can work in BARMM.” Absolutely not. The BOL guarantees rights for all people in BARMM regardless of religion (Art. IX). The government employs people of all faiths. The moral governance framework is inspired by Islamic principles but applies to all staff — and respecting it does not require converting to Islam.

What Comes Next

You now know the story — the struggle, the peace process, the BOL, and the transition. The next chapter shows you how the government is actually organized: who reports to whom, what each ministry does, and how to find your place in the structure. That chapter is built on BAA 13, the Bangsamoro Administrative Code — the law that turns the BOL’s vision into an organizational blueprint.
