03
How the Government Is Organized

How the Government Is Organized

BAA 13 is the blueprint. Learn it the way an architect learns a floor plan — not to memorize every room, but to know where everything is, how the spaces connect, and where you fit.

Bangsamoro Autonomy Act No. 13 — the Bangsamoro Administrative Code — defines the structure, powers, and functions of every ministry, office, and agency in the Bangsamoro government.1 Before you apply for any position, you need to know what the government looks like from the inside. This chapter gives you that map.

3.1 The Three Branches at a Glance

The Bangsamoro government operates under a parliamentary system — the only one in the Philippines.2 Unlike the national government’s separation of executive and legislative branches, the Bangsamoro system integrates them. Here is the high-level structure:

Branch Head Function
Parliament (BTA) Speaker Enacts laws (BAAs), approves budgets, conducts oversight
Executive (Cabinet) Chief Minister Implements laws, manages ministries, delivers services
Wali Wali Ceremonial head, represents moral and cultural authority

The Chief Minister is both head of government and a Member of Parliament. The Cabinet ministers are appointed by the Chief Minister with Parliament’s consent.3 This means the executive is drawn from and accountable to the legislature — a fundamental difference from the presidential system used nationally and in LGUs.

What does this mean for you? If you join the Parliament, your work supports lawmaking and oversight. If you join a ministry, your work implements the laws that Parliament enacts. The two tracks are distinct but tightly connected. A legislative briefer you draft in Parliament may shape a program that a ministry implements. A field report you write in a ministry may prompt a parliamentary inquiry.

3.2 The Parliament

Composition

The BOL (Article VII, Section 6) provides for 80 Members of Parliament.4 During the transition, MPs are appointed by the President.5 Currently, 77 MPs are sworn in (as of March 2025).6

The Parliament is led by: - The Speaker — presiding officer who directs parliamentary proceedings - The Deputy Speaker(s) — assist the Speaker - The Secretary General — the chief administrative officer of the Parliament, who heads the Secretariat7

Committees

Parliament work is organized through committees. Each committee has jurisdiction over specific subject areas — education, health, agriculture, finance, local government, and so on. Committees are where most legislative work happens: hearings, bill deliberations, report preparation.

There are two types: - Statutory (Standing) Committees — permanent committees organized by subject area (e.g., Committee on Agriculture, Committee on Education, Committee on Finance) - Parliamentary (Procedural) Committees — handle internal parliament operations (e.g., Rules, Accounts, Ethics)

The Secretariat

The Parliament Secretariat is the administrative and support structure that enables MPs to do their work. It is headed by the Secretary General and staffed by professional civil servants. The Secretariat includes several key offices:

If you want to work in Parliament, these are the offices you would join:

  • Legislative Services — supports the legislative process, including bill drafting, committee support, and legislative research
  • Parliamentary Operations — manages plenary sessions, committee hearings, scheduling, and records
  • Administrative Services — handles human resources, finance, procurement, and general administration for the Parliament

Parliamentary Staff Roles

Three staff roles appear throughout Parliament work. Understanding them helps you decide which one matches your skills:

Legislative Staff Officer (LSO) — The analytical backbone. LSOs research issues, draft bills, prepare legislative briefers, write committee reports, and provide policy analysis. If you enjoy reading, writing, and research, this is your role. Entry level: SG 11.

Political Affairs Officer (PAO) — The constituency link. PAOs handle constituent concerns, coordinate with LGUs and communities, and manage the MP’s field activities. If you enjoy community engagement and stakeholder relations, this is your role.

Public Information and Media Affairs Officer (PIMAO) — The communications specialist. PIMAOs manage the MP’s public communications, social media, press releases, and media relations. If you have skills in communications, journalism, or digital media, this is your role.

Each MP typically has a small team that includes one or more of each role. The committee itself may also have dedicated LSOs assigned by the Secretariat.

3.3 The Chief Minister and Cabinet

The Chief Minister

The Chief Minister is the head of government. Under the BOL (Article VII, Section 31), the Chief Minister is elected by the Parliament from among its members by a majority vote.8 The Chief Minister exercises executive power, appoints Cabinet ministers, and directs the overall operations of the Bangsamoro government.9

The Office of the Chief Minister (OCM)

The OCM is the executive staff and administrative office of the Chief Minister. It is separate from the line ministries. The OCM houses:

Executive Offices: - Office of the Senior Minister - Office of the Chief of Staff - Cabinet Secretariat - Bangsamoro Attorney General’s Office (BAGO)

Bangsamoro Management Support System (BMSS): - Technical Management Service (TMS) - Administrative Management Service (AMS) - Finance Management Service (FMS) - Intelligence and Security Service (ISS) - Procurement Service (PS) - Clinic - Bangsamoro Satellite Coordinating Offices - Bangsamoro Liaison Office in Metro Manila - Bangsamoro Library and Archives

Three Councils under the OCM: 1. Bangsamoro Disaster Risk Reduction and Management Council (DRRMC) 2. Bangsamoro Economic and Development Council (BEDC) 3. Bangsamoro Regional Peace and Order Council (BRPOC)

If you want to work in strategic coordination, policy advice, or executive operations, the OCM offices are where that work happens. Positions here tend to be closer to decision-making but also more politically sensitive.

3.4 The 15 Line Ministries

Line ministries are where most government employees work. Each ministry has a specific mandate defined in BAA 13,10 and many have enabling BAAs that flesh out their powers and functions. Here is the complete list:

1. Ministry of Agriculture, Fisheries, and Agrarian Reform (MAFAR)

Mandate: Agricultural development, fisheries management, and agrarian reform across BARMM.

Why it matters: BARMM has the largest agricultural land area in the Philippines. It is the top producer of seaweed (48.42% national share) and cassava (41.70%). There are 110,513 hectares of undistributed agricultural land under CARP (per MAFAR/DAR data as cited in BDP 2023-2028).11 MAFAR needs agricultural technicians, agrarian reform officers, fisheries specialists, and program managers.

2. Ministry of Basic, Higher, and Technical Education (MBHTE)

Mandate: Comprehensive education system — from basic education through higher education and technical-vocational training.

Enabling BAA: BAA 18 (Bangsamoro Education Code)12

Why it matters: BARMM has the lowest literacy rates, highest dropout rates, and lowest completion rates in the Philippines.13 MBHTE needs teachers, curriculum developers, education planners, and administrative staff at every level.

3. Ministry of Environment, Natural Resources, and Energy (MENRE)

Mandate: Environmental protection, natural resource management, and energy development.

Why it matters: BARMM’s territory includes Lake Lanao (the second largest lake in the Philippines), the Mindanao River Basin, the Sulu-Celebes Sea marine ecosystem, and multiple watersheds. Environmental management directly affects communities dependent on fishing, farming, and water resources.

4. Ministry of Finance, and Budget and Management (MFBM)

Mandate: Fiscal management — revenue collection, budget preparation, expenditure management, and financial accountability.

Enabling BAA: BAA 84 (Bangsamoro Budget System Act)14

Why it matters: MFBM manages the annual block grant (5% of net national internal revenue — exceeding PHP 80 billion).15 It needs budget analysts, accountants, procurement officers, and financial management specialists. Every peso the government spends passes through MFBM’s systems.

5. Ministry of Health (MOH)

Mandate: Health service delivery across the region.

Why it matters: Maternal mortality above the national average. Highest stunting, wasting, and underweight rates in the Philippines.16 Insufficient health professionals at all levels — from provincial hospitals to barangay health stations. MOH needs nurses, midwives, doctors, nutritionists, and health program officers.

6. Ministry of Human Settlements and Development (MHSD)

Mandate: Housing, shelter programs, and human settlements planning.

Why it matters: Displacement from conflict (especially the 2017 Marawi siege), natural disasters, and the ongoing need for social housing in urban and rural areas. MHSD works on resettlement, housing programs, and community development.

7. Ministry of Indigenous Peoples’ Affairs (MIPA)

Mandate: Protection and promotion of the rights, culture, and welfare of indigenous peoples within BARMM.

Enabling BAA: BAA 6417

Why it matters: BARMM is home to indigenous peoples including the Teduray, Lambangian, and Dulangan Manobo, among others.18 MIPA ensures that development programs respect indigenous rights, ancestral domains, and cultural traditions.

8. Ministry of the Interior and Local Government (MILG)

Mandate: Coordination with and supervision of local government units (LGUs) — provinces, cities, municipalities, and barangays.

Enabling BAA: BAA 49 (Bangsamoro Local Governance Code)19

Why it matters: MILG oversees 116 municipalities and 2,590 barangays (BDP 2023-2028 figures; exclude adjustments from Sulu’s exclusion from BARMM per SC ruling).20 It assesses LGU functionality, coordinates disaster response through BRDRRMO (an attached agency), and supports local governance capacity building. If you want to work at the intersection of regional and local government, MILG is where that happens.

9. Ministry of Labor and Employment (MOLE)

Mandate: Employment promotion, labor standards, and workers’ welfare.

Enabling BAA: BAA 82 (Bangsamoro Labor Code)21

Why it matters: With underemployment at 9% and a growing working-age population, MOLE manages employment programs, labor dispute resolution, and overseas worker protection (coordinated with BAA 19 on overseas Bangsamoro workers).22

10. Ministry of Public Order and Safety (MPOS)

Mandate: Public order, law enforcement coordination, and community safety.

Why it matters: BARMM’s peace and security situation directly affects development. MPOS coordinates with national law enforcement agencies (PNP, NAPOLCOM) and supports the normalization process under the CAB.

11. Ministry of Public Works (MPW)

Mandate: Infrastructure development — roads, bridges, buildings, water systems, and public facilities.

Why it matters: Infrastructure backlog across all five provinces and Cotabato City. Island provinces (Basilan, Tawi-Tawi) face connectivity challenges. MPW needs engineers, project managers, construction inspectors, and infrastructure planners.

12. Ministry of Science and Technology (MOST)

Mandate: Science, technology, and innovation for regional development.

Why it matters: Technology adoption, research and development, and digital infrastructure are growing priorities. MOST supports innovation in agriculture, health, education, and governance.

13. Ministry of Social Services and Development (MSSD)

Mandate: Social welfare programs, social protection, and community development.

Why it matters: MSSD runs livelihood programs, humanitarian assistance, and social protection services for the most vulnerable communities. It coordinates with DSWD on national social protection programs being devolved to BARMM.

14. Ministry of Trade, Investments, and Tourism (MTIT)

Mandate: Trade promotion, investment facilitation, and tourism development.

Attached agencies: Bangsamoro Economic Zone Authority (BEZA), Bangsamoro Halal Board

Why it matters: BARMM’s economic potential — in agriculture, halal industry, marine resources, and cultural tourism — remains largely untapped. MTIT and its attached agencies drive economic development strategy.

15. Ministry of Transportation and Communications (MOTC)

Mandate: Transportation networks and communications infrastructure.

Why it matters: Connectivity between mainland and island provinces is a persistent challenge. Flight routes, seaport development, road networks, and digital connectivity all fall under MOTC.

3.5 Attached Agencies and Offices Under the OCM

Beyond the 15 line ministries, 10 agencies are attached to the Office of the Chief Minister.23 These agencies have specialized mandates:

Agency Acronym Focus
Bangsamoro Planning and Development Authority BPDA Development planning, M&E, BDP coordination
Bangsamoro Board of Investments BBOI Investment promotion and facilitation
Bangsamoro Information Office BIO Government communications and public information
Bangsamoro ICT Office BICTO Information and communications technology
Office for Settler Communities OSC Welfare of settler communities in BARMM
Office for Other Bangsamoro Communities OOBC Bangsamoro communities outside BARMM territory
Bangsamoro Pilgrimage Authority BPA Hajj and Umrah coordination
Development Academy of the Bangsamoro DAB Capacity building and training
Bangsamoro Darul-Ifta’ BDI Islamic jurisprudence and religious guidance
Cooperatives and Social Enterprise Authority CSEA Cooperative development and social enterprises

BPDA deserves special mention. It coordinates the entire Bangsamoro Development Plan — the government’s roadmap. If you are interested in strategic planning, data analysis, monitoring and evaluation, or development research, BPDA is where that work concentrates.

3.6 Commissions and Independent Bodies

Bangsamoro Commissions (Attached to OCM)

Four commissions address specific sectors. Each was created by a specific BAA:24

Commission Acronym Enabling BAA Focus
Bangsamoro Women Commission BWC BAA 825 Gender equality, women’s empowerment
Bangsamoro Youth Commission BYC BAA 1026 Youth development, participation
Bangsamoro Sports Commission BSC BAA 1227 Sports development
Bangsamoro Commission for the Preservation of Cultural Heritage BCPCH BAA 13, Title XVI28 Cultural heritage preservation

Independent Bodies

Two bodies operate independently from the executive:

Body Acronym Enabling BAA Function
Bangsamoro Human Rights Commission BHRC BAA 429 Human rights monitoring and protection
Bangsamoro Internal Auditing Body BIAB BAA 13, Title XVII30 Internal audit and accountability

If you are passionate about human rights, gender, youth, sports, or cultural heritage, these commissions and bodies offer focused career paths. They are smaller than ministries, which means fewer positions but also more direct involvement in your area of interest.

3.7 Local Government Units

BARMM’s territory is organized into local government units at four levels:

Level Count Key Officials
Provinces 5 (Lanao del Sur, Maguindanao del Norte, Maguindanao del Sur, Basilan, Tawi-Tawi) Governor, Vice-Governor, Provincial Board Members
Cities Cotabato City + Lamitan City + Marawi City Mayor, Vice-Mayor, City Council Members
Municipalities 116 (including 8 SGA municipalities)31 Mayor, Vice-Mayor, Municipal Council Members
Barangays 2,59031 Barangay Captain, Barangay Council

The 8 SGA municipalities (Pahamuddin, Kadayangan, Nabalawag, Tugunan, Malidegao, Ligawasan, Kapalawan, and Old Kaabakan) were created by BAAs 41-48.32 These are the newest municipalities in BARMM — the 63 barangays in North Cotabato that voted to join during the 2019 plebiscite.

LGU governance in BARMM operates under BAA 49 (Bangsamoro Local Governance Code),33 which defines the structure, powers, and responsibilities of local governments within the autonomous region. BAA 49 differs from the national Local Government Code (RA 7160) in ways specific to the Bangsamoro context — including recognition of indigenous governance structures and the integration of moral governance principles.34

LGU Functionality Assessment

MILG assesses all LGUs based on seven elements of functionality:

  1. Presence of government center with facilities
  2. Working elected and appointed mandatory officials
  3. Functional local special bodies
  4. Functional local legislative body
  5. Approved mandated plans and budget
  6. Availability of basic services
  7. Sound fiscal management

As of 2021, 59% of 70 assessed LGUs were compliant — up from 50% in 2020.35 Island provinces register lower compliance due to geographic challenges. If you join an LGU, you will likely work on improving one or more of these elements.

3.8 How to Read BAA 13

BAA 13 is a long law. You do not need to read it cover to cover. But you do need to know how to navigate it. Here is the structure:

Book I — General provisions, principles, and the Bangsamoro government structure Book II — The Parliament (composition, powers, procedures) Book III — The Chief Minister and the Executive (OCM, Cabinet, executive functions) Book IV — Line Ministries (mandate, powers, organizational structure for each of the 15 ministries) Book V — Other offices, agencies, and commissions

For your purposes, the most useful sections are in Book IV — where you will find the mandate section, the powers section, and the organizational structure section for the specific ministry or office you want to join. Each ministry’s entry in BAA 13 tells you:

  • Mandate — what the ministry exists to do
  • Powers and functions — what it is authorized to do
  • Organizational structure — how it is internally organized (bureaus, services, divisions)

When you research a target office before applying, BAA 13 is your primary source. When an interviewer asks, “What do you know about this ministry?” your answer should draw from what BAA 13 says about its mandate and powers.

3.9 The Full Organizational Map

Here is the complete organizational structure at a glance:

Category Count Examples
Parliament (BTA) 1 body, 77 MPs Speaker, Deputy Speaker, Secretary General, Secretariat
Office of the Chief Minister 1 executive office Senior Minister, Chief of Staff, Cabinet Secretariat, BAGO
BMSS (Management Support) 9 support units TMS, AMS, FMS, ISS, Procurement, Clinic, Liaison Office
OCM Councils 3 DRRMC, BEDC, BRPOC
OCM Attached Agencies 10 BPDA, BBOI, BIO, BICTO, OSC, OOBC, BPA, DAB, BDI, CSEA
Line Ministries 15 MAFAR, MBHTE, MENRE, MFBM, MOH, MHSD, MIPA, MILG, MOLE, MPOS, MPW, MOST, MSSD, MTIT, MOTC
Ministry-Attached Agencies 3 BEZA, Bangsamoro Halal Board (under MTIT), BRDRRMO (under MILG)
Commissions 4 BWC, BYC, BSC, BCPCH
Independent Bodies 2 BHRC, BIAB
Wali 1 office Ceremonial head
LGUs 5 provinces, 3 cities, 116 municipalities, 2,590 barangays31 Elected local officials

Total executive units (OCM + 15 ministries): 16.39

3.10 Intergovernmental Relations: BARMM and the National Government

Because BARMM is autonomous but still part of the Philippines, coordination with the national government is constant. The BOL (Article VI) establishes several mechanisms:36

Mechanism Purpose Status
IGRB (Intergovernmental Relations Body) Resolve intergovernmental issues Operational
PCBPF (Philippine Congress-Bangsamoro Parliament Forum) Legislative coordination Convened November 2022
BSDB (Bangsamoro Sustainable Development Board) Sustainable development coordination Established via BAA 3137
Joint Normalization Committee Peace agreement implementation Operational

If you work in any ministry that coordinates with a national counterpart (MAFAR with DA, MOH with DOH, MBHTE with DepEd, MFBM with DBM), you will encounter these intergovernmental mechanisms. Understanding that BARMM has exclusive powers, shared powers, and residual powers (those not granted to BARMM remain with the national government) helps you navigate jurisdictional questions.38

3.11 Actionable Tool: “Know Your Target Office” Worksheet

Before you apply to any office, fill out this worksheet. It takes 30 minutes of research and demonstrates preparation in interviews. Use BAA 13 and the ministry’s website or Facebook page as your sources.

Question Your Research
Office name and acronym: ___
Created/established by: BAA 13 and/or specific BAA (e.g., BAA 18 for MBHTE)
Mandate (from BAA 13): One sentence — what does this office exist to do?
Key powers (from BAA 13): List 3-5 specific powers or functions
Current leadership: Minister/Director/Head name and title
Number of staff (if available): From the office’s website or annual report
Key programs or outputs: What does this office produce or deliver? List 3-5
BDP alignment: Which of the 6 BDP goals does this office serve?
12-Point Priority Agenda alignment: Which priority does this office support?
Position I am targeting: Specific position title and salary grade
Why I want to join this office: 2-3 sentences connecting your skills/motivation to the office’s mandate

How to use this worksheet: Bring it to your interview preparation (Chapter 9). When an interviewer asks, “Why this office?” your answer comes from the last row. When they ask, “What do you know about our mandate?” your answer comes from the third row. This worksheet is your cheat sheet — except it is not cheating, because the information is public and the effort to find it proves your seriousness.

What Comes Next

You now know the structure — Parliament, ministries, agencies, commissions, LGUs. The next chapter covers the rules that govern your employment: BAA 17, the Bangsamoro Civil Service Code.40 That law determines how you get hired, how you get evaluated, how you get promoted, and how you can be disciplined. It is the employee handbook for the entire Bangsamoro government.

Back to Table of Contents
04
Your Employment Framework

Your Employment Framework

BAA 17 governs your hiring, your evaluation, your promotion, and your dismissal. It is the Bangsamoro Civil Service Code — the employment framework for every government worker in BARMM. If the BOL is the constitution and BAA 13 is the organizational chart, BAA 17 is the employee handbook.

This chapter explains what BAA 17 covers, how it affects you from day one, and what systems you will encounter throughout your career. Read it before you apply. Read it again after you are hired.

4.1 What BAA 17 Covers

The Bangsamoro Civil Service Code (BAA 17), enacted in February 2021, establishes the complete employment framework for BARMM government workers.1 It covers:

  • Recruitment and appointment — how positions are filled
  • Qualification standards — what you need to qualify
  • Types of appointments — permanent, temporary, coterminous, contractual, casual
  • Performance management — how your work is measured
  • Rewards and recognition — how good performance is acknowledged
  • Discipline — what happens when rules are broken
  • Leave benefits — your time-off entitlements
  • Code of conduct — the ethical standards you must follow
  • Separation from service — how employment ends

BAA 17 does not exist in isolation. It works alongside national Civil Service Commission (CSC) rules, which still apply to BARMM in areas not specifically covered by BAA 17. The relationship is straightforward: where BAA 17 provides its own rules, those rules apply. Where BAA 17 is silent, national CSC rules fill the gap.

4.2 The Merit and Fitness Principle

The foundation of BAA 17 — and of the Philippine civil service generally — is the merit and fitness principle. This means:

  • Positions are filled based on qualifications, competence, and competitive selection — not on political connections, family ties, or personal loyalty
  • All applicants who meet the qualification standards have an equal opportunity to be considered
  • Selection is conducted by a Human Resource Merit Promotion and Selection Board (HRMPSB) — a multi-member panel that evaluates candidates

“Appointments in the civil service shall be made only according to merit and fitness, to be determined as far as practicable by competitive examination.” — Philippine Constitution, Article IX-B, Section 2(2)2

What this means in practice: When a position opens, it is posted publicly. Applicants submit credentials. The HRMPSB evaluates all qualified applicants against the same criteria. The most qualified applicant is recommended for appointment. The appointing authority (Minister, Secretary General, or other authorized official) makes the final appointment based on the HRMPSB recommendation.

Does this system work perfectly? No. Patronage pressures exist in any government, and BARMM is no exception. But the legal framework is designed to resist patronage. Understanding the merit-based process protects you — both as an applicant (you know what you are entitled to) and as a future government worker (you know what standards to uphold).

4.3 Types of Appointments

Not all government jobs are the same. The type of appointment determines your job security, benefits, and career trajectory. Understanding these distinctions is critical before you accept any position.

Permanent (Plantilla)

What it is: A permanent appointment to a position that is included in the approved staffing pattern (the “plantilla”) of the office. This is the gold standard of government employment.

What you get: - Security of tenure — you cannot be removed except for cause and with due process - Full benefits — GSIS, PhilHealth, Pag-IBIG, leave credits, retirement benefits - Promotion eligibility — you can be promoted to higher positions through the merit-based process - Step increments — automatic salary increases based on length of service and performance

What you need: - Meet all qualification standards for the position (education, experience, training, eligibility) - Civil Service eligibility — either Professional or Sub-Professional, depending on the position level - Pass the HRMPSB evaluation process

How it works: Permanent positions are the ones listed on the government’s plantilla — the authorized staffing pattern approved by the Department of Budget and Management (DBM) or MFBM. Each position has a salary grade (SG), a position title, and a set of qualification standards (QS).

Temporary

What it is: An appointment to a position for a specified period, usually when the appointing authority cannot find a qualified applicant who meets all requirements — particularly CSC eligibility.

What you get: - Benefits similar to permanent — but no security of tenure - The appointment expires after the specified period (typically 12 months) and may or may not be renewed - You can be replaced at any time if a fully qualified applicant becomes available

When it happens: A ministry has a vacant SG 15 position. No eligible applicant applies. An applicant with the right degree and experience but no CSC eligibility is available. The ministry can issue a temporary appointment. If that person later passes the CSC exam, the appointment can be converted to permanent.

Key warning: Temporary appointments do not carry security of tenure. If you are offered a temporary appointment, prioritize passing the Civil Service exam so your appointment can be converted.

Coterminous

What it is: An appointment that is tied to the appointing authority’s term. When the appointing authority leaves office, your appointment ends.

What you get: - Benefits during the term of appointment - No security of tenure — your appointment automatically expires when the appointing authority’s term ends

Who gets it: Staff directly serving officials with fixed terms — confidential staff, executive assistants, and special project staff. In BARMM, this is especially relevant during the BTA transition. If your appointment is coterminous with a BTA member’s term, your position ends when the BTA’s mandate expires or is reconstituted.

Key warning: If you are offered a coterminous position, understand that your employment is tied to someone else’s tenure. When the BTA transitions to an elected government, coterminous positions may not survive. Ask explicitly: “Is this position coterminous or plantilla?”

Contractual (Contract of Service / Job Order)

What it is: Employment under a Contract of Service (COS) or Job Order (JO). These are not regular government appointments — they are service contracts.

Contract of Service (COS): - A contract for a specific deliverable or project - Duration: usually 6 months to 1 year, renewable - You receive compensation but are not considered a government employee in the traditional sense - Limited benefits — no GSIS, no security of tenure, no leave credits (though some offices provide these voluntarily)

Job Order (JO): - The most basic form of government engagement - Task-based, short-term (usually less than 6 months) - Minimal benefits - No employer-employee relationship in the legal sense

The reality: Many fresh graduates enter government through COS or JO engagements. These are not ideal — the benefits are limited and the security is low. But they offer a way to gain government experience, build your network, and position yourself for a permanent appointment when one opens. Think of COS/JO as the entry point, not the destination.

Casual

What it is: Employment for seasonal or intermittent work. Less common in BARMM’s central offices, more common in LGUs and project-based programs.

Summary Table: Appointment Types

Type Security of Tenure Full Benefits Promotion Eligible Duration
Permanent Yes Yes Yes Until retirement or separation
Temporary No Partial Limited Up to 12 months, renewable
Coterminous No Partial Limited Tied to appointing authority’s term
COS No Minimal No Contract period (6-12 months)
Job Order No Minimal No Task-based (< 6 months)
Casual No Partial No Seasonal/intermittent

The bottom line: Aim for a permanent appointment. If you start as COS or temporary, make it your goal to convert. The two things you need: meet the qualification standards and obtain CSC eligibility.

4.4 Qualification Standards

Every position in the government has a set of qualification standards (QS) — the minimum requirements you must meet to be considered. QS have four components:

Education

The degree or level of education required. Examples: - SG 6-9 (clerical/sub-professional): High school diploma or vocational course - SG 11-15 (professional/entry): Bachelor’s degree (sometimes in a specific field — e.g., BS Agriculture for MAFAR technical positions) - SG 18-22 (supervisory/specialist): Bachelor’s degree + graduate study, or a master’s degree - SG 24+ (managerial): Master’s degree or higher

Experience

The number of years of relevant work experience required. Entry-level professional positions (SG 11) typically require none. Mid-level positions (SG 18) may require 2-4 years of relevant experience. Senior positions (SG 24+) may require 4-8 years.

What counts as “relevant”: Experience in the function area of the position. If you are applying for a Planning Officer III position, relevant experience means work in planning, research, data analysis, or M&E — whether in government, NGOs, or the private sector.

Training

Hours of relevant training completed. Expressed in hours: - Entry-level (SG 11-15): Often none required or 4 hours - Mid-level (SG 18-22): 16-40 hours of relevant training - Senior (SG 24+): 40-120 hours of relevant training

Training includes seminars, workshops, short courses, and certificate programs. Keep records of all training you attend — you will need them for every promotion throughout your career.

Eligibility

Civil Service eligibility — the gateway requirement. The CSC administers exams that grant eligibility:

  • Career Service Professional (CSP) — required for SG 11+ professional positions. Obtained by passing the Career Service Professional exam administered by the CSC, or through equivalent eligibility (RA 1080 for licensed professionals, PD 907 for college honor graduates, etc.)3
  • Career Service Sub-Professional (CSSP) — for SG 1-10 sub-professional positions

Key point: If you are a licensed professional (CPA, nurse, engineer, teacher, lawyer), your professional license may serve as your CS eligibility. This is called RA 1080 eligibility — bar and board exam passers are automatically granted civil service eligibility.4 College honor graduates (cum laude and above) receive eligibility through PD 907.5 Check with the CSC which licenses and honors qualify.

If you are not yet eligible: You can still apply for temporary appointments (the office can hire you temporarily while you prepare for the CS exam). But your career advancement will be blocked until you obtain eligibility. Take the exam seriously.

How BARMM QS Relate to National CSC Standards

BAA 17 allows BARMM to create its own qualification standards within the CSC framework. This means: - CSC eligibility remains the national standard — you still need to pass the CSC exam - BARMM can add requirements relevant to the autonomous region (e.g., knowledge of Bangsamoro governance, cultural competency, language skills for specific positions) - BARMM cannot lower standards below CSC minimums — the floor is national, but the ceiling can be higher

4.5 Performance Management

Once you are hired, your work is measured through the Strategic Performance Management System (SPMS).6 This system connects individual performance to organizational goals, all the way up to the BDP.

The SPMS Cascade

The system works top-down:

  1. BDP goals and targets — the government’s overall objectives
  2. Office Performance Commitment and Review (OPCR) — each office sets annual targets aligned with BDP goals
  3. Individual Performance Commitment and Review (IPCR) — each employee sets personal targets aligned with the OPCR

Your IPCR is your performance contract. At the beginning of each rating period, you and your supervisor agree on: - Performance targets — specific, measurable outputs you will deliver - Success indicators — how achievement is measured - Timeline — when deliverables are due

The Rating Cycle

Phase When What Happens
Target-setting January (or start of rating period) You and your supervisor set IPCR targets
Mid-year review June-July Progress check — are you on track? Adjust if needed
Year-end evaluation December-January Final assessment of performance against targets
Rating After year-end evaluation You receive a numerical rating (1-5 scale)

Performance Ratings

Rating Description Consequence
5 — Outstanding Exceeded all targets with exceptional quality Eligible for Performance-Based Bonus (PBB) and awards
4 — Very Satisfactory Met all targets, some exceeded Eligible for PBB
3 — Satisfactory Met most targets at acceptable quality Standard — meets expectations
2 — Unsatisfactory Failed to meet several targets Performance improvement plan required
1 — Poor Failed to meet most or all targets Subject to administrative action

What you need to know as a new employee: Your first rating period matters. It establishes your reputation and sets the baseline for your career. Take target-setting seriously — make sure your IPCR targets are specific, measurable, and achievable. Do not agree to vague targets (“improve office operations”) that cannot be measured. Push for clear deliverables (“process 50 applications per quarter with 95% accuracy”).

BDP Alignment in Your IPCR

Every IPCR should reference how your work contributes to BDP goals. This is not bureaucratic box-checking — it is how the government connects individual effort to regional development. When you set your targets, ask: “Which BDP goal does this support?” Document the link.

For example: - A budget analyst at MFBM → BDP Goal 1 (Stable, Just, and Accountable Government) - A teacher at MBHTE → BDP Goal 4 (Inclusive, Responsive, and Quality Social Services) - A project officer at MPW → BDP Goal 6 (Strategic, Adequate, and Climate-Resilient Infrastructure)

4.6 Rewards and Recognition

Good performance is recognized through several mechanisms:

Performance-Based Bonus (PBB)

The PBB is an annual bonus given to government employees and offices that meet their performance targets. Eligibility depends on both your individual IPCR rating (at least Satisfactory) and your office’s OPCR rating. If your office performs poorly as a whole, even individual high performers may not receive the PBB.

Step Increment

A step increment is a salary increase within your salary grade. Each SG has 8 steps. You advance one step every 3 years of satisfactory service (length of service increment) or for outstanding performance (merit increment).7 Step increments are automatic — but you must have at least a Satisfactory rating.

Loyalty Award

After 10 years of continuous government service, you are eligible for a Loyalty Award. Subsequent awards come at 5-year intervals (15, 20, 25 years, etc.). The award includes a cash bonus.8

PRAISE

The Program on Awards and Incentives for Service Excellence (PRAISE) is the government’s formal recognition program. Each office nominates outstanding employees for awards — Employee of the Month, Employee of the Quarter, Employee of the Year, and special citations.

4.7 Discipline

BAA 17 and the national 2017 Rules on Administrative Cases in the Civil Service (RACCS) govern disciplinary matters.9 As a government employee, you are subject to administrative discipline for violations of the Code of Conduct and other rules.

Grounds for Disciplinary Action

Grounds for administrative cases include, among others: - Dishonesty — lying, falsifying documents, misrepresenting facts - Neglect of duty — failing to perform assigned tasks - Misconduct — improper behavior in office - Habitual absenteeism or tardiness — chronic attendance problems - Inefficiency and incompetence — persistent inability to perform duties - Conduct prejudicial to the best interest of the service — behavior that damages the government’s reputation

Due Process

No government employee can be disciplined without due process. This means: - Written notice — you receive a formal complaint specifying the charges - Opportunity to respond — you have the right to answer the charges in writing - Investigation — a fact-finding process determines what happened - Decision — a written decision based on evidence, with explanation - Appeal — you can appeal the decision to a higher authority or the CSC

Penalties

Penalties are graduated based on the offense and whether it is a first, second, or third occurrence:

Severity Examples Penalty Range
Light offenses Discourtesy, improper attire, minor delay in reports Reprimand → 1-30 days suspension
Less grave offenses Simple neglect, simple misconduct 1-6 months suspension → dismissal (on 2nd offense)
Grave offenses Dishonesty, grave misconduct, falsification 6 months suspension → dismissal (even on 1st offense)

Key advice: The most common disciplinary issues for new employees are attendance-related — tardiness, unauthorized absences, failure to follow timekeeping rules. These seem minor but accumulate quickly. Follow the attendance rules from day one.

4.8 Leave Benefits

Government employment comes with leave benefits that are generally better than private sector standards:

Leave Type Annual Entitlement Key Rules
Vacation Leave (VL) 15 days per year Earned monthly (1.25 days/month). Can be accumulated. Requires advance filing.
Sick Leave (SL) 15 days per year Earned monthly (1.25 days/month). Can be accumulated. Requires medical certificate for 5+ consecutive days.
Maternity Leave 105 days (RA 11210)10 For female employees who give birth. Paid.
Paternity Leave 7 days (RA 8187)11 For married male employees whose legitimate spouse gives birth.
Solo Parent Leave 7 days (RA 8972)12 For qualified solo parents.
Study Leave Up to 6 months For employees pursuing studies relevant to their work. With pay, if approved.
Special Leave for Women 2 months (RA 9710)13 For gynecological surgery. Paid.
Forced/Mandatory Leave 5 days per year You must take at least 5 days of VL annually. Non-negotiable.

What you need to know: Leave credits accumulate. Unused VL and SL carry over to the next year. When you retire or separate from service, your accumulated leave credits are monetized — paid out as terminal leave pay. This can amount to a significant sum after decades of service. Start accumulating from your first month.

4.9 Government Systems and Compliance

From your first week, you will encounter several systems and requirements. None of these are optional.

SPMS — Strategic Performance Management System

Already covered in Section 4.5. Your IPCR is the central document — it defines your targets, measures your output, and determines your rating.

SALN — Statement of Assets, Liabilities, and Net Worth

Every government employee must file a SALN annually. This is a constitutional requirement (Article XI, Section 17 of the Philippine Constitution).14 You declare all your assets (property, bank accounts, investments), liabilities (loans, debts), and net worth.

The SALN is not a tax return — it is a transparency tool. It allows the government and the public to monitor whether your wealth is proportional to your legitimate income. Failure to file or filing a false SALN is a ground for administrative and criminal liability.

First-time tip: File your SALN honestly and on time. The biggest mistake new employees make is forgetting to file or treating it as unimportant. It is one of the most legally consequential documents you will sign.

Attendance and Timekeeping

Government offices follow prescribed working hours. Timekeeping is typically done through biometric systems or daily time records (DTRs). The standard work schedule is 8:00 AM to 5:00 PM, Monday to Friday, though some offices have flexible work arrangements.

Tardiness and absences are tracked and can result in disciplinary action. The rules are strict: habitual tardiness (reporting late 10 or more times in a month) and habitual absenteeism (unauthorized absences for 3 or more days in a month or at least 5 consecutive days) are grounds for administrative cases.

ARTA Compliance — Citizen’s Charter

Under the Anti-Red Tape Act (ARTA), originally enacted as RA 9485 (2007) and strengthened by RA 11032 (2018),15 every government office must maintain a Citizen’s Charter — a document listing all services the office provides, the steps to access each service, the processing time, and the responsible person. If your office delivers services to the public, you may be involved in maintaining and implementing this charter.

Government Benefits Enrollment

Upon appointment, you will be enrolled in three mandatory benefit systems:

  • GSIS (Government Service Insurance System) — your retirement, insurance, and loan provider
  • PhilHealth — health insurance
  • Pag-IBIG (HDMF) — housing and savings fund

Monthly contributions are deducted from your salary. These are not optional — they are mandatory for all government employees. In exchange, you get retirement benefits, loan access, health coverage, and housing fund accumulation.

4.10 The Code of Conduct

Two layers of ethical standards govern your behavior as a BARMM government worker:

RA 6713 — Code of Conduct and Ethical Standards for Public Officials and Employees16

This is the national standard. It requires all government employees to: - Act with professionalism, justness, and sincerity - Be accountable to the people - Respond to public needs promptly and adequately - Disclose conflicts of interest - Avoid using office resources for personal gain - Maintain a simple and modest lifestyle

Moral Governance Overlay

On top of RA 6713, BARMM applies the 12 Pillars of Moral Governance (covered in Chapter 1). This adds a faith-informed, culturally grounded ethical dimension that is unique to the Bangsamoro government. In practice, this means:

  • Integrity and amanah (trust) — you are a trustee of public resources, not an owner
  • Accountability and shura (consultation) — decisions are made through consultation, not unilateral authority
  • Justice and adl — fairness in all dealings, regardless of personal relationships

You do not need to be Muslim to serve in BARMM. But you do need to understand and respect these governance principles. They will come up in orientation, in performance discussions, and in the day-to-day culture of the workplace.

4.11 The CSC and BARMM

The relationship between the Civil Service Commission (CSC) and the Bangsamoro government can be confusing. Here is the short version:

The CSC is a national constitutional body. It has jurisdiction over all civil service matters in the Philippines, including BARMM.21

BAA 17 creates a Bangsamoro civil service system within the CSC framework. This means: - CSC eligibility exams are still the standard — you take the same exam as any other government applicant in the Philippines - CSC rules on appointments, promotions, and discipline apply unless BAA 17 provides otherwise - BARMM has its own HRMPSB — the merit selection board that evaluates applicants within BARMM - BARMM can set additional qualification standards beyond the CSC minimums, tailored to the needs of the autonomous region

The practical takeaway: Take the CSC exam. Without it, your career options are limited. The exam is offered multiple times per year in testing centers across Mindanao. Study for it the way you would study for a board exam — it covers vocabulary, grammar, mathematics, and general information about Philippine government and the Constitution.

4.12 Other Key Civil Service Issuances

Several CSC issuances affect your daily work. You do not need to memorize them, but you should know they exist:

Issuance Subject What It Means for You
CSC MC No. 14, s. 201817 Omnibus Rules on Appointments The detailed rules governing how appointments are made — posting, evaluation, attestation
2017 RACCS (CSC Res. No. 1701077)18 Rules on Administrative Cases in the Civil Service The disciplinary framework — complaints, investigation, penalties, appeals
OP MC No. 72, s. 2019 and OP MC No. 2, s. 202219 Complete Staff Work (CSW) The standard for professional staff work — your memos, briefers, and recommendations must be complete, accurate, and actionable
Various EOs Government working hours, flexible work Rules on work schedules, telecommuting, and flexible work arrangements

Complete Staff Work (CSW) deserves special attention. CSW is the principle that every document you submit to a decision-maker must be so thorough and well-prepared that the decision-maker only needs to approve or disapprove — no further research, no missing data, no incomplete analysis. CSW is the professional standard that separates competent government workers from mediocre ones. Chapters 11-14 of this guidebook will teach you how to do it.

4.13 Salary and Compensation

Government salaries follow the Salary Standardization Law (SSL), which assigns salary amounts to each Salary Grade (SG 1-33).20 All government employees at the same SG receive the same base pay, regardless of where they work.

Entry-Level Salary Ranges (SSL Step 1, approximate)

Salary Grade Monthly Base Pay (approx.) Typical Entry Positions
SG 6 ~PHP 18,000 Administrative Aide VI (clerical)
SG 9 ~PHP 22,000 Administrative Assistant III
SG 11 ~PHP 27,000 Professional entry — LSO I, Admin Officer I, Project Dev’t Officer I
SG 13 ~PHP 30,000 LSO II, Admin Officer III, DMO II
SG 15 ~PHP 38,000 LSO III, Senior Staff positions

Beyond Base Pay

Your actual take-home pay is higher than the base salary. Additional compensation includes: - COLA (Cost of Living Allowance) — PHP 2,000/month - PERA (Personnel Economic Relief Allowance) — PHP 2,000/month - Clothing/Uniform Allowance — PHP 6,000/year - Mid-Year Bonus — equivalent to 1 month salary (May) - Year-End Bonus — equivalent to 1 month salary (November) - Cash Gift — PHP 5,000 (November) - Performance-Based Bonus — variable, based on office and individual performance rating

An honest comparison: A fresh graduate at SG 11 earns approximately PHP 27,000/month base pay. With allowances and bonuses, the annualized compensation is higher. But in the private sector — especially in Manila — a comparable graduate might earn PHP 25,000-40,000/month with faster salary progression. The trade-off is job security, benefits (GSIS retirement), and the non-monetary value of public service. Make this trade-off consciously.

4.14 Your First 90 Days: What to Expect

Here is what typically happens during your first three months as a new government employee in BARMM:

Week 1: Orientation and Onboarding - Report to your office. Meet your supervisor and colleagues. - Complete enrollment forms for GSIS, PhilHealth, and Pag-IBIG. - Receive your office’s organizational chart, Citizen’s Charter, and any orientation materials. - Get access to your workspace, email, and any office systems. - Some offices conduct formal orientation programs. Others simply assign you a desk and a task. Be ready for either.

Week 2-4: Learning the Systems - Begin learning office processes, filing systems, and reporting requirements. - Draft your first IPCR targets with your supervisor’s guidance. - Start building relationships with colleagues — especially those who have been in the office longest. - Observe how memos, letters, and reports are formatted. Every office has unwritten format standards. - File your SALN if required upon assumption of duty.

Month 2: Building Competence - You should be handling routine tasks independently by now — processing documents, drafting correspondence, maintaining records. - Attend any available training — orientation on SPMS, CSW, ARTA compliance, or technical skills relevant to your office. - Ask questions. Lots of them. There is no penalty for asking; there is a penalty for guessing wrong.

Month 3: Contributing Productively - You should be producing work that contributes to your IPCR targets. - Your supervisor should have a clear picture of your strengths and development areas. - Start thinking about your career trajectory — what skills do you need to develop? What training should you pursue?

The unwritten rule: In BARMM government offices, the culture tends to be relationship-oriented. Making an effort to get to know your colleagues — their names, their roles, their working styles — is not socializing. It is essential to getting your work done. You will need their cooperation, their institutional knowledge, and their willingness to help you navigate a young bureaucracy that does not always have written procedures for everything.

What Comes Next

You now understand the employment framework — appointments, qualifications, performance management, benefits, and discipline. The next chapter covers the Bangsamoro Development Plan (BDP) — the government’s strategic roadmap. Every program, every budget allocation, and every performance target flows from the BDP. Knowing it helps you connect your daily work to the larger purpose of Bangsamoro self-governance.

  10. Rep. Act No. 11210 (2019). 105-Day Expanded Maternity Leave Law. ↩

  11. Rep. Act No. 8187 (1996). Paternity Leave Act. Grants seven (7) working days of paternity leave with full pay for the first four (4) deliveries of the legitimate spouse. ↩

  12. Rep. Act No. 8972 (2000). Solo Parents’ Welfare Act. Grants seven (7) working days of parental leave per year, as amended by Rep. Act No. 11861 (Expanded Solo Parents Welfare Act). ↩

  13. Rep. Act No. 9710 (2009). Magna Carta of Women, sec. 18. Special leave benefit of up to two (2) months with full pay for female employees who undergo surgery due to gynecological disorders. ↩

  14. Const. art. XI, sec. 17. “A public officer or employee shall, upon assumption of office and as often thereafter as may be required by law, submit a declaration under oath of his assets, liabilities, and net worth.” ↩

  15. Rep. Act No. 9485 (2007). Anti-Red Tape Act, as strengthened by Rep. Act No. 11032 (2018), Ease of Doing Business and Efficient Government Service Delivery Act. ↩

  16. Rep. Act No. 6713 (1989). Code of Conduct and Ethical Standards for Public Officials and Employees. ↩

  18. See note 9. ↩

Office of the President, Memorandum Circular No. 72, s. 2019, Strengthening the Standards of Complete Staff Work; Office of the President, Memorandum Circular No. 2, s. 2022, Mandating Strict Compliance with the Requirement of a Strengthened Complete Staff Work. Note: These are Office of the President issuances, not CSC memorandum circulars. ↩

05
The Bangsamoro Development Plan — What You're Working Toward

Chapter 5: The Bangsamoro Development Plan — What You’re Working Toward

Every memo you write, every briefer you draft, every program you implement serves one plan. Know it.

If you have ever wondered why the Bangsamoro government exists, the answer is in the 2nd Bangsamoro Development Plan (BDP) 2023-2028.1 This document is the government’s roadmap. It tells every ministry, every office, every staff member what BARMM is trying to achieve and how it plans to get there. When you join the government, your daily work connects to this plan. Your performance targets trace back to it. Your budget proposals must justify spending against it. The BDP is not a shelf document. It is the operating system.

This chapter gives you the essentials. You do not need to memorize all 14 chapters. But you do need to understand the goals, the strategies, the targets, and the ethical framework. When an interviewer asks “What do you know about the BDP?” you should be able to answer with substance.

5.1 What the BDP Is

The 2nd Bangsamoro Development Plan covers the period 2023 to 2028.2 It is the successor to the 1st BDP (2020-2022), which guided BARMM through its initial years of institution-building. The Bangsamoro Planning and Development Authority (BPDA) led the formulation of the plan in coordination with all 15 ministries, attached agencies, and development partners.3

The BDP is organized into 14 chapters covering governance, economy, peace and security, social services, culture, infrastructure, and cross-cutting concerns.4 It is publicly available through the BPDA website and the BARMM Official Gazette.

Why the BDP matters to you personally:

  • Every ministry and office aligns its Annual Work and Financial Plan to the BDP
  • Every Individual Performance Commitment and Review (IPCR) should reference BDP alignment
  • Every policy recommendation you write should cite the relevant BDP chapter
  • Every budget proposal must justify spending against BDP targets
  • During job interviews, knowledge of the BDP demonstrates strategic awareness that most applicants lack

5.2 The Overall Goal

The BDP’s overall goal is:

An empowered, cohesive, and progressive Bangsamoro.5

Each word carries specific meaning:6

Term What It Means
Empowered Improved capacity of communities through enhanced access to quality basic social services and widened economic opportunities via institution building and service delivery
Cohesive Improved relationships between stakeholders evidenced in reduced violent conflict and forged partnerships in governance and development
Progressive Sustained ability to optimize existing and potential resources addressing community development needs and realizing economic and human potential

When someone says “empowered Bangsamoro,” they are not using a slogan. They are referencing a measurable outcome: communities that have access to quality services and economic opportunity because the government built the institutions to deliver them.

5.3 The Vision and Mission

The Vision

“A Bangsamoro that is united, enlightened, self-governing, peaceful, just, morally upright, and progressive.”7

This vision is long-term. It extends beyond the 2028 plan period. Key elements like peace, unity, and justice remain relevant as desired states the Bangsamoro people continue to work toward.

The Mission

“Guided by moral governance and in pursuit of genuine and meaningful autonomy, the Bangsamoro government ensures the necessary conditions for enduring peace and sustained socioeconomic development suitable to the systems of life, needs, and aspirations of its people by providing services to communities, ensuring multi-stakeholder participation, and facilitating appropriate partnership.”8

Two phrases in the mission statement deserve your attention:

  • “Systems of life” – this means development that fits the Bangsamoro people’s way of living, not development imposed from outside
  • “Multi-stakeholder participation” – the government does not work alone. It partners with communities, civil society, the private sector, and development partners

5.4 The Six Development Goals

These six goals define what BARMM is working toward. Every office in the government contributes to at least one of them.9

Goal 1: Stable, Just, and Accountable Government

What it means:

  • Stable – the government continues to function during emergencies, elections, or changes in power. Ministries deliver services even during shocks.
  • Just – the government is the primary agent of economic and social justice. Leaders exercise power to advance the public interest, not personal gain.
  • Accountable – institutions, leaders, and staff are held responsible for how they use government resources and powers. Accountability frameworks and systems are in place.

Which offices work on this: All ministries (governance is everyone’s job), plus MFBM (fiscal accountability), the Bangsamoro Internal Auditing Body (BIAB),24 and the Office of the Chief Minister (OCM).

What your work might look like: Preparing compliance reports, developing standard operating procedures, processing financial documents with proper documentation, supporting performance evaluations.

Goal 2: Equitable, Competitive, and Robust Economy

What it means:

  • Equitable – economic gains benefit all, with special consideration for the poor and marginalized. Key inequality indicators (Gini coefficient, poverty gap) improve.
  • Competitive – BARMM enjoys comparative advantage in selected commodities (seaweed, cassava, rubber, palm oil) with optimized local and international market linkages.25
  • Robust – sustained growth over six years with a diversified, stabilized economy that creates quality jobs.

Which offices work on this: MTIT (trade and tourism), MAFAR (agriculture and fisheries), MOLE (labor and employment), BBOI (investment promotion), MOST (science and technology).

What your work might look like: Analyzing trade data, supporting cooperative registration, drafting investment incentive policies, coordinating livelihood programs, processing business permits.

Goal 3: Peaceful, Safe, and Resilient Bangsamoro Communities

What it means:

  • Achieve lasting peace through ensuring security and intensifying peace-building efforts
  • Create safe communities by protecting Bangsamoro people
  • Build resilient communities that can withstand natural calamities and human-induced disasters

Which offices work on this: MPOS (public order and safety), MILG (interior and local government), the Bangsamoro Regional Disaster Risk Reduction and Management Office (BRDRRMO), OCM peace and normalization units.

What your work might look like: Supporting peace and order councils, preparing disaster preparedness plans, coordinating with local governments on community safety, processing normalization program documents.

Goal 4: Inclusive, Responsive, and Quality Social Services

What it means:

  • Social services are inclusive – available to all sectors regardless of tribe, religion, or political affiliation
  • Services are responsive – they address the actual needs of communities, not just what the government decides to provide
  • Services are quality – they meet standards that improve lives

Which offices work on this: MBHTE (education), MOH (health), MSSD (social services), MHSD (human settlements), MOLE (employment services).

What your work might look like: Tracking school enrollment and dropout rates, processing beneficiary lists for social protection programs, coordinating health service delivery in municipalities, supporting scholarship program administration.

Goal 5: Rich and Diverse Bangsamoro Culture and Identity Preserved and Recognized

What it means:

  • The Bangsamoro exercises its powers to maintain and promote its rich, diverse culture and identity
  • Mandated under Section 24, Article IX of the BOL10 – cultural preservation is not optional. It is a constitutional responsibility.
  • The BOL recognizes that Bangsamoro identity is the primary cause of the Bangsamoro struggle. Preserving it is inseparable from the peace process.

Which offices work on this: BCPCH (cultural heritage preservation), MIPA (indigenous peoples’ affairs), MBHTE (cultural education), the Office of the Wali (moral and cultural authority).

What your work might look like: Documenting cultural heritage sites, supporting indigenous peoples’ rights programs, developing culturally responsive curricula, coordinating cultural events and festivals.

Goal 6: Strategic, Adequate, and Climate-Resilient Infrastructure

What it means:

  • Strategic – infrastructure that has broad-based, inclusive effects on industries and communities (not bridges to nowhere)
  • Adequate – infrastructure that meets the demands of communities and industries (not token projects)
  • Climate-resilient – infrastructure that withstands calamities induced by climate change

Which offices work on this: MPW (public works), MOTC (transportation and communications), MENRE (environment and natural resources), MHSD (human settlements).

What your work might look like: Processing infrastructure project proposals, monitoring construction progress, preparing environmental compliance documents, coordinating road network plans with LGUs.

5.5 The Eight Development Strategies

The six goals tell you what BARMM wants to achieve. The eight strategies tell you how.11

  1. Improve and strengthen governance mechanisms for a stable, just, and accountable Bangsamoro government
  2. Create an enabling environment towards an equitable, competitive, and robust economy
  3. Harness technology and innovations to increase socioeconomic opportunities and improve government services
  4. Improve ecological integrity and resilience of communities and ensure a healthy and clean environment
  5. Enhance and strengthen peace, public order, safety, and security and uphold human rights for inclusive, peaceful, progressive, and sustainable development
  6. Ensure inclusive and equitable access to quality services for social justice and human capital development
  7. Mainstream Bangsamoro cultural diversity, beliefs, heritage, and identity
  8. Scale up functional, strategic, climate-resilient, and quality infrastructure to support sustainable socioeconomic development

Notice how the strategies map to the goals:

Strategy Primary Goal Served
Strategy 1 (Governance) Goal 1 (Stable government)
Strategy 2 (Economy) Goal 2 (Robust economy)
Strategy 3 (Technology) Goals 1, 2, 4 (cross-cutting)
Strategy 4 (Environment) Goal 3 (Resilient communities)
Strategy 5 (Peace and security) Goal 3 (Peaceful communities)
Strategy 6 (Social services) Goal 4 (Quality services)
Strategy 7 (Culture) Goal 5 (Culture preserved)
Strategy 8 (Infrastructure) Goal 6 (Climate-resilient infrastructure)

Strategy 3 – harnessing technology and innovations – is cross-cutting. It applies to governance (digital government services), economy (technology-enabled agriculture and trade), and social services (e-learning, telemedicine). This means every office, not just MOST, has a role in technology adoption.

5.6 The Twelve Pillars of Moral Governance

The BDP is not only about economic targets and infrastructure. It is grounded in an ethical framework called the Twelve Pillars of Moral Governance.12 These pillars are the centerpiece of BARMM’s leadership and governance for 2023-2028. They are cited in performance evaluations, referenced in legislation, and expected in daily conduct.

  1. Integrity – Avoid conflicts of interest in executing official duties. Those in positions of public trust should not bind themselves in any manner, whether financially or otherwise.

  2. Accountability – Be open and transparent about decisions and actions. Document decisions and limit information only when the larger public interest clearly requires it.

  3. Transparency and Honesty – Declare private interests related to public duties. Resolve conflicts in a way that protects the public interest. All public transactions should be made public.

  4. Sense of Patriotism – Invest in the reputation and integrity of the Bangsamoro. Protect the community’s achievements, history, and way of life.

  5. Striving for Excellence – Uphold the legitimacy of all actions. Deliver the highest quality in everything, prioritizing process over results for the good of all.

  6. Cultural Understanding and Tolerance – Gain better understanding of socioeconomic differences between societies. Appreciate the variety within the Bangsamoro.

  7. Unwavering Loyalty to God – Strict adherence to rules and regulations as a result of devotion to God. This pillar reflects the Islamic foundation of Bangsamoro governance.

  8. Justice – Serve the public without prejudice. Equity should triumph without regard for oneself, family, companions, or political alliances.

  9. Inclusivity – Foster welcoming environments free from discrimination based on religion, ethnicity, or culture. Appreciate the region’s identity-building and cultural characteristics.

  10. Objectivity – Make merit-based decisions in public business, including appointments, contracts, and awards. Decide solely in the public interest.

  11. Dialogue and Meaningful Engagement – Consult the community. Engage in meaningful dialogue to identify needs and achieve positive change.

  12. Balance Between Practical Life and Moral Side – Balance practical and ethical values in carrying out government duties. Never deviate from the moral aspect of public service.

What the pillars mean for you as a new employee:

These are not decorative values. When your supervisor evaluates your performance, they may reference these pillars. When the HRMPSB interviews you, they may ask what moral governance means to you. When you face an ethical dilemma at work – a colleague asks you to backdate a document, a politician pressures you to approve an unqualified applicant, a contractor offers you a “gift” – these pillars are your guide.

The most practical pillar for daily work is Pillar 10: Objectivity. It says decisions must be merit-based. This applies to everything from hiring (the HRMPSB process) to procurement (competitive bidding) to budget allocation (BDP alignment). When in doubt, ask: “Is this decision based on merit and public interest?”

5.7 Macroeconomic Targets

The BDP sets measurable targets for the Bangsamoro economy. These numbers appear in budget proposals, program evaluations, and policy briefs. You should know the key ones.13

Indicator Baseline End-of-Plan Target (2028)
GRDP Annual Average Growth Rate 7.5% (2020-2021) 8-9% for six years
GRDP Per Capita Growth Rate 6.4% (2020-2021) 6-9%
Industry/Services Real Growth Industry 7.9%, Services 6.6% 10-12% (double-digit)
Gross Capital Formation (% of GRDP) 16.91% (2021) 18-20% by 2028
Inflation Rate 2.40% (2021) 2-4%
Unemployment Rate 3.80% (2021) 3-5%
Underemployment Rate 9.0% (2020) 4-7%
Poverty Incidence 29.80% (2021) 20-25% by 2028

Source: 2nd BDP, Chapter 4, Table 4.1 (PSA Reports)14

Key assumptions behind these targets:

  • No sustained large-scale armed conflict in the plan period
  • Significant progress in peace and normalization
  • Continued recovery from the pandemic
  • Government expenditure as an economic stimulus (“pump-priming”)
  • Passage of priority codes (Revenue Code, Investment Code) to create a predictable investment climate

What these numbers mean for your work:

If you work in MAFAR, your programs contribute to GRDP growth through agricultural productivity. If you work in MOLE, your employment programs help maintain the unemployment rate within target. If you work in MSSD, your social protection programs reduce poverty incidence. If you work in BPDA, you monitor all these indicators and report progress. Every ministry’s work connects to at least one macroeconomic target.

5.8 What the 1st BDP Achieved

The 1st BDP (2020-2022) set six macroeconomic targets. Five were achieved:15

Target Result
GRDP Growth Rate 7.5% – Achieved
Population Growth 3.26% – Achieved
Labor Force Participation 60% – Exceeded (target was 46-50%)
Unemployment 7.60% – Not Met (target was 3%)
Inflation 2.40% – Achieved
Poverty Incidence (families) 29.80% – Exceeded (target was 49.6%)

The poverty reduction from 54.20% (2018) to 29.80% (2021) was the most significant achievement – a 24.4 percentage point reduction in three years.16 However, unemployment remained above target, and the COVID-19 pandemic disrupted economic activity.

The 2nd BDP builds on these gains while addressing persistent challenges: underemployment, infrastructure gaps, education quality, and health service access.

5.9 Vertical Alignment – How the BDP Connects to National and International Plans

The BDP does not exist in isolation. It aligns vertically with higher-level plans:

  • National: Philippine Development Plan 2023-2028 and Ambisyon Natin 204017
  • Regional: Mindanao Development Agenda26
  • International: United Nations Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs)27
  • BARMM-specific: Enhanced 12-Point Priority Agenda18

This alignment matters because BARMM receives an Annual Block Grant from the national government (equal to 5% of net national internal revenue)19 and a Special Development Fund (PHP 5 billion per year).20 The national government expects BARMM’s spending to align with national priorities. Development partners (UNDP, World Bank, JICA, USAID) align their support with the SDGs. When you write a project proposal, showing alignment with both the BDP and the SDGs strengthens your case.

5.10 The Enhanced 12-Point Priority Agenda

The Enhanced 12-Point Priority Agenda is the Chief Minister’s implementation framework within the BDP.21 It translates the BDP’s broad goals into specific priorities for the transition period.

Priority Focus Key Offices
1. Bureaucracy Strengthen the regional bureaucracy All MOAs
2. Priority Codes Enact the 7 priority codes Parliament, Legal offices
3. Revenue Generation Increase revenue and economic advantages MFBM, BBOI, MTIT
4. Social Services Education, health, social welfare MBHTE, MOH, MSSD
5. Infrastructure Strategic infrastructure development MPW, MOTC
6. Economic Development Inclusive economic growth MTIT, MAFAR, MOLE
7. Environment Natural resources protection MENRE
8. Disaster Resilience Community resilience MILG, BRDRRMO
9. Peace and Normalization Peace agreement provisions OCM, MPOS
10. Marawi Rehabilitation Marawi City recovery Task Force Bangon Marawi, MHSD
11. Peace and Security Sustain peace, justice, security MPOS, BHRC
12. Cultural Heritage Preserve Bangsamoro culture BCPCH, MIPA

When you research a prospective office, identify which of the 12 priorities it supports. This gives you a concrete answer when an interviewer asks: “How does this office’s work connect to the Chief Minister’s agenda?”

5.11 How to Connect YOUR Work to the BDP

Here is the chain of connection:

BDP Goal → Development Strategy → Ministry/Office Mandate → Office Work Plan → Your IPCR

Example 1 – Education Staff at MBHTE: - BDP Goal 4: Inclusive, responsive, and quality social services - Strategy 6: Ensure inclusive and equitable access to quality services - MBHTE Mandate (BAA 13):22 Comprehensive education system - Office Work Plan: Increase school enrollment in underserved barangays - Your IPCR Target: Process 200 AHME scholarship applications per quarter

Example 2 – Legislative Staff at Parliament: - BDP Goal 1: Stable, just, and accountable government - Strategy 1: Improve and strengthen governance mechanisms - Parliament Mandate (BOL Art. VII):23 Legislate for the Bangsamoro - Committee Work Plan: Draft and deliberate priority codes - Your IPCR Target: Prepare 4 legislative briefers per quarter on referred bills

Example 3 – Planning Staff at BPDA: - BDP Goal 1: Stable, just, and accountable government (cross-cutting) - Strategy 1: Improve and strengthen governance mechanisms - BPDA Mandate: Coordinate BDP implementation, monitor progress - Office Work Plan: Conduct mid-term review of BDP performance - Your IPCR Target: Collect and consolidate progress reports from 4 assigned ministries per quarter

How to use BDP alignment in your application:

In your cover letter, write one sentence connecting your target office’s work to a specific BDP goal. In your interview, when asked “Why this office?” reference the BDP. This demonstrates strategic awareness that separates you from applicants who have not read the plan.

5.12 Where to Read the Full BDP

The complete BDP has 14 chapters. Here are the ones most relevant to your job search:

Chapter Content Why You Need It
Chapter 1 BARMM extended transition and 1st BDP review Understand the context and what BARMM has achieved so far
Chapter 3 Bangsamoro spatial strategy Know the functional roles of provinces and geographic areas
Chapter 4 Development framework The goals, strategies, and targets (this chapter covers it)
Chapter 5 Governance If you are targeting Parliament, OCM, or any governance office
Chapter 6 Economy If you are targeting MTIT, MAFAR, MOLE, BBOI
Chapter 7 Peace and security If you are targeting MPOS or peace-related offices
Chapter 8 Social services If you are targeting MBHTE, MOH, or MSSD
Chapter 9 Culture If you are targeting BCPCH or MIPA
Chapter 10 Infrastructure If you are targeting MPW or MOTC

Where to find it:

  • BPDA website (official digital copy)
  • BARMM Official Gazette
  • Ask the BPDA office directly – they distribute printed and digital copies

Practical tip: You do not need to read all 14 chapters before applying. Read Chapter 4 (development framework – the chapter you just learned) and the chapter relevant to your target office. That gives you enough to be the most informed applicant in any interview panel.

5.13 Actionable Tool: “Connect Your Work to BDP” Worksheet

Complete this worksheet for your target office. It demonstrates strategic awareness in your cover letter and interview.

BDP Goal Actions My Target Office Takes How My Skills Contribute
Goal 1: Stable, just, and accountable government ___ ___
Goal 2: Equitable, competitive, and robust economy ___ ___
Goal 3: Peaceful, safe, and resilient communities ___ ___
Goal 4: Inclusive, responsive, and quality social services ___ ___
Goal 5: Culture and identity preserved ___ ___
Goal 6: Strategic, climate-resilient infrastructure ___ ___

Instructions:

  1. Identify your target office (from Chapter 3 or Chapter 6)
  2. For each BDP goal, list 2-3 specific actions that office takes to advance it. If the goal is not directly relevant to the office, write “N/A” – no office serves all six goals equally.
  3. In the third column, write how your education, skills, or experience can contribute to those actions
  4. Use this worksheet when writing your cover letter (Chapter 8) and preparing for your interview (Chapter 9)

Example – Target Office: MSSD

BDP Goal Actions MSSD Takes How My Skills Contribute
Goal 1 Submits compliance reports, maintains performance management system My public administration degree covers government accountability systems
Goal 4 Administers BANGON livelihood program, processes humanitarian assistance, runs feeding programs My thesis on community development in Maguindanao gives me direct knowledge of beneficiary communities
Goal 5 Supports cultural activities for settler communities and IP groups My volunteer work with OOBC exposed me to multicultural community engagement

Key Takeaways

  • The 2nd BDP (2023-2028) is the government’s roadmap. All offices align to it.
  • The overall goal is an empowered, cohesive, and progressive Bangsamoro.
  • Know the 6 development goals – they define what your work contributes to.
  • Know the 8 development strategies – they define how goals become action.
  • The 12 Pillars of Moral Governance are not aspirational. They are the ethical standard you will be held to.
  • The macroeconomic targets (GRDP 8-9%, poverty 20-25%, unemployment 3-5%) are the numbers your work is measured against.
  • Connect your work to the BDP in your cover letter and interview. It separates informed applicants from uninformed ones.

In the next chapter, you will choose your career track: Parliament, Ministry, Office/Agency, or LGU. Each serves the BDP differently – and each offers a different kind of working life.
