09
The Interview and Assessment

Chapter 9: The Interview and Assessment

The interview is 25% of your score. For a fresh graduate with limited experience, it might be the difference between getting hired and getting a regret letter. Prepare like it matters – because it does.

You submitted your application. The HRMPSB screened it and found that you meet the qualification standards. Now they want to meet you. This chapter covers everything you need to know about the government interview: the format, the questions, the scoring, the preparation, and the follow-up. If you prepare using this chapter, you will walk into the panel more prepared than most applicants – including those with more experience than you.

9.1 What to Expect: The Interview Format

Government interviews are structured. They are not casual conversations. Here is what you will encounter:

Panel interview: Typically 3-5 members of the HRMPSB.1 The panel usually includes: - The HR head or HR officer - A division chief or supervisor from the hiring unit - A subject matter expert - An employee representative - Sometimes an external resource person

Duration: 20-45 minutes depending on the position level. Entry-level interviews (SG 11-15) are usually 20-30 minutes.

Format: Competency-Based Interview (CBI)2 – structured questions designed to assess specific competencies from the position description. This is not a freestyle Q&A. Each question targets a specific competency. Each panelist scores independently.

Scoring: Each panelist uses a standardized rubric. They rate your answers on a scale (typically 1-5) for each competency assessed. Your interview score is the averaged total.

Recording: Some offices record interviews. You may be asked to sign a consent form before the interview begins. This is standard procedure for documentation purposes.

9.2 Competency-Based Interviews (CBI) – The STAR Method

Every CBI question asks you to describe a past situation where you demonstrated a specific competency. The panel is not interested in what you would hypothetically do. They want to know what you actually did.

The standard framework for answering is STAR:3

Element What It Is How to Answer
S – Situation Set the scene. Where? When? What was happening? Be specific. “During my thesis research on agricultural cooperatives in Maguindanao in April 2025…”
T – Task What was your specific role or responsibility? Clarify YOUR role, not the team’s. “I was responsible for conducting interviews with 30 cooperative leaders across 5 municipalities…”
A – Action What did YOU specifically do? Use “I” not “we.” “I designed a bilingual questionnaire in Filipino and Maguindanaon, coordinated with barangay captains for introductions, and scheduled interviews over three weeks…”
R – Result What happened? Quantify if possible. Show the outcome. “We completed all 30 interviews on schedule. The thesis earned a grade of 1.25, and the cooperative federation adopted two of our recommendations.”

STAR Rules

  • Be specific. “I helped with a project” tells the panel nothing. “I managed the logistics for a youth summit with 200 participants from 5 provinces” tells them everything.
  • Use “I” not “we.” The panel is assessing you, not your team. Even in team projects, clarify what you personally contributed.
  • Quantify results. Numbers make your answers credible. “30 interviews,” “200 participants,” “3-week timeline,” “grade of 1.25” are all stronger than vague descriptions.
  • Keep it under 2 minutes. A good STAR answer is 90-120 seconds. Longer answers lose the panel’s attention.

Where Fresh Graduates Find STAR Stories

You do not need work experience to have STAR stories. Your “situations” come from:

  • Thesis or capstone research – research design, data collection, stakeholder engagement, writing under deadline
  • Student organization leadership – planning events, managing budgets, coordinating teams, resolving conflicts
  • Volunteer work and community service – disaster response, barangay programs, community outreach
  • Part-time jobs, OJT, or internships – real work experience even if short-term
  • Family responsibilities – managing a household IS project management. Coordinating family events IS logistics.
  • Personal challenges overcome – resilience, problem-solving, adapting to difficult circumstances

9.3 Common Interview Questions for BARMM Positions

Governance Awareness Questions

Expect these in every BARMM interview. They test whether you understand the government you are joining.

“What do you know about the Bangsamoro Organic Law?”

How to prepare: Review Chapter 2. Know that RA 110544 established BARMM, was ratified by plebiscite in 2019, gives the Bangsamoro government 55 enumerated powers, creates a parliamentary system, and provides fiscal autonomy through the Annual Block Grant and Special Development Fund.5 You do not need to recite sections. Demonstrate understanding.

“Can you name the 6 goals of the Bangsamoro Development Plan?”

How to prepare: Review Chapter 5. List them:16 1. Stable, just, and accountable government 2. Equitable, competitive, and robust economy 3. Peaceful, safe, and resilient Bangsamoro communities 4. Inclusive, responsive, and quality social services 5. Rich and diverse Bangsamoro culture and identity preserved and recognized 6. Strategic, adequate, and climate-resilient infrastructure

Then connect one goal to the office you are applying to. “Since I am applying to MSSD, I am most drawn to Goal 4. MSSD’s social protection programs directly advance inclusive and quality social services.”

“What is the mandate of [this office] under BAA 13?”

How to prepare: Before every interview, look up the office’s mandate in BAA 13 (the Bangsamoro Administrative Code).6 You studied this in Chapter 3. State the mandate in your own words, not word-for-word recitation.

“What does moral governance mean to you?”

How to prepare: Review the 12 Pillars of Moral Governance from Chapter 5.7 Choose 2-3 pillars that resonate with you and explain why. Connect them to how you would conduct yourself in the position.

“How does BARMM differ from ARMM? Why did ARMM fail?”

How to prepare: ARMM (Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao) had limited powers and was widely seen as ineffective. BARMM has a parliamentary system, enumerated powers over 55 areas,8 its own civil service code,9 fiscal autonomy,10 and was created through a peace agreement (the Comprehensive Agreement on the Bangsamoro, signed March 27, 2014).15 The key difference is genuine autonomy with real powers, not just a name change.

Competency Questions (STAR Format)

These questions assess your core competencies. Prepare a STAR story for each.

Critical Thinking: “Tell me about a time you had to analyze a complex problem and propose a solution.” - Thesis research, data analysis, case studies, organizational problem-solving

Cultural Competence: “Describe a situation where you worked with people from different backgrounds.” - Group projects with diverse classmates, volunteer work in multicultural communities, student exchange, community immersion

Results Orientation: “Give an example of when you had to meet a tight deadline with limited resources.” - Academic deadlines, event planning with a small budget, thesis submission, OJT deliverables

Openness to Feedback: “Tell me about a time you received negative feedback. How did you respond?” - Thesis defense revisions, supervisor corrections during OJT, constructive criticism from org advisers

Initiative and Project Management: “Describe a project you led from start to finish.” - Student organization events, community projects, research from proposal to defense, family or community occasions you organized

BARMM-Specific Questions

These questions are unique to BARMM. They test your motivation and cultural fit.

“Why do you want to work in BARMM government specifically?”

This is your most important answer. Do not say “I need a job” or “Government work has benefits.” Connect your motivation to: - The peace process and what BARMM represents - Specific development challenges you want to help address - Personal connection to the Bangsamoro community (if applicable) - The opportunity to build systems in a new government

“How would you handle working with colleagues from different ethnolinguistic groups?”

BARMM has 13 ethnolinguistic groups.11 Acknowledge the diversity. Share an experience working with people different from you. Show respect, openness, and willingness to learn.

“What role does Islamic governance play in public service?”

Answer respectfully regardless of your faith. The 12 Pillars of Moral Governance12 are rooted in Islamic principles (amanah/trustworthiness, adl/justice, shura/consultation) but apply to all government workers. Focus on values: integrity, accountability, service orientation.

“What contribution can you make to the Bangsamoro transition?”

This is a vision question. Speak about the specific skills you bring, the work you want to do, and how you see yourself contributing to building institutions that will serve the first elected Bangsamoro government.

9.4 For Fresh Graduates: Compensating for Limited Experience

The panel knows you are a fresh graduate. They do not expect 5 years of government experience. They expect potential, preparation, and authenticity. Here is how to stand out.

Lead with Preparation

The single most powerful thing you can say in an interview:

“I may not have government experience, but I have studied your office’s mandate under BAA 13, read the relevant BDP chapter, and prepared a draft [output relevant to the position] to show what I can contribute.”

Most applicants – even experienced ones – have not read the BOL, BAA 13, or the BDP. If you have, you are already ahead.

Reframe Academic Work as Professional Competence

Your academic experience is real experience. Frame it as such:

Academic Experience How to Frame It
Thesis research “I conducted primary research involving interviews with 30 stakeholders across 5 municipalities”
Student org presidency “I led a team of 15 officers, managed an annual budget of PHP 50,000, and organized 8 events”
Volunteer work “I coordinated a disaster response program that distributed relief goods to 200 families”
OJT / Internship “I completed 600 hours of on-the-job training at [organization], where I [specific deliverable]”
Group thesis “I managed the data collection and analysis components for a team of 4 researchers”

Show Learning Speed

Fresh graduates win interviews by demonstrating that they learn fast:

“In my OJT at [organization], I was given a beneficiary tracking database with no instructions. Within two weeks, I had learned the system, identified three data entry errors, and proposed a validation checklist that the office adopted.”

The panel takes away: this person can be productive quickly without hand-holding.

Demonstrate BOL/BDP Knowledge

When you reference the BOL or BDP naturally in your answers, the panel notices. For example:

“I am drawn to this position because MSSD’s work on social protection directly supports BDP Goal 4. I read in the BDP Chapter 8 that BARMM has the highest stunting rates in the Philippines. I want to be part of the team addressing that.”

Bring a Portfolio

A physical folder with your best academic output is unusual and impressive. Include: - Thesis or capstone abstract (1-2 pages) - A sample research paper or policy brief - Event documentation from student organization activities - Any certificates of recognition

You do not need to hand it over unless asked. Having it signals preparation.

9.5 Written Exams and Skills Tests

Some positions require additional assessments beyond the panel interview.

What to Expect

  • Analytical writing test: Write a memo, policy brief, or essay on a given topic (common for planning and policy positions)
  • Multiple choice exam: General knowledge, government policies, position-specific content
  • Practical exercise: Draft a sample document relevant to the position (e.g., a legislative briefer for LSO positions, a project proposal for planning positions)
  • Technical exam: Subject matter test for specialized positions (health, agriculture, education, finance, engineering)

How to Prepare

  • Review the office’s recent outputs. If you are applying to a Parliament committee, read its published committee reports. If you are applying to MSSD, review its published program guidelines.
  • Study the relevant BAA. The written exam may ask about provisions of BAA 13, BAA 17, or the BAA specific to the office.
  • Practice government writing. Government writing is different from academic writing. It is shorter, more direct, action-oriented, and follows specific formats (memo, briefer, endorsement). Review the memo format in Chapter 13 if this guidebook’s later chapters are available, or ask the HR office what format the office uses.
  • For technical positions: Review your professional subject matter. An Agricultural Technician position will test agricultural knowledge. A Nurse position will test clinical knowledge.

9.6 Cultural Appropriateness

How you present yourself matters. These guidelines help you make the right impression.

Dress Code

Business formal. The interview is a professional setting.

  • For men: Long-sleeved polo or barong tagalog, slacks (not jeans), closed shoes (not sandals). A necktie is optional but appropriate.
  • For women: Modest business attire. Consider that many BARMM offices observe Islamic dress norms. Avoid sleeveless tops. Wear knee-length or longer skirts/slacks. A headscarf is not required unless you choose to wear one, but dress modestly.

Greetings

“Assalamu Alaikum” (peace be upon you) is the standard greeting in BARMM offices. It is appropriate regardless of your faith. The standard response is “Wa Alaikum Assalam” (and upon you, peace).

If you are unsure, “Good morning” or “Good afternoon” is always acceptable.

Addressing Panelists

  • “Sir” or “Ma’am” is always safe
  • Use professional titles when known: “Director,” “Attorney,” “Engineer,” “Doctor”
  • For Members of Parliament: “Honorable” (e.g., “Honorable Member” or “Your Honor”)
  • When in doubt, “Sir/Ma’am” works

Demeanor

  • Respectful confidence. You are not begging for a job. You are presenting your qualifications.
  • Look panelists in the eye when speaking. This signals sincerity.
  • Do not interrupt. Wait for the panelist to finish the question completely before answering.
  • “Thank you for the question” before answering buys you 3-5 seconds of thinking time. Use it.
  • Sit upright but not stiffly. Lean slightly forward to show engagement.
  • Speak clearly. If you tend to speak quickly, slow down.

Punctuality

Arrive 30 minutes early. Use the extra time to review your notes, observe the office environment, and compose yourself. Being late is disqualifying in most offices. If unforeseen circumstances delay you, call the contact number on the interview notice immediately.

Phone

Silent mode, not vibrate. Do not check your phone during the interview. If it rings, ignore it. Checking your phone tells the panel that something else is more important than this interview.

9.7 After the Interview – What Happens Next

Understanding the post-interview process helps you manage expectations and plan your next steps.

Step 1: HRMPSB Deliberation

After all candidates have been interviewed, the panel consolidates individual scores into a Comparative Assessment ranking. Each candidate receives a total score based on the five criteria:

  • Education (25%)
  • Experience (25%)
  • Training (15%)
  • Eligibility (10%)
  • Interview (25%)

Step 2: Shortlist Submission

The HRMPSB submits the top 3-5 candidates to the appointing authority. The appointing authority is typically: - The Minister (for ministry positions) - The Secretary General (for Parliament Secretariat positions) - The Executive Director (for office/agency positions) - The Mayor or Governor (for LGU positions)

Step 3: Appointing Authority Decision

The appointing authority makes the final selection. They may or may not follow the HRMPSB ranking. The appointing authority has discretion, but the appointment must be justified and documented.

Step 4: Appointment Letter

If selected, you receive an appointment letter specifying: - Position title and plantilla item number - Salary grade and monthly salary - Effectivity date (when you start) - Type of appointment (permanent, temporary, coterminous) - Reporting instructions

Step 5: Oath of Office

Before you begin work, you take an oath of office. This is usually administered by the appointing authority or an authorized officer. The oath commits you to faithfully discharge your duties and uphold the Constitution and the Bangsamoro Organic Law.

Step 6: Pre-Employment Requirements

After appointment but before starting work, you typically need to: - Complete pre-employment medical examination - Open a government bank account (for salary disbursement) - Register with GSIS, PhilHealth, and Pag-IBIG (if not yet registered) - Submit additional documentary requirements as specified by the HR office

Timeline

Expect 2-8 weeks from interview to appointment. Some offices move quickly (2-3 weeks). Others take longer due to administrative processes. This is normal.

If Not Selected

You will receive a regret letter. This is not a failure. Consider the following:

  • You may request feedback from the HRMPSB. Ask what competencies to develop. Frame it as professional development, not a complaint.
  • Apply again. Most successful government workers were not hired on their first attempt. Each interview is practice for the next one.
  • Update your tracker. Note what went well and what you would do differently. Use this to prepare for your next opportunity.
  • Build your weak areas. If the feedback points to experience gaps, look for COS or volunteer opportunities. If it points to training gaps, attend seminars. If it points to interview performance, practice with a friend using the mock interview tool at the end of this chapter.

9.8 The Appointment Types You May Receive

Understanding what each appointment type means helps you make informed decisions if you receive an offer.

Type What It Means Security of Tenure Benefits
Permanent13 You met all qualification standards. You hold a plantilla position. Full tenure – you can only be removed through a formal administrative process Full: GSIS, PhilHealth, Pag-IBIG, leave credits, PBB, step increments
Temporary14 You met all QS except eligibility. Given up to 2 years to pass the CSC exam. Limited – appointment expires if you do not obtain eligibility within the period Full benefits while appointed
Coterminous17 Your appointment is tied to the appointing authority’s term (common in political staff positions) Ends when the appointing authority leaves office Full benefits while appointed
COS18 Contract of Service – project-based, no plantilla position None – contract can be terminated at the end of the period Limited – typically no GSIS or leave credits
JO Job Order – task-based, no employer-employee relationship None None

For fresh graduates: A permanent appointment is the gold standard. A temporary appointment is excellent – you have up to 2 years to pass the CSC exam while enjoying full benefits and building experience. COS is a strong stepping stone if plantilla positions are not yet available.

9.9 Actionable Tool: “Interview Preparation Worksheet”

Complete this worksheet before every interview. It organizes your preparation and gives you confidence walking into the panel.

Section 1: My STAR Stories

Prepare at least one STAR story for each competency category. Write them out in advance and practice saying them aloud.

Story 1: Thinking (Critical/Analytical) | Element | Your Answer | |—|—| | Situation | | | Task | | | Action | | | Result | |

Story 2: Communication (Written or Oral) | Element | Your Answer | |—|—| | Situation | | | Task | | | Action | | | Result | |

Story 3: Collaboration (Teamwork/Coordination) | Element | Your Answer | |—|—| | Situation | | | Task | | | Action | | | Result | |

Story 4: Values (Integrity/Service Orientation) | Element | Your Answer | |—|—| | Situation | | | Task | | | Action | | | Result | |

Story 5: Bangsamoro-Specific (Cultural Competence/BOL/BDP Awareness) | Element | Your Answer | |—|—| | Situation | | | Task | | | Action | | | Result | |

Section 2: Office Research Checklist

Complete this research before every interview. Walking in with this knowledge separates you from other candidates.

Item Your Research
Office mandate (from BAA 13)
Minister / Director / Head of Office (name and title)
Relevant BDP Goal(s)
Relevant BDP Chapter
Current programs or projects
Recent news or achievements
How this position contributes to the office’s mandate

Section 3: Mock Interview Practice

Practice these 10 questions with a friend, classmate, or family member. Have your practice partner score you on clarity, confidence, and substance (1-5 each).

# Question Clarity (1-5) Confidence (1-5) Substance (1-5)
1 What do you know about the Bangsamoro Organic Law?
2 Can you name the 6 goals of the BDP?
3 What is the mandate of this office under BAA 13?
4 Why do you want to work in BARMM government specifically?
5 Tell me about a time you analyzed a complex problem. (STAR)
6 Describe working with people from different backgrounds. (STAR)
7 Give an example of meeting a tight deadline. (STAR)
8 How did you respond to negative feedback? (STAR)
9 What does moral governance mean to you?
10 What contribution can you make to the Bangsamoro transition?

Scoring guide: - 13-15 per question: Strong. You are ready. - 10-12 per question: Good, but refine your answers. Be more specific or add numbers. - Below 10: Practice more. Write out your answers and rehearse until you can deliver them naturally.

Key Takeaways

  • The interview is a Competency-Based Interview (CBI) with a panel of 3-5 HRMPSB members. It is worth 25% of your total score.
  • Use the STAR method (Situation, Task, Action, Result) for every competency question. Be specific. Use “I” not “we.” Quantify results.
  • Expect three types of questions: governance awareness (BOL, BDP, BAA 13, moral governance), competency questions (STAR format), and BARMM-specific questions (motivation, cultural fit, transition contribution).
  • Fresh graduates compensate for limited experience through preparation (read the BOL, BDP, BAA 13), reframing academic work as professional competence, and demonstrating learning speed.
  • Cultural appropriateness matters: dress in business formal, greet with “Assalamu Alaikum” or “Good morning,” address panelists respectfully, arrive 30 minutes early, keep your phone silent.
  • After the interview: the HRMPSB ranks candidates, submits the top 3-5 to the appointing authority, and the authority makes the final decision. Expect 2-8 weeks from interview to appointment.
  • If not selected: request feedback, build your weak areas, and apply again. Most successful government workers were not hired on their first attempt.
  • Complete the Interview Preparation Worksheet before every interview. It is 30 minutes of preparation that can determine the outcome.

You now have everything you need to find a position, apply competitively, and ace the interview. Part III of this guidebook prepares you for what comes after: succeeding once you are inside.

10
How Work Gets Done: Complete Staff Work

Chapter 10: How Work Gets Done: Complete Staff Work

Every assignment you receive in government follows a process. That process has a name: Complete Staff Work (CSW). Learn it now, because it will shape every document you write, every recommendation you make, and every problem you solve for the rest of your government career.

What Complete Staff Work Means

Complete Staff Work is a doctrine, not just a concept.1 It means that when you give your supervisor a document, that document should be so thorough, so well-researched, and so clearly written that your supervisor only needs to do one thing: approve or disapprove.

Your supervisor should not need to: - Ask you for missing information - Rewrite your sentences - Check your legal citations - Call you back to explain what you meant - Fix your formatting

If your supervisor has to do any of those things, your staff work is not complete.

Think of it this way. A doctor does not hand a patient a half-finished diagnosis and say, “Can you look up the rest?” A mechanic does not return a car with three wheels attached and say, “I ran out of time.” Your CSW output is your professional product. It should be finished, accurate, and ready for decision.

The CSW Standard: The decision-maker should only need to approve or disapprove. Everything else is your job.

The ADDRESS IT Process

In the Bangsamoro government, CSW follows a 7-step process called ADDRESS IT.2 This is the methodology used across BARMM — in the Parliament, in ministries, in offices, and in local government units.

ADDRESS IT is a mnemonic. Each letter (or pair of letters) represents one step:

Step Letter(s) Action
Step 1 A Analyze the Context3
Step 2 DD Define and Deliberate the Problem or Need4
Step 3 R Research Needed Information5
Step 4 E Explore Options6
Step 5 SS Submit Proposal or Report7
Step 6 (SS cont.) Sustain Efforts to Implement the Solution8
Step 7 IT Iterate and Improve the Solution9

The process is cyclical.20 Step 7 can loop back to Step 1 when circumstances change or new problems emerge. Government work is never truly “done” — policies evolve, communities grow, and new challenges appear.

Let us walk through each step as if you have just received your first assignment.

Step 1: Analyze the Context

Your supervisor says: “I need a report on why our feeding program in Basilan is behind schedule.”

Before you start writing, stop and analyze. This step lays the foundation for everything that follows.

What to do in Step 1

Understand the assignment. What exactly does your supervisor want? A report? A recommendation? A memo for the Minister? Clarify ambiguities now — not after you have written 10 pages in the wrong direction.

Assess your office’s function. How does this assignment connect to your office’s mandate under BAA 1310 (the Bangsamoro Administrative Code)? If you work for the Ministry of Social Services and Development (MSSD), the feeding program falls squarely within your mandate. If you work for the Ministry of Finance, and Budget and Management (MFBM), your angle might be the budget utilization rate instead.

Identify stakeholders. Who is affected by this feeding program? The beneficiaries (children and families in Basilan), the implementing LGUs, the municipal social workers, the provincial government, development partners who co-funded the program. List them. You will need their perspectives later.

Examine internal and external factors. Internally: does your office have enough staff to monitor the program? Is the budget released on time? Externally: are there access issues in Basilan’s island municipalities? Are there security concerns? Is it typhoon season?

Review relevant laws and policies. What BAA or memorandum order governs the feeding program? What national laws apply (RA 11037,11 the Masustansyang Pagkain para sa Batang Pilipino Act)? What are the BDP targets for child nutrition (Goal 4: Inclusive, Responsive, and Quality Social Services)?12

Gather preliminary data. Pull whatever information is readily available — last quarter’s monitoring report, the program’s approved work plan, disbursement records.

Why this step matters

Skipping contextual analysis is the most common mistake new staff make. You rush to write the report without understanding the full picture. The result: a document that addresses symptoms instead of causes, ignores key stakeholders, or recommends actions outside your office’s mandate.

Fresh graduate tip: When your supervisor gives you an assignment, do not immediately start typing. Spend the first 30 minutes reading, asking questions, and mapping the landscape. Those 30 minutes will save you hours of rework.

Step 2: Define and Deliberate the Problem or Need

Now that you understand the context, define the problem clearly. This sounds simple. It is not.

What to do in Step 2

Articulate the problem. Write it in one sentence. Not “The feeding program has issues” but “The Basilan feeding program has reached only 38% of its FY 2026 target beneficiaries as of March 2026, primarily due to delayed fund release and insufficient municipal social workers in 3 island municipalities.”

Identify root causes. Use the “5 Whys” method21: 1. Why is the program behind? Because only 38% of beneficiaries have been served. 2. Why only 38%? Because 3 island municipalities have not started implementation. 3. Why haven’t they started? Because the funds were released 2 months late. 4. Why were funds late? Because the municipality did not submit liquidation reports for the previous tranche. 5. Why didn’t they submit? Because they have no trained staff to prepare the reports.

The root cause is not “the program is slow.” The root cause is capacity and compliance gaps at the municipal level.

Engage stakeholders. Talk to the municipal social workers, the provincial coordinator, the budget officer. Their perspectives will reveal things that data alone cannot show. In the Bangsamoro context, this deliberation reflects the Islamic principle of shura — collective consultation before decision-making.13

Establish scope. Define what your CSW covers and what it does not. You are analyzing the Basilan feeding program delays, not redesigning the entire national feeding strategy.

Set SMART objectives.22 Your CSW should aim for something specific: “Identify the root causes of implementation delays in 3 Basilan municipalities and recommend corrective actions achievable within Q3 FY 2026.”

Why this step matters

A poorly defined problem leads to a poorly designed solution. If you define the problem as “the program is slow,” your recommendation might be “speed it up” — which tells your supervisor nothing useful. If you define it as a capacity gap, your recommendation can be specific: deploy a technical assistance team, conduct liquidation report training, or adjust the disbursement schedule.

Step 3: Research Needed Information

With your problem defined, gather the evidence. This step separates CSW from guesswork.

What to do in Step 3

Develop a research plan. What information do you need? Where will you find it? How long will it take? A simple research plan prevents you from drowning in data or running out of time.

Identify sources. For a BARMM staff member, your sources include: - Government data: MSSD monitoring reports, MFBM disbursement records, PSA statistics - Legal references: BAAs, national laws, memorandum circulars,14 executive orders - BDP targets: The relevant chapter of the 2nd Bangsamoro Development Plan 2023-202815 - Stakeholder input: Interviews with municipal staff, field reports from social workers - Best practices: How other BARMM provinces or national programs handle similar challenges

Evaluate source credibility.23 Not all data is equal. An official MSSD report is more reliable than an unverified social media post. A PSA-validated statistic is more credible than an estimate from a single barangay captain.

Synthesize findings. Do not just collect data — analyze it. Look for patterns, contradictions, and gaps. If three municipalities report the same problem (no trained staff), that is a systemic issue, not an isolated case.

Document everything. Keep a record of every source you consulted, every person you interviewed, every document you reviewed. Your supervisor may ask, “Where did you get this number?” You should be able to answer immediately.

Why this step matters

CSW built on insufficient or unreliable data will fail. Your supervisor and the decision-maker above them will lose trust in your work if your facts are wrong. In government, credibility is your most valuable asset. Once lost, it takes years to rebuild.

Step 4: Explore Options

You have the context, the problem definition, and the evidence. Now generate solutions — more than one.

What to do in Step 4

Generate multiple options.24 Never present only one solution. Decision-makers need choices. For the Basilan feeding program, your options might be:

Option Description Cost Timeline Risk
Option A Deploy MSSD technical assistance team to 3 municipalities for 2 weeks PHP 150,000 (travel, per diem) 1 month Low — uses existing staff
Option B Conduct online liquidation training for all Basilan municipal social workers PHP 50,000 (platform, materials) 2 weeks Medium — internet connectivity issues
Option C Transfer implementation to provincial government with MSSD oversight PHP 0 additional 2 months (transition) High — capacity at provincial level unverified

Evaluate each option against clear criteria: - Feasibility: Can you actually do this with available resources? - Effectiveness: Will it solve the root cause? - Efficiency: Does it use resources wisely? - Sustainability: Will the fix last, or will the problem recur? - Alignment: Does it support BDP goals and your office mandate?25

Conduct cost-benefit analysis.26 Option B costs the least but may not work due to connectivity. Option A costs more but directly addresses the capacity gap. Option C costs nothing upfront but risks further delays.

Recommend the best option. Based on your analysis, recommend one option and explain why. Provide your reasoning clearly so the decision-maker can follow your logic.

Why this step matters

Presenting a single option forces the decision-maker into a yes-or-no choice. Presenting multiple options with analysis demonstrates thoroughness and respect for the decision-maker’s authority. It also protects you — if the decision-maker disagrees with your top recommendation, they can choose an alternative without sending you back to start over.

Step 5: Submit the Proposal or Report

This is where your staff work becomes a physical document that moves through the approval chain.

What to do in Step 5

Structure your document.27 A typical CSW output follows this structure: 1. Executive Summary — 1-2 paragraphs capturing the problem, recommendation, and expected benefit 2. Background and Context — why this issue matters 3. Problem Statement — what you found in Step 2 4. Research Findings — what the evidence shows (Step 3) 5. Options Explored — the alternatives and your analysis (Step 4) 6. Recommended Solution — your recommendation with supporting rationale 7. Implementation Plan — specific actions, timelines, responsible persons, resources needed 8. Conclusion and Call to Action — what you need the decision-maker to do

Write clearly.28 Government writing is direct, structured, and action-oriented. Short sentences. Numbered paragraphs. Bold key terms. No jargon without definition. (See Chapter 13 for detailed writing guidance.)

Review and proofread.29 Check every fact, every citation, every number. Run the quality checklist: Is it accurate? Is it complete? Is it properly formatted? Are all attachments included?

Get supervisor clearance. Before your document reaches the decision-maker, your immediate supervisor must review and endorse it. This is not optional — it is part of the CSW process.16 Your supervisor’s endorsement signals: “I have reviewed this work and I am satisfied with its quality.”

Submit through proper channels. Attach a transmittal memorandum17 (see Chapter 13) and route it through the established approval chain. Do not skip levels. Do not hand it directly to the Minister if it should go through your Division Chief first.

The transmittal chain

Your CSW follows this path:

Your desk → Supervisor review → Supervisor endorsement →
Division Chief (if applicable) → Director → Decision-maker

At each level, the reviewer may: - Approve and forward — your work passes - Return with comments — revise and resubmit - Reject — start over (rare if you followed Steps 1-4 properly)

Why this step matters

Even excellent analysis fails if it is poorly written, badly formatted, or submitted through the wrong channel. The decision-maker may receive dozens of documents each day. Yours must be clear enough to read in 10 minutes and complete enough to act on immediately.

Step 6: Sustain Efforts to Implement the Solution

Your CSW does not end when the decision-maker approves it. Now you implement.

What to do in Step 6

Mobilize resources. If your recommendation was approved, coordinate with the relevant offices to secure the budget, personnel, and logistics needed.

Develop action plans.30 Break the approved solution into specific tasks with deadlines and responsible persons. Share the action plan with everyone involved.

Monitor progress. Track whether implementation is on schedule. Use simple tools — a checklist, a shared spreadsheet, or a tracking memo. Report progress to your supervisor regularly.

Engage stakeholders. Keep everyone informed — the implementing units, the beneficiaries, the partner offices. In the Bangsamoro context, this includes community engagement in the local language and through culturally appropriate channels.

Provide support. If the implementing team encounters obstacles, help remove them. CSW is not “submit and forget.” Your ownership continues until the solution is working.

Why this step matters

Approved solutions that are never implemented are the most common waste in government. The filing cabinets of every ministry contain brilliant recommendations that never became action. Sustained follow-through is what separates effective staff from paper-pushers.

Step 7: Iterate and Improve the Solution

Government work is never finished. After implementation, you evaluate and improve.

What to do in Step 7

Monitor and evaluate.31 Is the solution working? Are the KPIs being met? In the feeding program example: after deploying the technical assistance team, did the 3 municipalities start implementing? Did beneficiary numbers increase?

Collect feedback. Ask the municipal social workers: Was the training useful? What challenges remain? Ask the beneficiaries: Are you receiving the feeding supplies on time?

Identify improvements. Based on the data and feedback, identify what can be done better. Maybe the training needs a follow-up session. Maybe the disbursement schedule needs further adjustment.

Document lessons learned.32 Write a brief after-action review. What worked? What did not? What would you do differently? This becomes institutional knowledge — the next person who handles a similar issue will benefit from your experience.

Restart if necessary. If monitoring reveals that the problem has fundamentally changed — perhaps a new policy directive changes the feeding program’s design — go back to Step 1 and start a new CSW cycle.

Why this step matters

Without iteration, the same problems recur. Lessons from one project never inform the next. The organization fails to learn and improve. The best government workers are not those who produce one perfect document. They are those who continuously improve their work over time.

CSW Types You Will Encounter

Not all CSW looks the same. Here are the most common types you will produce in BARMM:33

CSW Type Purpose Where used Key feature
Legislative Briefer18 Prepares a legislator for a committee hearing or plenary session Parliament Comprehensive bill analysis with recommendation
Budget Briefer Analyzes a budget proposal for decision-makers MFBM, Parliament Committee on Appropriations Financial data, fiscal impact, BDP alignment
Policy Recommendation Proposes a policy change with evidence and analysis Any MOA Options analysis, cost-benefit, implementation plan
Project Proposal Requests approval and funding for a new project Any MOA Work plan, budget, timeline, expected outcomes
Legal Opinion Provides legal analysis on a question or issue Legal divisions, Parliament Legal basis, jurisprudence, interpretation
General CSW Covers any other staff work product (reports, assessments, reviews) Any office Depends on the assignment

As a new staff member, you will most likely start with general CSW — responding to specific assignments from your supervisor. As you gain experience, you may be asked to produce briefers, policy recommendations, or project proposals.

What “Completed” Actually Means

Here is a checklist to verify that your staff work is truly complete before you submit it:34

Check Question Yes/No
Accuracy Are all facts, figures, and citations verified?
Completeness Does the document cover everything the assignment requires?
Clarity Can the decision-maker understand it without calling you for explanation?
Structure Does it follow the standard format for this type of CSW?
Legal basis Are relevant laws, BAAs, and policies cited correctly?
BDP alignment Does the recommendation connect to a BDP goal or priority?
Attachments Are all supporting documents included and properly labeled?
Formatting Is it properly formatted (margins, fonts, page numbers, headers)?
Proofreading Is it free of spelling, grammar, and typographical errors?
Transmittal Does it have a transmittal memorandum with the correct action phrase?
Supervisor clearance Has your supervisor reviewed and endorsed it?

If you answered “No” to any of these, your work is not yet complete. Go back and fix it before submitting.

ADDRESS IT in Practice: A Quick Example

Here is how ADDRESS IT applies to a common first assignment — preparing a memorandum requesting approval of a training schedule.35

Step What you do Time
1. Analyze Read the training policy, check the budget, identify participants, confirm venue availability 1 hour
2. Define “The office needs to conduct mandatory CSW training for 25 new staff by Q2 FY 2026 per OP MC 72-2019”19 30 minutes
3. Research Check available trainers, review past training schedules, confirm per diem rates, verify CSC requirements 2 hours
4. Explore Option A: 3-day face-to-face in Cotabato City. Option B: 5-day blended (online + 2-day face-to-face). Option C: Self-paced online only. 1 hour
5. Submit Write the memo with your recommendation (Option B — balances cost and effectiveness), attach the proposed schedule, budget estimate, and participant list. Route through your supervisor. 2 hours
6. Sustain After approval, coordinate logistics, send invitations, prepare materials, monitor attendance Ongoing
7. Iterate After the training, collect feedback, assess learning outcomes, adjust the next cycle Post-training

Total time for Steps 1-5: approximately one working day. That is reasonable for a routine CSW assignment. Complex assignments (legislative briefers, policy recommendations) may take days or weeks.

The CSW Quick Reference Card

Keep this card at your desk until ADDRESS IT becomes second nature:36

Step Action Key Question
A Analyze Context What is the situation? Who is involved? What laws apply?
DD Define and Deliberate What exactly is the problem? What are the root causes?
R Research What does the evidence say? What have others done?
E Explore Options What are the possible solutions? Which is best and why?
SS Submit Proposal or Report Is my document complete and decision-ready?
(SS) Sustain Implementation Is implementation on track? Are stakeholders engaged?
IT Iterate and Improve Is the solution working? What can be done better?

Key Takeaways

  • CSW is a doctrine. Your output should be so complete that the decision-maker only needs to approve or disapprove.
  • ADDRESS IT is your process.37 Seven steps, cyclical, applicable to every assignment from a simple memo to a complex policy recommendation.
  • Start with analysis, not writing. The most common mistake is jumping to Step 5 (Submit) without doing Steps 1-4.
  • Document everything. Your research, your sources, your reasoning. Government decisions must be traceable and defensible.
  • Get supervisor clearance. No CSW output goes to the decision-maker without passing through your supervisor first.
  • Follow through. Submission is not the end. Implementation (Step 6) and improvement (Step 7) are where your work creates real impact.

Your degree got you through the door. Your ability to produce complete staff work will determine how far you go.

Back to Table of Contents
11
Competencies of a Bangsamoro Government Worker

Chapter 11: Competencies of a Bangsamoro Government Worker

Your degree got you in the door. Your competencies determine how far you go.

You may not have work experience yet. That is fine. You already have many of the competencies this chapter describes — from school, from community work, from life. This chapter helps you name them, develop them, and show them in interviews and on the job.

What Competencies Are

The Civil Service Commission (CSC) defines competencies as knowledge, skills, and abilities (KSAs) demonstrated through observable behaviors.1 That last part matters. A competency is not something you claim to have. It is something people can see you do.

  • Knowledge is what you know (facts, laws, theories, procedures)
  • Skills are what you can do (write a memo, analyze data, facilitate a meeting)
  • Abilities are your capacity to perform (critical thinking, leadership, adaptability)

In BARMM government, competencies fall into five categories. The first four are standard across government. The fifth is unique to the Bangsamoro.

Category What it covers Why it matters
Thinking Skills Critical, creative, systems, and strategic thinking Every CSW assignment starts with analysis
Communication Skills Written and oral communication Your output is judged by how well you write and speak
Collaboration Skills Teamwork, coordination, stakeholder engagement Government is never solo work
Values-Based Competencies Integrity, professionalism, accountability, service The foundation of public trust
Bangsamoro Competencies BOL literacy, cultural competence, moral governance, CSW, peace process awareness What makes BARMM different from every other employer2

Each category builds on the others. Strong thinking without communication produces analysis no one reads. Strong communication without values produces persuasive documents that mislead. All five categories together produce a competent Bangsamoro government worker.

11.1 Thinking Skills

Thinking skills are the foundation of all analytical government work. Every time your supervisor hands you an assignment, they are asking you to think before you act.

Critical Thinking26

What it is. Examining issues from multiple angles, questioning assumptions, distinguishing evidence from opinion, and drawing logical conclusions.

In government. Every CSW assignment requires you to analyze before you recommend. When your supervisor asks, “Why is the feeding program behind schedule?” — critical thinking is what keeps you from answering with the first explanation that comes to mind. Instead, you examine the data, question the obvious narrative, and identify the actual root cause.

What it looks like at different levels:

Level Behavior
Entry Presents information clearly; identifies key issues; draws logical conclusions
Intermediate Analyzes complex issues and root causes; synthesizes information from multiple sources
Advanced Conducts comprehensive research on multifaceted issues; develops innovative solutions; builds analytical capacity in others

How fresh graduates demonstrate it. Your thesis research required you to formulate a research question, review literature, analyze data, and defend your conclusions before a panel. That is critical thinking. So is debating in class, evaluating case studies, or questioning a professor’s assumption during a lecture.

Creative Thinking27

What it is. Generating novel ideas, approaches, and solutions. Challenging conventional thinking. Proposing alternatives that others have not considered.

In government. BARMM is building systems from scratch. Many offices do not have established procedures, standard templates, or proven workflows. When your supervisor says, “We need a better way to track beneficiary complaints,” creative thinking is what helps you design a solution that does not exist yet — perhaps a simple shared spreadsheet, a community feedback hotline, or a text-based reporting system for areas with limited internet.

How fresh graduates demonstrate it. Student organizations run on creative thinking. You planned events with no budget, designed recruitment campaigns with no marketing team, and found solutions to problems your advisers had never seen. That counts.

Systems Thinking28

What it is. Understanding how parts connect to form a whole. Seeing that your output affects another office’s input, which affects a community’s outcome.

In government. A budget decision at MFBM affects whether MSSD can release funds, which affects whether a municipal social worker can distribute feeding supplies, which affects whether a child in Basilan eats today. Systems thinking helps you see these connections — and avoid actions that solve one problem while creating another.

This is not just about your office. BARMM has 15 ministries, 13 executive offices, 5 provinces, 116 municipalities, and 2,590 barangays29 (BDP 2023-2028 figures; exclude adjustments from Sulu’s exclusion from BARMM per SC ruling).30 Every policy, every program, and every budget line connects to something else. A government worker who sees only their own desk will miss the bigger picture.

How fresh graduates demonstrate it. If you have ever organized a multi-day event and coordinated logistics, food, speakers, transportation, and volunteers simultaneously — you practiced systems thinking. If you have ever noticed that one failed component (a delayed speaker, a broken projector) cascaded into other problems — you understood systems.

Strategic Thinking

What it is. Seeing the big picture and planning toward it. Connecting your daily tasks to long-term goals. Anticipating consequences before they happen.

In government. Strategic thinking means connecting your memo to a BDP goal,3 your project proposal to the 12-point priority agenda, and your IPCR targets to your office’s mandate under BAA 13.4 It means asking: “If we implement this recommendation, what happens next year? In five years?”

What it looks like:

Level Behavior
Entry Understands organizational goals; aligns work with office mandate
Intermediate Connects work to BDP priorities and BOL mandates; anticipates second-order effects
Advanced Shapes organizational strategy; integrates cross-cutting concerns (gender, indigenous peoples, peace); drives long-term institutional reform

How fresh graduates demonstrate it. If you have ever planned a student organization’s activities for an entire semester — setting priorities, allocating limited funds, sequencing events so each built on the last — you practiced strategic thinking. If you have ever decided to take specific elective courses because they aligned with your career goal, that was strategic thinking too.

11.2 Communication Skills

In government, writing is working. Your supervisor judges your competence primarily through your written output. A staff member who writes well is trusted with bigger assignments. A staff member who writes poorly will spend their career doing data entry.

Written Communication31

What it is. Clear, concise, structured writing that enables the reader to understand and act.

In government. You will produce memos, briefers, policy recommendations, reports, transmittal memoranda, endorsements, minutes, letters, and emails. Every day. (Chapter 13 teaches you the specific formats.)

Government writing is NOT academic writing:

Academic writing Government writing
Long sentences, complex syntax Short sentences, simple structure
Passive voice (“It was determined that…”) Active voice (“The committee determined…”)
Builds to a conclusion at the end States the conclusion FIRST, then supports it
Demonstrates the writer’s knowledge Enables the reader’s decision
Hedging (“It appears that there may be…”) Direct (“There is a shortage of…”)

Common mistakes fresh graduates make: - Writing 300-word paragraphs when 50 words would suffice - Using “the undersigned” instead of “I” (the CSC has discouraged this)5 - Burying the main point in paragraph four instead of paragraph one - Using academic citations (APA format) instead of inline references - Forgetting to state what action they want the reader to take

How fresh graduates demonstrate it. Your thesis, term papers, and reports show writing ability. But recognize that you will need to unlearn some academic writing habits. Government writing is shorter, more direct, and always action-oriented.

Oral Communication32

What it is. Presenting ideas clearly, adapting to different audiences, listening actively, and speaking with appropriate confidence.

In government. You will brief your supervisor, present to committees, facilitate stakeholder consultations, speak in meetings, and engage communities. The contexts range from formal (committee hearings, budget defense) to informal (supervisor check-ins, team discussions) to public (community consultations, press interactions).

The multilingual dimension. BARMM operates in Filipino, English, and Arabic.6 Community engagement often requires regional languages — Meranaw, Maguindanaon, Tausug, Sama, Yakan, and others. If you speak any of these, it is a professional asset. If you do not, learn basic greetings and respectful phrases in the language of your assigned community.

How fresh graduates demonstrate it. Class presentations, thesis defense, debate competitions, student journalism, peer tutoring, event hosting — all of these developed your oral communication skills. In your interview, you demonstrate this competency in real time.

11.3 Collaboration and Teamwork Skills

Government is never solo work. Every output involves multiple people, offices, and stakeholders. The ability to work effectively with others is not optional — it is essential.

Working in Teams33

What it is. Contributing to group goals, respecting diverse viewpoints, sharing information willingly, and supporting colleagues.

In government. Your CSW output will pass through multiple reviewers. Your project will involve colleagues from different divisions. Your program will require coordination with other ministries. You cannot succeed alone.

What it looks like:

Level Behavior
Entry Works cooperatively with colleagues; shares information willingly; respects diverse viewpoints
Intermediate Coordinates across offices and ministries; resolves team conflicts; facilitates inter-agency cooperation
Advanced Builds and leads cross-functional teams; establishes inter-entity partnerships; creates collaborative frameworks

Cross-Functional Coordination34

What it is. Working with people outside your office — other MOAs, Parliament committees, LGUs, national agencies, and development partners.

In government. A feeding program involves MSSD (implementation), MFBM (budget release), MILG (LGU coordination), MOH (nutrition standards), and the LGU (service delivery). No single office can do it alone. Cross-functional coordination is how things actually get done.

Stakeholder Engagement35

What it is. Consulting communities, civil society organizations (CSOs), the private sector, and academe. Gathering input, building consensus, and maintaining relationships.

In BARMM. Stakeholder engagement means working across 13 ethnolinguistic groups36 with different languages, customs, and perspectives. A community consultation in Tausug-speaking Tawi-Tawi requires different approaches than one in Meranaw-speaking Lanao del Sur. Cultural sensitivity is not a bonus skill — it is a core competency.

Shura (Consultation)37

What it is. The Islamic principle of collective decision-making. In Arabic, shura means consultation.

In BARMM. Shura is not just a value — it is a governance practice. Major decisions are made through consultation, not unilateral directives. Committee hearings, stakeholder forums, community assemblies, and inter-agency coordination meetings are all expressions of shura. As a government worker, you participate in this practice every time you consult before deciding.

How fresh graduates demonstrate collaboration. Group projects, student organization leadership, volunteer work, community service, sports teams, family responsibilities that required coordination — all of these show teamwork capacity. In your interview, describe a specific team achievement and your role in it using the STAR method.

11.4 Values-Based Competencies

Technical competence without values is dangerous. A government worker who can write excellent memos but lacks integrity produces polished documents that mislead decision-makers. Values are the foundation of public trust.

Integrity and Ethics

What it is. Honesty, transparency, and compliance with laws and organizational policies. In BARMM: amanah (trustworthiness) — a core Islamic value and one of the 12 Pillars of Moral Governance.7

In government. Every document you produce is a public record. Every recommendation you make affects real people. Every budget you process uses public funds. Integrity means: - Reporting facts accurately, even when they are unfavorable - Declaring conflicts of interest - Not using your position for personal benefit - Following procurement rules even when shortcuts are tempting - Maintaining confidentiality on sensitive matters

What the ACSCCRaPATFEE framework says:8

Level Behavior
Entry Acts honestly; follows ethical guidelines; declares conflicts of interest; maintains confidentiality
Intermediate Navigates ethical dilemmas; ensures compliance with BOL and BAAs; promotes transparency
Advanced Shapes ethical frameworks for the organization; leads anti-corruption initiatives; serves as moral compass

Professionalism and Service Orientation38

What it is. Putting the public first. Showing up on time. Meeting deadlines. Treating colleagues and constituents with respect. Maintaining composure under pressure.

In BARMM. You serve the Bangsamoro people — including marginalized communities, conflict-affected families, indigenous peoples, and underserved barangays. Professionalism means treating the barangay captain from a remote island municipality with the same respect you give the Minister.

Results Orientation

What it is. Delivering outputs on time and to standard. Setting goals and meeting them. Not just working hard, but working toward measurable outcomes.

In government. Your work is measured through the Strategic Performance Management System (SPMS).9 Your Individual Performance Commitment and Review (IPCR) sets specific targets. At year-end, you are rated on a 5-point scale: Poor, Unsatisfactory, Satisfactory, Very Satisfactory, Outstanding. Your rating affects your Performance-Based Bonus, promotion ranking, and career trajectory.

Accountability39

What it is. Taking responsibility for your work — both successes and failures. In BARMM: adl (justice) and transparency in governance.40

In government. When your CSW contains an error, own it. When your project falls behind schedule, report it honestly. When your supervisor asks why a deadline was missed, explain the cause and propose a solution — do not make excuses or blame others.

How fresh graduates demonstrate values. Values are shown through behavior, not statements. Returning extra change. Citing sources instead of plagiarizing. Completing your share of a group project even when others slack off. Admitting when you are wrong. These behaviors, from your everyday life, are evidence of the values government demands.

11.5 The Bangsamoro Dimension

These competencies are not in the CSC Competency Dictionary.10 They are unique to BARMM — a recognition that working in the Bangsamoro government requires knowledge and sensitivities that no other Philippine government employer demands.

BOL Literacy41

What it is. Understanding Republic Act 11054,11 the Bangsamoro Organic Law — the law that created the autonomous government you are joining.

Why it matters. Every decision you make, every policy you recommend, and every program you implement operates within the BOL’s framework. If you do not know what BARMM can and cannot do under the BOL, you will propose actions outside your government’s competence — embarrassing yourself and your office.

What you should know: - Article III — Territory12 (5 provinces, Cotabato City, 63 barangays in North Cotabato as SGA municipalities; Sulu excluded per Supreme Court ruling, now Region IX)42 - Article V, Section 2 — The 55 areas of competence13 (what BARMM can do that other regions cannot) - Article VII — The Bangsamoro Government structure, including the Parliament14 - Article X — The Shari’ah justice system15 - Article XII — Fiscal autonomy (block grant, revenue powers)16

Bangsamoro Cultural Competence43

What it is. Awareness of the Bangsamoro people’s diversity, history, and identity. Understanding that “Bangsamoro” encompasses 13 ethnolinguistic groups17 — Meranaw, Maguindanaon, Tausug, Sama, Yakan, Iranun, Kalibugan, Palawanon, Molbog, Jama Mapun, Badjao, Sangil, and Teduray — plus settler communities, Other Bangsamoro Communities, and indigenous peoples.

Why it matters. You will work with colleagues and communities whose languages, customs, food, clothing, kinship systems, and conflict resolution practices differ from yours. Cultural competence is not about knowing every tradition. It is about approaching differences with respect and curiosity rather than judgment.

Practical examples: - Learning basic greetings in the language of your assigned community - Understanding that time, hierarchy, and hospitality operate differently across groups - Respecting Islamic practices (prayer times, halal food, Ramadan observance) without being asked - Recognizing that “the Bangsamoro story” is not a single story — it is many stories from many peoples

Complete Staff Work (ADDRESS IT)

What it is. The CSW methodology described in Chapter 10. ADDRESS IT is not just a process — it is a competency. Your ability to produce complete, accurate, decision-ready staff work is the single most important functional skill in BARMM government.

Why it is listed as a Bangsamoro competency. While CSW exists in concept across Philippine government, the ADDRESS IT framework is specific to BARMM.18 It was designed for this government’s context — autonomous governance, transitional period, system-building, peace agreement implementation. Knowing ADDRESS IT sets you apart from applicants who only know the generic CSC version.

Moral Governance Practice44

What it is. Living the 12 Pillars of Moral Governance19 — not just reciting them.

The 12 pillars are: integrity, accountability, transparency, patriotism, excellence, cultural understanding, loyalty to God, justice, inclusivity, objectivity, dialogue, and balance.45

Why it matters. These are not aspirational values on a poster. They are cited in performance evaluations, referenced in legislation, and expected in daily conduct. When you prepare a budget, transparency means making the numbers verifiable. When you engage a community, dialogue means genuinely listening. When you face pressure to cut corners, integrity means refusing.

Peace Process Awareness46

What it is. Understanding that your work implements the Comprehensive Agreement on the Bangsamoro (CAB)20 — a peace agreement that ended decades of armed conflict.

Why it matters. BARMM is not just another government agency. It is the institutional expression of the Bangsamoro people’s right to self-governance, won through negotiation after decades of war. Every program you run, every policy you draft, every barangay you serve delivers peace dividends to communities that have known conflict for generations.

What this means for your work. The feeding program you track in MSSD is not just nutrition data — it is a tangible benefit of peace for a family in a conflict-affected barangay. The bill you brief in Parliament is not just legislation — it is the exercise of self-governance that the CAB promised. When your work feels tedious, remember what it represents.

11.6 Functional Competencies by Track

Beyond the universal competencies above, each career track requires specialized functional skills. These are the competencies that make a legislative staff officer different from a municipal planner or a media affairs officer.

Parliament: Political Affairs Officers (PAOs) — 5 Competencies21

# Competency What it means in practice
1 Stakeholder Engagement, Community Organizing, and Constituency Building Managing the MP’s relationship with constituents; organizing community dialogues; building political support for legislation
2 Representation Skills Speaking on behalf of the MP at events; articulating the MP’s positions; liaison with other offices
3 Social Marketing, Advocacy, and Lobbying Building public support for the MP’s legislative agenda; engaging CSOs and media; persuading colleagues
4 Project, Program, and Events Management Organizing committee hearings, public consultations, and constituency events; managing logistics and budgets
5 Monitoring, Reporting, and Evaluation Tracking the MP’s legislative scorecard; reporting on committee progress; evaluating program impact

Parliament: Legislative Staff Officers (LSOs) — 5 Competencies22

# Competency What it means in practice
1 Investigation and Research Skills Conducting legislative research; gathering evidence for committee hearings; reviewing proposed bills against existing law
2 Policy Making, Analysis, and Evaluation Analyzing policy options; drafting policy papers; evaluating the impact of proposed and enacted legislation
3 Legal and Legislative Competence Understanding the BOL, BAAs, and national laws; drafting bills and resolutions; ensuring legal consistency
4 Attention to Details and Quality Producing error-free briefers, committee reports, and bill drafts; cross-referencing legal citations; maintaining version control
5 Oversight Skills Supporting the committee’s oversight function; monitoring implementing agencies; preparing oversight reports

Parliament: Public Information and Media Affairs Officers (PIMAOs) — 5 Competencies23

# Competency What it means in practice
1 Strategic Communications Developing the MP’s communication strategy; crafting key messages; managing public perception
2 Documentation and Editing Skills Photographing and recording events; editing video and written content; maintaining media archives
3 Social Media Skills Managing the MP’s official social media accounts; creating engaging content; monitoring online engagement
4 Print and Digital Media Skills Writing press releases; designing infographics and publications; producing newsletters and digital content
5 Media Handling Skills Coordinating press conferences; preparing media kits; briefing the MP before media interviews; managing media relations

Ministry, Office/Agency, and LGU Tracks47

Track Key functional competencies
Ministry (technical) Program management, budget analysis, technical expertise in your sector (health, education, agriculture, etc.), regulatory compliance
Ministry (administrative) Records management, procurement processing, HR administration, financial management, property and supply management
Office/Agency Program design and evaluation, multi-stakeholder coordination, monitoring and evaluation (M&E), policy brief writing, data analysis
LGU Community relations and engagement, frontline service delivery, local regulatory compliance, basic development planning, multi-tasking across functions

11.7 How Competencies Are Assessed

Your competencies are measured at every stage of your government career. Knowing how assessment works helps you prepare.48

Stage Assessment Method What is measured Your preparation
Hiring Competency-Based Interview (CBI) using the STAR method Specific competencies from the position description Prepare 5 STAR stories covering thinking, communication, collaboration, values, and Bangsamoro awareness
Probation (Months 1-6) Supervisor observation against position description Whether you can perform the job’s core functions Focus on producing quality CSW outputs and building relationships
Annual evaluation SPMS/IPCR with competency ratings Results (what you achieved) and behaviors (how you achieved them) Track your outputs, document your achievements, connect your work to BDP goals
Promotion Comparative Assessment with competency scoring25 Education (25%), Experience (25%), Training (15%), Eligibility (10%), Interview (25%) Accumulate training hours, build a track record of rated outputs, prepare for another CBI
Training Pre/post competency assessment Whether training improved your competency level Engage fully in training; apply new skills immediately on the job

The ACSCCRaPATFEE Framework

The 12 competencies of a CSW practitioner are organized using the ACSCCRaPATFEE mnemonic.24 This framework maps each competency to specific steps in the ADDRESS IT process:

# Competency Mnemonic Primary ADDRESS IT Steps
1 Analytical and Critical Thinking A Step 1 (Analyze), Step 3 (Research)
2 Creativity and Innovation C Step 4 (Explore Options)
3 Strategic Thinking S Step 1 (Analyze), Step 4 (Explore), Step 7 (Iterate)
4 Communication C Step 5 (Submit), Step 6 (Sustain)
5 Collaboration and Teamwork C Step 2 (Deliberate), Step 6 (Sustain)
6 Research and Analysis Ra Step 3 (Research)
7 Problem-Solving and Decision Making P Step 2 (Define), Step 4 (Explore)
8 Attention to Details and Quality A Step 5 (Submit)
9 Time and Attention Management T All steps (cross-cutting)
10 Flexibility and Adaptability F Step 7 (Iterate), Step 6 (Sustain)
11 Emotional Intelligence and Accountability E Step 2 (Deliberate), Step 6 (Sustain)
12 Ethics and Integrity E All steps (cross-cutting)

You do not need to memorize this table. But understand the principle: every competency connects to a specific part of your work process. If your supervisor identifies a weakness in your Step 3 (Research), the competency to develop is #6 (Research and Analysis). If your Step 5 outputs have frequent errors, focus on #8 (Attention to Details and Quality).

11.8 Self-Assessment: Where Do You Stand?

Before you apply for a government position — or during your first months on the job — use this self-assessment to identify your strengths and development areas.49

Instructions. Rate yourself honestly on each competency category. Use a 4-point scale:

  • 4 = Strong — I can demonstrate this consistently with evidence
  • 3 = Adequate — I can do this but need more practice
  • 2 = Developing — I have done this occasionally but it is not a strength
  • 1 = Needs Development — I have limited experience with this

Thinking Skills

Competency Rating (1-4) Evidence from your experience
Critical Thinking ___ Example: “Analyzed 3 policy options in my thesis and recommended one based on evidence”
Creative Thinking ___
Systems Thinking ___
Strategic Thinking ___

Communication Skills

Competency Rating (1-4) Evidence from your experience
Written Communication ___
Oral Communication ___

Collaboration Skills

Competency Rating (1-4) Evidence from your experience
Teamwork ___
Cross-Functional Coordination ___
Stakeholder Engagement ___

Values-Based Competencies

Competency Rating (1-4) Evidence from your experience
Integrity and Ethics ___
Professionalism ___
Results Orientation ___
Accountability ___

Bangsamoro Competencies

Competency Rating (1-4) Evidence from your experience
BOL Literacy ___
Cultural Competence ___
CSW / ADDRESS IT ___
Moral Governance Practice ___
Peace Process Awareness ___

Interpreting Your Results

Categories where you scored 3-4. These are your strengths. Highlight them in your cover letter and prepare STAR stories around them for interviews. You can confidently say: “I have demonstrated [this competency] through [specific experience].”

Categories where you scored 1-2. These are your development areas. They do not disqualify you — they guide your preparation:

Development area What to do BEFORE applying What to do ON the job
Thinking skills Practice case analysis; read policy documents; join a debate or critical thinking group Ask your supervisor for feedback on your analysis; study how senior colleagues structure their CSW
Communication Start writing — blog posts, reports, summaries. Practice presentations. Volunteer to draft memos and minutes; ask colleagues to review your writing; study Chapter 13
Collaboration Join group activities; volunteer for team-based projects; seek leadership roles in organizations Proactively offer help to colleagues; participate actively in meetings; join inter-office working groups
Values Reflect on ethical dilemmas you have faced; study the 12 Pillars of Moral Governance Observe how senior colleagues handle ethical situations; hold yourself accountable daily
Bangsamoro Read the BOL (RA 11054), the BDP 2023-2028, and key BAAs; study Bangsamoro history and culture Attend office orientation sessions; ask colleagues about cultural practices; learn basic greetings in local languages

Training Resources to Build Your Competencies

Resource What it offers Access
CSC Learning Hub Free online courses on government competencies, ethics, CSW csc.gov.ph
BARMM Training Programs Office-specific orientation, CSW training, technical courses Through your HR office after hiring
Online Courses (Coursera, edX) Policy analysis, public administration, data analysis, project management Free audit options available
Volunteer Work Real-world practice of collaboration, communication, stakeholder engagement Local CSOs, LGUs, faith-based organizations
BOL and BDP Study Self-study of key governance documents Available at BARMM Official Gazette and BPDA website

Key Takeaways

  • Competencies are observable behaviors, not self-declarations. You must be able to show, not just claim, your abilities.
  • Five categories matter in BARMM: thinking, communication, collaboration, values, and Bangsamoro-specific competencies.
  • Systems Thinking helps you see how your work connects to the larger government ecosystem. A budget memo is never just about numbers — it is about the services those numbers fund.
  • The Bangsamoro dimension is real. BOL literacy, cultural competence, moral governance practice, and peace process awareness are not theoretical concepts. They shape daily work in every BARMM office.
  • Functional competencies differ by track. Parliament staff (PAOs, LSOs, PIMAOs) need different specialized skills than ministry technical staff, agency planners, or LGU frontline workers.
  • Assessment is continuous. From hiring (CBI) through probation, annual evaluation (SPMS/IPCR), promotion, and training — your competencies are measured at every stage.
  • Start where you are. Use the self-assessment to identify gaps. Develop your weak areas before applying and continue developing on the job. No one starts as an expert. The best government workers are those who keep growing.

Your degree proved you can learn. Your competencies prove you can contribute.
