Back to Table of Contents
12
Working with Your Supervisor

Chapter 12: Working with Your Supervisor

Your supervisor’s job is to develop you. Your job is to make that possible.

This chapter is written from your perspective — the staff member. Your supervisor has their own guidebook (the Supervision Guidebook)1 that teaches them how to manage, coach, and evaluate you. This chapter teaches you what to expect from them, what they expect from you, and how to build the kind of working relationship that advances your career.

What Your Supervisor Expects From You

Before you worry about whether your supervisor is fair, competent, or easy to work with, understand what every supervisor expects from their staff. These expectations are universal — they apply whether you work in Parliament, a ministry, an office, or an LGU.

1. Come Prepared15

When your supervisor calls a meeting, a check-in, or a review — arrive ready. That means: - Know the status of your assignments - Bring your notes, your data, and your questions - Have a brief summary ready: “Here is where I am, here is what I need, here is what is blocking me.” - Do not wait for your supervisor to ask you what is happening. Tell them.

What this looks like in practice. Your supervisor schedules a 30-minute check-in for Monday morning. Before you walk in, review your pending assignments, note which ones are on track and which are behind, prepare your questions, and have a list of decisions you need from them. That 30-minute meeting will be productive. Without preparation, it becomes 30 minutes of your supervisor pulling information from you one question at a time.

2. Receive Feedback Without Defensiveness

Your supervisor will give you feedback — both positive and corrective. The corrective feedback is not a personal attack. It is professional development. Your ability to receive it gracefully is itself a competency (Emotional Intelligence and Accountability — ACSCCRaPATFEE #11).2

The SBI model from your end. Your supervisor may use the Situation-Behavior-Impact (SBI) model to give you feedback.3 Here is what it sounds like and how to respond:

Your supervisor says What they mean How to respond
“In yesterday’s committee hearing [Situation], you did not present the options analysis [Behavior], which meant the committee could not make a decision [Impact].” Your incomplete briefer caused a real problem. “Thank you for pointing that out. I should have included the options analysis. I will make sure the next briefer has the full analysis section before I submit it.”
“During last week’s staff meeting [S], you volunteered to help the Basilan team with their data [B], and that allowed them to meet their deadline [I].” Your proactive collaboration made a real difference. “Thank you. I noticed they were struggling and thought I could help. I am glad it worked out.”

What NOT to do when receiving feedback: - Do not argue or get defensive (“But that is not what I meant…”) - Do not blame others (“The data was not available because Finance did not send it…”) - Do not shut down or go silent - Do not dismiss it (“It was not a big deal anyway…”)

What TO do: - Listen fully before responding - Acknowledge the feedback (“I understand”) - Ask clarifying questions if needed (“Can you help me understand what the options analysis should have included?”) - Commit to specific improvement (“Next time, I will complete the options analysis and have you review it before the hearing”) - Follow through — and let your supervisor see the change

3. Take Ownership of Your Development16

Your supervisor will coach you, train you, and provide opportunities. But they cannot develop you against your will. You must be an active participant in your own growth.

What ownership looks like: - Asking your supervisor: “What should I be learning in my first 6 months?” - Requesting training opportunities before they are offered - Seeking feedback proactively: “How was my last memo? What can I improve?” - Studying on your own — reading the BOL, reviewing BAAs, learning the office’s systems - Watching how senior colleagues work and learning from their approach

What it does NOT look like: - Waiting to be told what to learn - Attending training only when forced - Never asking for feedback - Repeating the same mistakes because you never sought to understand why

4. Produce CSW That Does Not Need Rework17

This is the practical test. Your supervisor measures your competence primarily through your written output. If every memo you submit comes back with red marks, your supervisor’s confidence in you decreases. If your work is consistently clean, complete, and well-structured, their confidence grows — and so do your assignments.

The standard (from Chapter 10): Your supervisor should only need to approve or disapprove. If they have to rewrite your sentences, check your citations, fill in missing data, or fix your formatting — your staff work is not complete.

The Check-In Rhythm

Your supervisor should conduct regular check-ins — at least monthly, often more frequently during your first year. Check-ins are structured conversations, not casual chats. They follow a 4-step protocol.4

The 4-Step Check-In Protocol (From Your Perspective)

Step 1: Discuss Roles and Responsibilities

Your supervisor shares the office’s direction, clarifies expectations, and connects your work to the bigger picture. Your role: listen actively, ask questions, make sure you understand what is expected. If something is unclear, this is the time to clarify — not three weeks later when a deadline is missed.

What to ask: - “Has anything changed in our priorities since our last check-in?” - “Are there new assignments I should know about?” - “How does my current project connect to the office’s OPCR targets?”

Step 2: Review Goals and Assignments

You and your supervisor review your current workload against your goals (IPCR targets, assignment deadlines, project milestones). Your role: come prepared with a status update for every active assignment. Be honest about what is behind schedule and why.

What to bring: - A list of your active assignments with status (on track / behind / completed) - Specific questions about priorities (“I have three deadlines this week — which is most urgent?”) - Updates on completed work (“I submitted the feeding program memo last Tuesday — have you had a chance to review it?”)

Step 3: Evaluate Progress and Provide Coaching

Your supervisor assesses how you are doing — not just what you are producing, but how you are producing it. They may use the SBI model to give feedback (see above). They may also provide positive feedback to reinforce what you are doing well.

Your role: receive feedback openly. Ask follow-up questions. If your supervisor says your analysis was strong but your formatting needs work, write it down and fix it. If they praise your initiative, keep doing it.

What to ask: - “What is one thing I could do differently to improve my output?” - “Are there specific skills I should be developing right now?” - “Can you show me an example of what a strong [memo / briefer / report] looks like in our office?”

Step 4: Determine Next Steps

Agree on what you will do before the next check-in. Your supervisor may assign new tasks, set development goals, or ask you to follow up on specific items. Your role: write down the agreed actions. Do them. Report back at the next check-in.

What to confirm: - “So my priorities for the next two weeks are [X, Y, Z] — is that correct?” - “I will complete the policy recommendation by Friday and submit it through you for review.” - “Can we schedule the next check-in for [date]?”

How You Are Being Assessed: The PPR Framework

Your supervisor uses the Performance-Potential-Readiness (PPR) framework to assess where you stand.5 Understanding this framework helps you see your career from your supervisor’s perspective — and take action accordingly.

Step 1: Performance (Your Current Role)

Your supervisor evaluates how well you are performing right now — in the position you currently hold. Performance includes both results (what you achieve) and behaviors (how you achieve them).

  • Results: Are you meeting your IPCR targets? Are your CSW outputs complete and on time? Are your projects progressing?
  • Behaviors: Are you collaborative? Professional? Responsive to feedback? Reliable?

A staff member who produces excellent briefers but is consistently late, combative in meetings, and unwilling to help colleagues is not performing well — because behaviors matter as much as results.

Step 2: Potential (Your Future Capacity)

If your performance is solid, your supervisor assesses your potential — your capacity to grow into a larger role. Indicators of high potential include:

  • Ability to solve complex problems (not just routine ones)
  • Persistence through setbacks (you do not give up when things get hard)
  • Drive to take on challenging work (you volunteer for difficult assignments)
  • Improvement based on feedback (you do not make the same mistake twice)

Important: Potential is assessed only when you are meeting or exceeding your current role’s expectations.18 If your performance is low, your supervisor focuses on closing the performance gap first — not on your future potential.

Step 3: Readiness (For a New Assignment)

Readiness means you already have the skills, knowledge, and abilities for a specific new role or assignment. This is assessed when there is a concrete opportunity — a promotion, a lateral move, a stretch assignment, a project leadership role.

The 4-Quadrant Coaching Guide

Based on your PPR assessment, your supervisor places you in one of four quadrants.6 Each quadrant has a different coaching focus:

Quadrant Performance Potential What your supervisor will do What you should do
Low-Low Low Low CHANGE — Revisit goals and expectations, emphasize ownership, coach for behavior change Be honest about your challenges. Ask for clear expectations. Accept coaching without defensiveness. Work hard to improve results.
Low-High Low High CHANGE — Clarify goals, show how your behavior affects outcomes, schedule frequent check-ins You have potential, but you are not delivering yet. Focus on results. Show your supervisor that their investment in you is paying off.
High-Low High Low DEVELOP — Explore your interests and aspirations, identify development opportunities, keep you engaged You are doing well now but may plateau. Ask about stretch assignments. Express interest in growth areas. Volunteer for cross-functional projects.
High-High High High SUSTAIN, REINFORCE, RECOGNIZE — Help you become a mentor, consider stretch assignments, prepare you for promotion Keep performing. Mentor junior colleagues. Take on leadership opportunities. Make your case for promotion through consistent excellence.

Where do you want to be? The High-High quadrant. You get there by delivering results, behaving professionally, learning continuously, and demonstrating potential through initiative.

Where most fresh graduates start. Realistically, most new staff begin in the Low-High quadrant19 — you have potential (your education, your motivation), but you have not yet demonstrated results in government. Your supervisor knows this. Your job in the first 6-12 months is to move from Low-High to High-High by converting your potential into performance.

How to Receive Feedback

Feedback is your fastest path to improvement. Yet most people resist it. Understanding how feedback works in your office will help you respond constructively.

The SBI Model from the Receiving End

Your supervisor structures feedback using Situation, Behavior, Impact:7

Corrective feedback example:

“In the committee hearing last Thursday [Situation], you read directly from your briefer instead of presenting the key points conversationally [Behavior]. The MPs lost interest after 5 minutes and started checking their phones [Impact].”

Your response: 1. Acknowledge. “Thank you for telling me. I did not realize I was reading verbatim.” 2. Understand. “Should I prepare bullet-point talking points instead of reading the full text?” 3. Commit. “For the next hearing, I will practice presenting from notes rather than the full document.” 4. Follow through. At the next hearing, present from talking points. Your supervisor notices.

Positive feedback example:

“In the inter-agency coordination meeting yesterday [S], you volunteered to draft the joint action plan and completed it within 24 hours [B]. That allowed us to submit the plan to the Minister on time and positioned our office as the lead coordinator [I].”

Your response: 1. Accept graciously. “Thank you. I saw the urgency and wanted to help us meet the deadline.” 2. Learn from it. This tells you that initiative, speed, and quality are valued. Keep doing it.

Five Feedback Traps to Avoid20

Trap What it sounds like Why it hurts you
Defensiveness “But I did include the data — it was on page 7” Your supervisor stops giving you feedback because it always becomes an argument
Deflection “The real problem was that Finance did not send the numbers on time” Your supervisor sees you as someone who does not take responsibility
Silence [Says nothing, looks at the floor] Your supervisor cannot tell if you understood or plan to change
Over-apologizing “I am so sorry, I am so sorry, I will never do it again” Emotional responses make supervisors uncomfortable giving future feedback
Immediate pushback “That is not fair — I worked on this all weekend” Effort is not the same as quality. Your supervisor is evaluating the output, not the hours

The right approach: Listen. Acknowledge. Ask for clarification. Commit to improvement. Demonstrate the change.

Your Performance Evaluation

Once a year, your supervisor conducts a formal performance evaluation. This follows the 6-step evaluation protocol.8 Understanding what your supervisor is doing at each step helps you prepare.

Step 1: Your Supervisor Gets Your Input

Your supervisor asks you to summarize your key accomplishments. Provide this in writing using the Situation-Action-Result format: - Situation: “The feeding program in Basilan was 62% behind target as of March.” - Action: “I conducted root cause analysis, proposed a 3-option corrective plan, and coordinated the TA deployment to 3 municipalities.” - Result: “The program reached 87% of target by year-end — a 25-percentage-point improvement.”

Important: Your supervisor will NOT ask you to rate yourself (self-ratings create defensiveness).21 They only want your accomplishments in narrative form.

Step 2: Your Supervisor Gets Input from Others

Your supervisor may ask colleagues, partners, or subordinates about your work. This is not about collecting gossip — it is a multi-source feedback process to get a complete picture.

What you can do: Build strong working relationships with colleagues so that when asked, they have positive and specific things to say about your collaboration.

Step 3: Your Supervisor Evaluates Results (the “What”)

Your supervisor assesses what you accomplished against your IPCR targets. They look at: - Context — what circumstances affected your work (budget delays, staffing gaps, emergencies) - Accomplishment — what you actually delivered - Impact — what difference your work made

Step 4: Your Supervisor Evaluates Behaviors (the “How”)

Results are not enough. Your supervisor evaluates how you achieved them. Did you collaborate or work in isolation? Did you meet deadlines or consistently miss them? Did you support team goals or only pursue your own assignments?

This is where the competencies from Chapter 11 matter. Your supervisor assesses you against the 5-10 most important competencies for your position.

Step 5: Your Supervisor Writes the Review

Your supervisor prepares a written evaluation with: - A rating for both results and behaviors (using the SPMS scale:9 Poor through Outstanding) - A narrative explaining why each rating was given - Development recommendations for the coming year

Step 6: Your Supervisor Discusses the Evaluation with You

This is a face-to-face conversation. Your supervisor: - Summarizes the check-in discussions throughout the year - Shares the rating and narrative - Asks for your thoughts - Focuses on development for the next year

The golden rule of evaluations: If check-ins were done well throughout the year, the evaluation should contain no surprises.22 Every piece of feedback in the evaluation should have been discussed at least once during a check-in.

How to Prepare for Your Evaluation

Preparation What to do
Track your work all year23 Keep a running list of completed assignments, achievements, and impact data. Do not rely on memory in December.
Save your outputs Keep copies of your best CSW products — briefers, memos, reports, project proposals. These are evidence.
Document BDP alignment For each major output, note which BDP goal or priority agenda item it supports.
Note your training Record every training, seminar, and workshop you attended, with dates and topics.
Prepare your accomplishments Write 3-5 accomplishments in Situation-Action-Result format before the evaluation meeting.
Identify your development needs Be honest about areas where you struggled and propose what training or support would help.

When Things Go Wrong

Not every supervisor-staff relationship works smoothly. Here is what to do when challenges arise.

Performance Improvement Plan (PIP)

If your supervisor determines that your performance is consistently below standard, they may place you on a Performance Improvement Plan (PIP).10 This is a formal, documented intervention — not a punishment.

What a PIP includes: - Specific performance gaps identified - Measurable improvement targets - Timeline for improvement (typically 30-90 days) - Support the supervisor will provide (coaching, training, resources) - Consequences if improvement is not achieved

What you should do if placed on a PIP: 1. Take it seriously. A PIP is a warning that your employment may be at risk. 2. Understand the gaps clearly. Ask your supervisor: “What specifically do I need to change?” 3. Follow the plan. Meet every milestone, attend every check-in, document your progress. 4. Ask for help. Use the coaching and training offered. Seek mentorship from senior colleagues. 5. Do not take it personally. A PIP means your supervisor still sees potential — otherwise they would move directly to disciplinary action.

Progressive Discipline

If performance does not improve or if you commit a workplace violation, the 2017 Rules on Administrative Cases in the Civil Service (2017 RACCS)11 governs the disciplinary process. The progression is:

Step Action Typical grounds
1 Written warning Minor violations (tardiness, dress code, minor discourtesy)
2 Reprimand Repeated minor violations or first moderate offense
3 Suspension (1-90 days) Serious violations (gross negligence, insubordination, unauthorized absence)
4 Dismissal Grave offenses (dishonesty, corruption, grave misconduct, conviction of a crime involving moral turpitude)

Your rights under due process: - You must be informed of the charges in writing - You have the right to answer and present evidence - You have the right to a formal hearing if the offense is grave - You can appeal to the CSC or the appropriate body - You cannot be dismissed without due process — even if you are guilty

When the Problem Is Your Supervisor

Sometimes the challenge is not your performance but the supervisory relationship. If your supervisor is: - Unclear about expectations — request a written set of priorities - Unavailable for check-ins — propose a schedule and follow up in writing - Giving inconsistent direction — document instructions and confirm in email - Hostile or abusive — document incidents, report to HR, and know your rights under the 2017 RACCS14 and RA 787712 (Anti-Sexual Harassment Act) or RA 1131313 (Safe Spaces Act)

The professional response: Address issues through proper channels. Document everything. Seek guidance from HR or a trusted senior colleague. Do not escalate on social media, gossip with colleagues, or retaliate. Your professionalism in difficult situations demonstrates the values-based competencies from Chapter 11.

Building a Productive Relationship

The best supervisor-staff relationships are built on mutual investment.24 Here is what that looks like:

Your supervisor’s investment in you Your investment in the relationship
Clarifies expectations regularly Delivers on those expectations
Gives honest feedback using SBI Receives feedback without defensiveness
Creates development opportunities Takes ownership of your development
Advocates for your promotion when ready Produces work that justifies advocacy
Protects you from unfair demands Protects your supervisor from surprises
Coaches you through mistakes Learns from mistakes and does not repeat them

The last point deserves emphasis. “Protects your supervisor from surprises” means: if something goes wrong with your assignment, tell your supervisor before they hear about it from someone else. If a deadline will be missed, flag it early. If a stakeholder complained, report it immediately. Supervisors can manage problems. They cannot manage problems they do not know about.

Key Takeaways

  • Come prepared to every check-in. Your supervisor’s time is limited. Make meetings productive by bringing status updates, questions, and proposed next steps.
  • Receive feedback as a gift, not an attack. The SBI model (Situation, Behavior, Impact) structures feedback so it is specific and actionable. Your job is to listen, acknowledge, and improve.
  • Understand how you are being assessed. The PPR framework (Performance, Potential, Readiness) places you in one of four quadrants. Your goal is to move to High-High through consistent results and professional behaviors.
  • The 4-step check-in protocol structures your monthly conversations: roles and responsibilities, goals and assignments, progress and coaching, next steps.
  • The 6-step evaluation protocol is your annual formal review. Prepare by tracking accomplishments all year using Situation-Action-Result format.
  • No surprises. If check-ins are done well, nothing in your evaluation should be unexpected. If something surprises you, the check-in process broke down — raise this with your supervisor.
  • When things go wrong, respond professionally. A PIP is a chance to improve, not a death sentence. Due process protects you. And if the problem is your supervisor, address it through proper channels with documentation.
  • Your supervisor cannot develop you against your will.25 Take ownership. Ask for feedback. Seek training. Study on your own. The staff members who grow fastest are those who pursue development, not wait for it.

13
Government Communication: Writing That Gets Read

Chapter 13: Government Communication: Writing That Gets Read

In government, writing IS working. If you cannot write a proper memo, you cannot do the job.

Your supervisor will judge your competence primarily through your written output. A staff member who writes well is trusted with bigger assignments. A staff member who writes poorly will spend their career doing data entry. This chapter teaches you the formats, conventions, and habits that make government writing effective.

13.1 The Memorandum: The Workhorse of Government

The memorandum (memo) is the most common document you will produce.1 Every request, report, recommendation, and notification moves through memos. You may write dozens in a single week. Learn this format first.

Standard Memo Structure

REPUBLIC OF THE PHILIPPINES
BANGSAMORO AUTONOMOUS REGION IN MUSLIM MINDANAO
[MINISTRY/OFFICE NAME]
[Address]

MEMORANDUM

TO      : [Full name and title of recipient]
FROM    : [Full name and title of sender]
DATE    : [Date of memo]
SUBJECT : [Clear, specific subject]

---

1. [Purpose paragraph — why you are writing. State the action needed upfront.]

2. [Background paragraph — context the reader needs.]

3. [Details paragraph — specifics, data, findings, attachments referenced.]

4. [Action paragraph — what you need the reader to do, by when.]

[Signature block]
[Name]
[Position]

Annotated Example

Here is a real-world example with annotations:

REPUBLIC OF THE PHILIPPINES
BANGSAMORO AUTONOMOUS REGION IN MUSLIM MINDANAO
MINISTRY OF SOCIAL SERVICES AND DEVELOPMENT
Cotabato City

MEMORANDUM

TO      : HON. RAISSA H. JAJURIE
           Minister, MSSD
FROM    : AHMAD M. SANTOS
           Administrative Officer III, Social Protection Division
DATE    : 26 March 2026
SUBJECT : Request for Approval of BANGON Beneficiary List for
           Municipality of Paglat, Maguindanao del Sur

---

1. This is to request your approval of the attached BANGON beneficiary
   list for the Municipality of Paglat, Maguindanao del Sur, covering
   45 qualified beneficiaries for FY 2026 Batch 1.

2. Per MSSD Memorandum Order No. 2026-003, all BANGON beneficiary lists
   must be validated by the Municipal Social Welfare Office, endorsed
   by the Municipal Mayor, and approved by the Minister before
   disbursement.

3. The attached list has been validated against the following criteria:
   (a) residency in conflict-affected barangay, (b) income below
   poverty threshold, (c) no duplication with other livelihood programs.
   Supporting documents are attached as Annexes A-C.

4. In view of the above, approval of the attached beneficiary list is
   respectfully requested to enable disbursement before the end of
   Q1 FY 2026.

[Signature]
AHMAD M. SANTOS
Administrative Officer III
Social Protection Division

Key Rules for Fresh Graduates14

The SUBJECT line is the most important line. Your supervisor may receive 20 or more memos per day. A vague subject line (“Request”) gets buried. A specific one (“Request for Approval of BANGON Beneficiary List for Municipality of Paglat, Maguindanao del Sur”) gets read and acted on.

One memo = one subject.6 Do not combine unrelated requests in a single memo. If you need to request budget approval AND report on a field visit, write two separate memos.

Number your paragraphs.7 Government readers scan — they do not read every word. Numbered paragraphs allow them to reference specific points: “See paragraph 3 of your memo dated 26 March 2026.” This also helps during discussions when your supervisor says, “I have a question about your third point.”

State the action requested in BOTH the first paragraph AND the last paragraph. Decision-makers often read only the beginning and end. Your purpose should be clear whether they read the full memo or skim it.

Attach supporting documents rather than embedding lengthy data in the body. A memo with 10 pages of tables in the body is hard to read. A memo with 4 short paragraphs and 10 pages of tables in an annex is easy to read.

13.2 The Transmittal Memorandum

A transmittal memo accompanies a CSW output2 — a briefer, policy recommendation, project proposal, or report — as it moves from your desk to the decision-maker. It is NOT the CSW itself. It is the cover page that says: “Here is the work. Here is what I need you to do with it.”

Transmittal Memo Structure

MEMORANDUM

TO      : [Decision-maker]
FROM    : [You]
DATE    : [Date]
SUBJECT : Transmittal of [Name of Document] re: [Topic]

---

1. Transmitted herewith is the [Legislative Briefer / Policy Recommendation /
   Project Proposal / Budget Analysis] on [topic], prepared pursuant to
   [assignment source — e.g., "your directive during the March 15, 2026
   staff meeting" or "Committee Resolution No. 2026-001"].

2. [1-2 sentence summary of the key finding or recommendation.]

3. [List of attachments and how they are organized.]

4. For your [approval / information / appropriate action].

[Signature block]

Annotated Example

MEMORANDUM

TO      : HON. MEMBER OF PARLIAMENT [Name]
           Chair, Committee on Agriculture, Fisheries, and Agrarian Reform
FROM    : [Your name]
           Legislative Staff Officer II
DATE    : 26 March 2026
SUBJECT : Transmittal of Legislative Briefer re: Proposed Bangsamoro
           Fisheries Code (BTA Bill No. 2026-0045)

---

1. Transmitted herewith is the Legislative Briefer on BTA Bill No. 2026-0045,
   "An Act Establishing the Bangsamoro Fisheries Code," prepared pursuant to
   your directive during the March 20, 2026 committee staff meeting.

2. The briefer recommends approval of the proposed bill with three amendments
   to align Section 12 with BOL Article V, Section 2, Item 14 (fisheries
   competence) and to harmonize penalty provisions with the national
   Fisheries Code (RA 8550 as amended by RA 10654).

3. The following documents are attached:
   (a) Legislative Briefer (10 pages)
   (b) Bill Comparison Matrix — Proposed vs. National Fisheries Code
   (c) Stakeholder Position Summary

4. For your review and appropriate action in preparation for the committee
   hearing scheduled on April 3, 2026.

[Signature]
[Name]
Legislative Staff Officer II

When to Use a Transmittal Memo vs. a Regular Memo15

Use a regular memo when… Use a transmittal memo when…
Your memo IS the document (a request, report, or notification) Your memo ACCOMPANIES a more substantive document
The body of the memo contains all the information the reader needs The attachments are more important than the memo itself
No attachments, or attachments are supporting documents The main deliverable is the attachment

Rule of thumb: If your memo has attachments that are more important than the memo itself, it is a transmittal.

13.3 The Endorsement Chain

Endorsements are how documents move between offices.3 When Office A sends a document to Office B for action, and Office B forwards it to Office C, each forwarding is an endorsement. Understanding endorsements is essential because you will both write them and receive them.

How It Works

ORIGINAL MEMO: Municipality of Paglat --> MILG
(Requesting technical assistance for CDP update)

1st ENDORSEMENT: MILG --> BPDA
"Respectfully endorsed to the Bangsamoro Planning and Development
Authority for appropriate action. The Municipality of Paglat requests
technical assistance in preparing its Comprehensive Development Plan
update."
[Signed by MILG Director]

2nd ENDORSEMENT: BPDA Executive Director --> Planning Division
"Referred to the Planning Division for evaluation and recommendation."
[Signed by BPDA Executive Director]

3rd ENDORSEMENT: Planning Division --> BPDA Executive Director
"Respectfully returned with the recommendation that BPDA deploy a
planning team to Paglat municipality for a 5-day CDP workshop, with
an estimated cost of PHP 85,000 chargeable to the FY 2026 Technical
Assistance Fund."
[Signed by Division Chief]

Each endorsement adds a layer to the document’s history.16 By the time you receive a document with three endorsements, you can trace its entire journey — who originated it, who reviewed it, what they recommended, and what is expected of you.

Key Endorsement Phrases and What They Mean

These phrases are standard across Philippine government.4 Each one signals a specific action expectation:

Phrase What it means Who uses it
“Respectfully endorsed for appropriate action” “Please handle this — you have authority to decide” Superior forwarding to subordinate office
“Respectfully endorsed for information” “FYI only — no action needed from you” Lateral or upward endorsement
“Respectfully endorsed for approval” “I have reviewed this and recommend approval — please sign” Subordinate forwarding to superior
“Respectfully endorsed for comment” “Please review and give your input” Any direction
“Respectfully returned with recommendation” “I have done my review — here are my findings” Subordinate returning to superior

Fresh graduate tip: When you receive a document with endorsements, read all the endorsements. They tell you the document’s journey — who sent it, who reviewed it, what they said, and what is expected of you. Many new staff skip the endorsements and miss critical context.

13.4 Email in Government

Government email is formal correspondence — not casual communication.17 Every email you send is potentially a public record. It can be subject to Freedom of Information (FOI) requests.5 It can be subpoenaed in administrative or legal proceedings. Write every email as if it will be read by someone other than the recipient.

Email Template 1: Request Email

Subject: Request for FY 2026 BANGON Disbursement Data -
         Paglat, Maguindanao del Sur

Dear [Name / Sir / Ma'am],

Assalamu Alaikum.

I am writing to request the FY 2026 disbursement data for the BANGON
program in the Municipality of Paglat, Maguindanao del Sur. This data
is needed for the mid-year OPCR review of the Social Protection
Division, due on July 15, 2026.

Specifically, we need:
1. Total amount disbursed (January - June 2026)
2. Number of beneficiaries served per barangay
3. Liquidation status per tranche

May we request this data by [date — give at least 5 working days]?

Thank you and Wassalam.

[Your name]
[Your position]
[Your office]
[Contact number]

Email Template 2: Follow-Up Email

Subject: Follow-Up: Request for FY 2026 BANGON Disbursement Data
         (sent [original date])

Dear [Name],

Assalamu Alaikum.

I am following up on my email dated [date] requesting the FY 2026
BANGON disbursement data for Paglat, Maguindanao del Sur. We have
not yet received the requested information.

As the mid-year OPCR review deadline is July 15, 2026, may we kindly
request the data by [new deadline — give 3 working days]?

Please let me know if there are any issues with the request. I am
happy to coordinate with your staff directly if that would help.

Thank you and Wassalam.

[Signature block]

Email Template 3: Routing/Transmittal Email

Subject: FOR APPROPRIATE ACTION: Referral of Constituent Complaint -
         Water System, Brgy. Kalanganan, Marawi City

Dear [Recipient],

Assalamu Alaikum.

Please find attached the constituent complaint filed by [name],
Brgy. Captain of Kalanganan, Marawi City, regarding the non-functional
Level II water system in Sitio [name].

This was referred to our office by [source] on [date]. Per our office
mandate, this falls under your division's responsibility.

Recommended action: Site inspection and status assessment within
15 working days.

Please advise on next steps.

Thank you and Wassalam.

[Signature block]

Email Rules in Government

CC (Carbon Copy).18 Copy those who need to know but do not need to act. Always CC your immediate supervisor on substantive emails. This prevents surprises and keeps your supervisor informed.

BCC (Blind Carbon Copy). Use sparingly. Appropriate only when sending to a large distribution list where recipients do not need to see each other’s addresses.

Key response words and what they mean:

Word Meaning Action required
“Noted” “I have read and acknowledged” NO — it does NOT mean “approved”
“Approved” “I agree — this is an official action” YES — proceed with implementation
“For compliance” “You MUST do what this email says” YES — it is a directive

Response time. Acknowledge within 24 hours, even if you cannot fully respond yet. A quick reply — “Received. I will provide the requested data by [date]” — is professional. Silence is not.

Never use: - Informal language or text speak (“pls,” “thx,” “ASAP” is acceptable but borderline) - All caps (reads as shouting) - Unprofessional tone (“I already sent this three times…”)

13.5 Meeting Minutes

Every government meeting should produce minutes.19 As a new staff member, you will likely be assigned to take minutes. This is considered junior work — and it is also the best way to learn what is happening in your office. Embrace it.

Meeting Minutes Template

MINUTES OF THE MEETING

Meeting Title : [e.g., "Weekly Staff Meeting, Social Protection Division"]
Date and Time : [e.g., "26 March 2026, 9:00 AM - 10:30 AM"]
Venue         : [e.g., "MSSD Conference Room B, Cotabato City"
                 or "Via Google Meet"]
Presiding Officer : [e.g., "Division Chief Ahmad M. Santos"]

ATTENDEES:
1. [Name] — [Position]
2. [Name] — [Position]
3. [Name] — [Position]

ABSENT:
1. [Name] — [Reason if known]

---

AGENDA AND PROCEEDINGS:

I. Call to Order
   [Time called to order, by whom]

II. Approval of Previous Minutes
    [Status: approved / approved with corrections — specify corrections]

III. Matters Arising from Previous Minutes
     [Follow-up items from last meeting and their current status]

IV. [Agenda Item 1 — use a specific title, not "Item 1"]
    [Summary of discussion — key points, not verbatim]
    [Decision reached or action item assigned]

V. [Agenda Item 2]
   [Same format]

VI. Other Matters
    [Any items raised not on the original agenda]

VII. Schedule of Next Meeting
     [Date, time, venue]

---

ACTION ITEMS:

| # | Action Item | Person Responsible | Deadline | Status |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | [Specific action] | [Name] | [Date] | Pending |
| 2 | [Specific action] | [Name] | [Date] | Pending |
| 3 | [Specific action] | [Name] | [Date] | Pending |

Prepared by  : [Your name, position]
Noted by     : [Presiding officer, position]
Date prepared: [Date]

Meeting Minutes Rules20

Minutes are a RECORD, not a transcript. Capture decisions and action items, not every word spoken. If a 30-minute discussion ends with a decision to deploy a team to Basilan, write the decision — not the 30 minutes of debate.

Distribute within 48 hours. Minutes lose value quickly. If people forget what was discussed, the minutes arrived too late.

The action items table is the most important part. Many readers skip the proceedings and go straight to the action items. Every action item must have a specific responsible person and a specific deadline — not “the team” and not “soon.”

Get the presiding officer to note/approve before distributing. This ensures accuracy and gives the minutes official status.

Keep a running file. Maintain a folder (physical or digital) of all meeting minutes in chronological order. You will need to reference previous minutes regularly — “Per the March 12 staff meeting minutes, Item IV…”

13.6 Official Letters

Letters are for external correspondence21 — communicating with other government agencies, LGUs, development partners, CSOs, or the public. They are more formal than memos and follow a different format.

Standard Letter Format

[Date]

[Recipient's full name and title]
[Position]
[Office/Organization]
[Address]

Dear [Title and Last Name / Sir / Ma'am]:

Assalamu Alaikum.

[Body — same structure as memo: purpose, background, details, action]

[Complimentary close]

Respectfully,

[Signature]
[Name]
[Position]
[Office]

Key Differences Between Memos and Letters

Feature Memo Letter
Audience Internal (within your office or MOA) External (other agencies, LGUs, partners, public)
Header TO / FROM / DATE / SUBJECT Full address block with recipient details
Salutation None (the TO line serves this purpose) “Dear [Title and Name]:”
Tone Direct and functional Formal and courteous
Closing Signature block only “Respectfully,” or “Very truly yours,” + signature
When to use Routine internal communication When communicating outside your immediate organization

13.7 Action Phrases: The Five Phrases That Control What Happens

These five phrases determine what the recipient does with your document. Using the wrong one creates confusion, delays, or inaction. Master them.

Action Phrase What it means When to use it Example
“For information”8 Read and note — no response or action needed Sharing data, reports, or updates the recipient should know about but does not need to act on “The attached FY 2025 Year-End Report is forwarded for your information.”
“For appropriate action” The recipient should decide what to do and act — you are NOT prescribing the action Routing a complaint, referral, or request to the office responsible for handling it “The attached constituent complaint is endorsed for your appropriate action.”
“For approval” You have done the work, you are recommending an action, and the decision-maker needs to sign off Submitting a completed CSW (briefer, proposal, budget, beneficiary list) to a superior “The attached project proposal is submitted for your approval.”
“For comment” You want the recipient’s input before finalizing Circulating a draft document for review “The attached draft Administrative Order is forwarded for your comment.”
“For compliance” This is a directive — the recipient MUST do what is stated Issuing instructions, citing regulations, or following up on unmet requirements “Per CSC MC No. 14, s. 2018,9 compliance with posting requirements is directed.”

Additional Routing Phrases22

Phrase What it means
“Respectfully submitted” This is my original work — I created this document
“Respectfully transmitted” I am forwarding someone else’s document
“Respectfully endorsed” I am routing this to you with my recommendation
“Respectfully returned” I am sending this back to you with my findings

Common mistake:10 Using “submitted” and “transmitted” interchangeably. They mean different things. Use “submitted” for work you produced. Use “transmitted” for work someone else produced that you are forwarding.

13.8 Government Writing Voice: Rules for Fresh Graduates

Government writing is NOT academic writing.11 The transition from university to government requires you to unlearn several habits.23

Academic vs. Government Writing

Academic writing Government writing
Long sentences, complex syntax Short sentences, simple structure
Passive voice (“It was determined that…”) Active voice (“The committee determined…”)
Hedging (“It appears that there may be…”) Direct (“There is a shortage of…”)
Citations in footnotes Citations inline or in attachments
Builds to a conclusion at the end States the conclusion FIRST, then supports it
Demonstrates the writer’s knowledge Enables the reader’s decision

The Golden Rule of Government Writing

Lead with the action. Your reader is busy. They receive dozens of documents daily. Tell them what you need in the first paragraph — not the fourth.

Before (academic habit) After (government standard)
“In light of the recent developments in the implementation of the BANGON program, and considering the various challenges encountered by the implementing municipalities, particularly in terms of fund utilization and beneficiary validation, this memorandum seeks to…” “This memorandum requests approval of the attached BANGON beneficiary list for Paglat, Maguindanao del Sur (45 beneficiaries, FY 2026 Batch 1).”

The “before” version takes 40 words to say nothing. The “after” version takes 25 words to say everything the reader needs.

Common Government Writing Mistakes

Mistake Example Correction
Using “the undersigned” “The undersigned respectfully requests…” “I respectfully request…” (the CSC has explicitly discouraged “the undersigned”)12
Confusing “submitted” and “transmitted” Using them interchangeably “Submitted” = your original work. “Transmitted” = forwarding someone else’s document.
Using “thru” “Thru the Secretary General” “Through the Secretary General” (“thru” is informal)
Unnecessary preambles “In connection with the above-captioned subject matter and pursuant to…” State the purpose directly in the first sentence
Acronyms without definition “The HRMPSB reviewed the QS per CSC MC 14”13 Spell out on first use: “The Human Resource Merit Promotion and Selection Board (HRMPSB)…”
Run-on paragraphs One paragraph covering 3 different topics One paragraph per point. Number them.
Passive voice “It is recommended that the proposal be approved” “I recommend approving the proposal”
Burying the conclusion Main recommendation appears in paragraph 6 Move the recommendation to paragraph 1

Voice Principles for BARMM Writing24

Be direct. Government readers want answers, not suspense.

Be specific. “Several municipalities” is vague. “Three municipalities in Basilan (Lamitan City, Tipo-Tipo, and Ungkaya Pukan)” is useful.

Be concise. If you can say it in 15 words, do not use 40.

Be structured. Number your paragraphs. Use headings in long documents. Include tables for comparative data. Bold key terms.

Be courteous but not flowery. “Respectfully” is appropriate. “I humbly beg your most esteemed consideration of this insignificant submission” is not.

13.9 Government Writing Quick Reference Card

Keep this at your desk until these conventions become habit.25

Side 1: Document Types Quick Reference

Document Use when Key element
Memo Internal communication Subject line + action requested
Transmittal Forwarding a CSW output “Transmitted herewith…” + attachment list
Endorsement Routing between offices “Respectfully endorsed…” + action phrase
Letter External correspondence Formal salutation and complimentary close
Minutes Recording meeting proceedings Action items table with persons and deadlines
Email Fast internal/external communication Specific subject line + clear ask + professional tone

Side 2: Action Phrases Cheat Sheet

Write this When you mean
“For your approval” “Please sign this — I have done the work”
“For your information” “FYI — no action needed”
“For appropriate action” “Please handle this — it is your responsibility”
“For your comment” “Please review and give feedback”
“For compliance” “You must do this — it is a requirement”
“Respectfully submitted” “This is my original work”
“Respectfully transmitted” “I am forwarding someone else’s work”
“Respectfully endorsed” “I am routing this to you with my recommendation”
“Respectfully returned” “I am sending this back with my findings”

Key Takeaways

  • The memo is your workhorse. Learn the format cold: TO, FROM, DATE, SUBJECT, numbered paragraphs, action requested in paragraphs 1 and 4.
  • The transmittal memo is your CSW’s cover page. It accompanies your substantive work and tells the decision-maker what to do with it.
  • Endorsements trace a document’s journey. Read all endorsements to understand context. Write endorsements with the correct action phrase.
  • Government email is formal correspondence. CC your supervisor. Acknowledge within 24 hours. Write as if it will be FOI’d.
  • Meeting minutes are your learning tool. Capture decisions and action items, not verbatim transcripts. Distribute within 48 hours.
  • Five action phrases control everything. “For information,” “for appropriate action,” “for approval,” “for comment,” and “for compliance” each trigger different responses. Use the right one.
  • Government writing leads with the action. State what you need in paragraph 1. Support it in paragraphs 2-3. Restate it in your closing paragraph.
  • Unlearn academic habits. Short sentences. Active voice. Specific numbers. No hedging. No preambles. The reader needs a decision, not a literature review.

Your writing is your professional identity.26 In a government where most work is documented in writing, the quality of your memos, briefers, and reports is the quality of your work. Write well, and your career advances. Write poorly, and it stalls. Start practicing now.

14
The Bangsamoro Government Employee Journey

Chapter 14: The Bangsamoro Government Employee Journey

Your career is a journey with defined stages. Know what is coming at each one.

You have applied, interviewed, and received your appointment letter. Now what?

This chapter maps the complete employee lifecycle from application to retirement, based on BAA 17 (Bangsamoro Civil Service Code).1 Think of it as a roadmap – you are at the starting point, and this chapter shows you every stage ahead so you can prepare, not just react.

Every Bangsamoro government employee – whether you work in the Parliament, a ministry, an office, or an LGU – follows this journey. The milestones are the same. The timeline varies based on your performance, the availability of vacancies, and the choices you make along the way.

14.1 The Journey Map

Here is your career from beginning to end, in one view:

APPLY --> HIRE --> ONBOARD --> PROBATION --> PERFORM --> DEVELOP --> PROMOTE --> LEAD --> TRANSITION
Stage Duration What Happens Key Milestone
Apply 1-3 months Job search, PDS submission, interview, Comparative Assessment Appointment letter
Onboard Days 1-30 Oath of office, ID processing, orientation, office tour, first assignments Completed orientation checklist
Probation Months 1-6 Learning the job, first CSW assignments, supervisor check-ins Probationary evaluation PASSED
Perform Years 1-3 IPCR targets, regular CSW output, SPMS ratings, training First “Very Satisfactory” or higher rating
Develop Years 2-5 Functional training, CSW mastery, lateral assignments, graduate studies Competency proficiency across core areas
Promote Year 3+ Next-in-rank competition, competitive selection, salary grade advancement First promotion
Lead Year 5+ Supervisory role, team management, system design, mentoring First supervisory appointment
Transition Varies Transfer, secondment, retirement, resignation, or new career Graceful exit with knowledge transfer

Not everyone follows this timeline exactly. Some people reach the “Lead” stage in 5 years. Some take 15. The stages are the same – the speed depends on you, on vacancies, and on opportunity.

What matters is that you know where you are at every stage, and you know what comes next.

14.2 Onboarding: Your First 30 Days

Your first 30 days set the tone for your entire career. This is when your supervisor, your colleagues, and your office leadership form their first impression of you. Make it count.

Day 1: The Administrative Foundation

Your first day is mostly paperwork – but every step matters.

  • Report to HR. You will process your appointment documents, take your oath of office, and receive your government ID. Bring all original documents (diploma, TOR, CSC eligibility certificate, birth certificate, NBI clearance). HR will need certified true copies.
  • Oath of office. This is administered by the appointing authority or an authorized officer. It is a brief ceremony, but it is legally significant. From the moment you take the oath, you are bound by RA 6713 (Code of Conduct),2 BAA 17 (Bangsamoro Civil Service Code),3 and all applicable laws governing public officials and employees.
  • Government ID and system access. You will be enrolled in biometric attendance (or receive a Daily Time Record form), issued your government email, and given access to office systems. Some offices use Google Workspace. Others use Microsoft. Learn whichever your office uses.
  • Benefits enrollment. HR will process your enrollment in GSIS (Government Service Insurance System),4 PhilHealth,5 and Pag-IBIG Fund.6 These are mandatory deductions from your salary – but they provide retirement benefits, health insurance, and housing loan access.

Days 1-3: Know Your Office

  • Meet your immediate supervisor. This is the most important relationship in your first year. Ask about their expectations, communication preferences, and how they want you to report progress. Some supervisors want daily email updates. Others prefer weekly face-to-face check-ins. Ask – do not guess.
  • Receive your position description. This document defines your official duties and responsibilities. Read it carefully. If anything is unclear, ask your supervisor to explain. Your IPCR (Individual Performance Commitment and Review) will be based on this.7
  • Get your office tour. Learn where the conference room is, where the prayer room is, where the filing room is, where the comfort room is. Small details, but they matter when you need to find a document at 4:30 PM.
  • Meet your team. Introduce yourself to every colleague in your division. Learn their names, their positions, and what they do. In Bangsamoro culture, relationships are foundational – take time to build them.

Week 1: Understand the System

  • Read the Manual of Procedures (MOP) – if your office has one. Not all offices do. If there is no MOP, ask your supervisor how key processes work: How does a memo get approved? How are documents filed? What is the routing for outgoing correspondence?
  • Study the organizational chart. Know where your division sits in the office structure. Know who your Division Chief is, who the Director is, who the Minister or head of office is. Understand the chain of command.
  • Learn the filing system. Every office has one – some digital, some paper-based, some both. Learn it immediately. You will be filing and retrieving documents from Day 1.
  • Attend your first staff meeting. Listen more than you speak. Take notes. Observe how people interact, who leads the discussion, and what the current priorities are. You will learn more about your office from one staff meeting than from reading 10 documents.

Week 1-2: Your First Supervisor Check-In

Your supervisor will schedule a check-in – or you should request one. This is your opportunity to:

  • Clarify expectations. What does your supervisor expect you to produce in your first month? What does “good work” look like in this office?
  • Receive your first assignments. These will likely be small: drafting a memo, organizing a file, preparing data for a report. Treat them as seriously as you would a major project. Your supervisor is testing your reliability and attention to detail.
  • Ask about communication preferences. How should you submit work – email, printed copy, shared drive? When should you escalate problems versus solving them yourself? How does your supervisor prefer to be addressed?

Week 2-4: Your First CSW Assignment

Within your first month, you should complete at least one substantive Complete Staff Work assignment using the ADDRESS IT methodology (see Chapter 10). This is your proof of concept – your demonstration that you can produce decision-ready work.

Your supervisor will evaluate not just the content but the process:

  • Did you understand the assignment correctly? (Step 1: Analyze Context)
  • Did you research adequately? (Step 3: Research)
  • Did you present options, not just information? (Step 4: Explore)
  • Is the output complete – no loose ends, no missing attachments? (Step 5: Submit)

By Day 30: Your Checklist

By the end of your first month, you should be able to answer “yes” to all of these:

  • ☐ I know my office’s mandate under BAA 138
  • ☐ I have read my position description and understand my duties
  • ☐ I know my supervisor’s expectations and communication preferences
  • ☐ I know the approval workflow for documents in my office
  • ☐ I have submitted at least one completed staff work assignment
  • ☐ I am enrolled in GSIS, PhilHealth, and Pag-IBIG
  • ☐ I have my government ID and system access
  • ☐ I know my colleagues’ names and what they do
  • ☐ I know where the MOP is (or I know there is none)
  • ☐ I have attended at least one staff meeting

If you cannot check all of these, you are behind. Ask your supervisor for help catching up.

14.3 Probation: Months 1-6

The first six months of your appointment are a probationary period. This is when your supervisor formally evaluates whether you can do the job.

What Is Being Evaluated

Your probationary evaluation measures two things:

  1. Results – Are you completing assignments on time? Is your work accurate? Does your CSW meet the standard? Are you meeting the targets in your position description?
  2. Behaviors – Are you reliable? Do you follow the chain of command? Do you accept feedback constructively? Do you work well with your team? Do you observe the 12 Pillars of Moral Governance9 in your daily conduct?

The Probationary Evaluation

At the end of your probationary period, your supervisor submits a formal evaluation to HR. The evaluation states whether you have performed satisfactorily or unsatisfactorily.

  • Satisfactory: You pass probation. Your appointment is confirmed. You are now a regular employee with full security of tenure (if you hold a permanent appointment).
  • Unsatisfactory: Your appointment may be terminated. BAA 1710 and CSC rules allow for termination of probationary employees who fail to meet performance standards. This is rare – but it happens.

How to Succeed During Probation

Ask questions early. In your first month, asking questions shows initiative. By month three, not knowing basic processes signals that you are not learning. Front-load your questions.

Seek feedback proactively. Do not wait for your supervisor to tell you what you did wrong. After completing an assignment, ask: “Is this the level of detail you expect?” or “How can I improve this for next time?”

Document your work. Keep a log of every assignment you complete, every training you attend, and every positive feedback you receive. This becomes evidence during your evaluation.

Produce quality CSW from Day 1. Your supervisor is looking for the answer to one question: “Can this person produce work that I do not need to redo?” Every memo, every spreadsheet, every briefer you submit is your answer.

Be on time. Every day. Attendance matters. Chronic tardiness during probation is one of the most common reasons for a negative evaluation. Government tracks attendance through biometrics or DTR – there is no hiding it.

The Most Common Reasons Probation Fails

Reason What It Looks Like How to Avoid It
Poor attendance Frequent absences, chronic tardiness Set an alarm. Arrive 15 minutes early. File leave properly.
Incomplete work Memos with missing attachments, data with errors Use a checklist before submitting anything. Double-check numbers.
Inability to follow instructions Producing different output than what was assigned Repeat back the assignment in your own words before starting.
Poor attitude toward feedback Becoming defensive or argumentative when corrected Thank your supervisor for feedback. Fix the issue. Move on.
Not learning office systems Still not knowing the filing system in month 3 Take notes. Create your own reference sheet. Ask colleagues.

14.4 Civil Service Systems You Must Know

Every BARMM civil servant interacts with these systems. Learn them in your first month – they will govern your career for as long as you serve.

SPMS: Strategic Performance Management System

The SPMS is the official CSC performance management framework used across all government agencies, including BARMM.11 It connects your individual work to your office’s targets and to the Bangsamoro Development Plan.

IPCR (Individual Performance Commitment and Review) – This is YOUR performance contract. At the beginning of each rating period, you and your supervisor agree on specific targets. At the end, you are evaluated against those targets.

OPCR (Office Performance Commitment and Review) – This is your office’s performance contract. Your office commits to specific outputs and outcomes for the year. Your IPCR targets should support the OPCR.

The annual cycle:

Month Activity Your Role
January Target-setting Meet with your supervisor. Set IPCR targets for the year. Align with OPCR.
July Mid-year review Submit mid-year accomplishment report. Discuss progress with your supervisor. Adjust targets if needed.
December Year-end evaluation Submit accomplishment report. Your supervisor rates your performance.

The 5-point rating scale:

Rating Score What It Means Consequence
Outstanding 5 Exceeded all targets significantly Priority for promotion, training, awards
Very Satisfactory 4 Met or exceeded most targets Eligible for PBB, strong promotion candidate
Satisfactory 3 Met basic targets Eligible for PBB, meets minimum requirement
Unsatisfactory 2 Failed to meet some targets Not eligible for PBB. Performance improvement plan.
Poor 1 Failed to meet most targets Grounds for non-renewal, demotion, or dismissal

Your SPMS rating determines: - PBB eligibility – you need at least “Satisfactory” AND your office must meet its OPCR - Promotion ranking – higher ratings give you an edge in the Comparative Assessment - Training priority – Outstanding performers get first pick of training opportunities - Step increment – satisfactory performance is required for your periodic salary increase

SALN: Statement of Assets, Liabilities, and Net Worth

SALN is a constitutional requirement.12 Every government official and employee must file one annually. The deadline is April 30.

  • Your first SALN: Filed within 30 days of your appointment
  • Annual SALN: Filed every year covering assets as of December 31 of the previous year
  • Last SALN: Filed within 30 days of separation from service

What to declare: all real property, personal property over PHP 50,000, liabilities, business interests, financial connections, and relatives in government within the 4th degree of consanguinity or affinity. See Chapter 16 for a complete SALN walkthrough.

Failure to file is grounds for dismissal. This is not optional.

Leave Management

Permanent employees earn: - 15 days vacation leave per year (earned monthly at 1.25 days)13 - 15 days sick leave per year (earned monthly at 1.25 days)14 - Maternity leave: 105 days (RA 11210, Expanded Maternity Leave Act)15 - Paternity leave: 7 days - Solo parent leave: 7 days - VAWC leave: 10 days (for victims of violence against women and children) - Special privilege leave: 3 days per year - Study leave: up to 6 months after 7 years of service (with supervisor endorsement) - Force leave: 5 days of your VL are mandatory (you must use them)

How to file leave: 1. Fill out the CSC Leave Application Form (CS Form 6) 2. Indicate the type of leave, dates, and reason 3. Submit to your supervisor for recommendation 4. HR processes the approval 5. For VL: file at least 5 days in advance. For SL: file within 5 days of return with medical certificate if applicable

COS and JO employees do NOT receive leave benefits. This is one of the strongest reasons to pursue a permanent appointment.

PDS: Personal Data Sheet

Keep your CS Form 212 updated. You will need it for every promotion, transfer, and appointment. When you complete a training, earn a degree, or change your personal information, update your PDS and submit the revised version to HR.

PRAISE and PBB

PRAISE (Program on Awards and Incentives for Service Excellence) is the government’s recognition framework.16 Your office should have a PRAISE committee that identifies and recognizes outstanding performance monthly, quarterly, and annually.

PBB (Performance-Based Bonus) is an annual cash bonus. To qualify: - Your office must meet its OPCR targets (at least “Satisfactory”) - You must individually meet your IPCR targets (at least “Satisfactory”) - Amount varies by performance tier: typically PHP 10,000-35,000

If your office fails its OPCR, no one in the office receives PBB – even if your individual performance was Outstanding. This creates shared accountability.

Step Increment

Your salary increases within your current Salary Grade through step increments. Each SG has 8 steps. You move to the next step every 3 years of continuous satisfactory service.17 This is an automatic salary increase – no competition required – as long as your SPMS ratings are satisfactory.

Government Benefits

Upon appointment, you are automatically enrolled in:

System What It Provides Your Contribution
GSIS Retirement pension, life insurance, disability benefits, salary loans Monthly premium deducted from salary
PhilHealth Health insurance coverage Monthly premium deducted from salary
Pag-IBIG Housing loan, savings program, calamity loan Monthly contribution deducted from salary

These deductions reduce your take-home pay but provide long-term security that private sector employment rarely matches.

14.5 Cultural Navigation

Working in the Bangsamoro government is not just about mastering systems and procedures. It is about navigating a cultural environment that may be different from what you experienced in school or in other workplaces.

Prayer Times and Prayer Rooms

Every BARMM office provides a prayer space. The five daily prayers (Fajr, Dhuhr, Asr, Maghrib, and Isha) are observed. During work hours, Dhuhr and Asr are the most relevant. Staff will briefly leave their desks to pray. This is normal, expected, and protected.

If you are Muslim: Coordinate with your supervisor so work continuity is maintained during prayer times. Most offices have informal rotating arrangements.

If you are not Muslim: Maintain quiet and respect during prayer times. Do not schedule urgent meetings during these periods. You will not be required to pray – but you are expected to create space for those who do.

Ramadan

Ramadan transforms the workplace. For approximately 30 days each year:

  • Adjusted work hours. Many BARMM offices shift to earlier schedules (e.g., 7:30 AM - 3:30 PM) to accommodate fasting employees.
  • Energy levels change. Fasting colleagues may have less physical energy, especially in the afternoon. Be considerate in scheduling demanding tasks.
  • Iftar (breaking of fast). Some offices organize collective iftar gatherings. Participating – even if you are not fasting – is a meaningful gesture of solidarity.
  • Heightened spiritual observance. The office atmosphere becomes more reflective. Respect this tone.

For non-fasting staff: Avoid eating conspicuously in front of fasting colleagues. Use the pantry or cafeteria. This is not a rule – it is respect.

Eid al-Fitr and Eid al-Adha

These are the two major Islamic holidays. BARMM offices close on these days. Learn the appropriate greeting: “Eid Mubarak” (Blessed Eid). For non-Muslim staff, saying “Eid Mubarak” to your colleagues is appreciated and appropriate.

Bangsamoro Hospitality and Workplace Culture

  • Food sharing is common. When a colleague shares food, accepting is a sign of respect.
  • Respect for elders is deeply valued. Address senior colleagues with appropriate honorifics. “Sir” and “Ma’am” are baseline; “Ama” (father/elder) and “Ina” (mother/elder) are used in some groups.
  • Clan and family networks are real. Your colleague’s cousin may work in another ministry. Treat every professional interaction with integrity because reputation travels through networks.
  • Pakikisama (getting along) and hiya (social propriety) operate here as they do elsewhere in the Philippines, with the added dimension of Islamic and Bangsamoro cultural values.

Working Across Ethnolinguistic Groups

BARMM has 13 ethnolinguistic groups: Meranaw, Maguindanaon, Tausug, Sama, Yakan, Iranun, Kalibugan (from Zamboanga Peninsula), Palawanon, Molbog, Jama Mapun, Badjao (Sama Dilaut), Sangil, and Teduray. Plus settler communities (Visayan, Ilocano, Tagalog) and indigenous peoples.

Your office may include colleagues from several of these groups. Here is how to navigate:

  • Do not assume all Bangsamoro are the same. A Tausug colleague and a Meranaw colleague may have very different customs, languages, and perspectives.
  • Learn basic greetings in your colleagues’ languages. Even “Salamat” (thank you) in Meranaw or Maguindanaon signals respect.
  • Ask about customs you do not understand. Genuine curiosity is always appreciated. Assumptions are not.
  • Language in meetings: Official business is conducted in English and Filipino. But informal conversations often happen in local languages. If you feel excluded, politely ask colleagues to include you. Most will.

“I Am Not Muslim” – Your Place in a Muslim-Majority Workplace

If you are not Muslim and you work in BARMM government, here is what you need to know:

  • You are welcome. The BOL guarantees the rights of all communities in the Bangsamoro, including settler communities (Article IX, Section 15)18 and indigenous peoples (Article IX, Section 3).19 The 12 Pillars of Moral Governance include inclusivity as a core value.
  • You will not be required to practice Islam. No one will ask you to pray, fast, or wear Islamic clothing.
  • You will be expected to respect Islamic governance values. Amanah (trustworthiness), adl (justice), shura (consultation), rahmah (compassion), and sidq (truthfulness) are universal values. Respecting them does not require changing your faith.
  • The greeting “Assalamu Alaikum” means “peace be upon you.” It is appropriate for anyone to use. The response is “Wa Alaikum Assalam” (and upon you, peace). Using it signals respect and cultural awareness.
  • Non-Muslim staff serve in every part of BARMM government – including the Parliament Secretariat, ministries, commissions, and LGUs. Your competence, not your faith, determines your career.

14.6 Ten Mistakes New Staff Make (And How to Avoid Them)

Every mistake on this list has been made by real government employees in their first year. Learn from their experience.

Mistake 1: Not Reading the BOL

You work for the only autonomous region in the Philippines – a government created by Republic Act 11054 (the Bangsamoro Organic Law).20 If you have not read at least the key provisions of the BOL, you do not fully understand the institution you serve. Read Articles IV-V (powers), Article VII (the Parliament), Article IX (basic rights), Article X (justice system), and Article XII (fiscal autonomy).

Mistake 2: Assuming National Government Rules Apply

BARMM has its own Civil Service Code (BAA 17),21 its own Administrative Code (BAA 13),22 its own Budget System Act (BAA 84),23 its own Education Code (BAA 18),24 and its own Local Governance Code (BAA 49).25 These may differ from national rules. When in doubt, check the BAA – not the national law.

Mistake 3: Not Learning the Approval Workflow

Every document in government follows a routing chain. A memo does not go directly from your desk to the Minister. It goes through your supervisor, your Division Chief, possibly a Director, and then to the head of office. Learn this chain on Day 1. Skipping it – even once – erodes trust.

Mistake 4: Waiting to Be Told What to Do

Government rewards initiative. If you see a problem, propose a solution. If you finish your assignment early, ask for the next one. If you notice a process that could be improved, write a brief memo suggesting improvements. Your supervisor will notice the difference between a staff member who waits and one who acts.

Mistake 5: Submitting Incomplete CSW

The “Completed” in Complete Staff Work means completed. No missing attachments. No placeholder data. No sections marked “TBD.” No forwarding a problem without a recommendation. Before you submit any work, ask yourself: “If my supervisor signs this right now, will there be any unanswered question?” If the answer is yes, the work is not complete.

Mistake 6: Not Knowing Your Supervisor’s Communication Style

Some supervisors want detailed daily updates by email. Others want a weekly verbal report. Some prefer printed documents. Others work entirely through shared drives. Some like formal “Sir/Ma’am” address. Others prefer first names. Ask your supervisor directly in your first week. Getting this wrong creates friction that has nothing to do with the quality of your work.

Mistake 7: Avoiding the Cultural Context

You are in the Bangsamoro. Learn the greetings. Learn the prayer times. Learn the cultural calendar. Attend the iftar. Say “Eid Mubarak” on Eid. If you treat the cultural context as something to endure rather than something to embrace, you will always be an outsider – regardless of how technically competent you are.

Mistake 8: Skipping Training Opportunities

Every training you attend is recorded and counts toward your competency profile for promotion. Training accounts for 15% of the Comparative Assessment score when you compete for your next position.26 Accept every training offered in your first year. Attend free CSC webinars. Complete online courses. Build your training hours aggressively.

Mistake 9: Not Connecting Your Work to BDP Goals

Your IPCR should reference the Bangsamoro Development Plan. When you write a project proposal, cite which BDP goal it supports. When you draft a briefer, note the relevant BDP chapter. When you present to your team, connect your division’s output to the BDP framework. This demonstrates strategic awareness – a competency that separates staff who get promoted from staff who get passed over.

Mistake 10: Thinking This Is Just a Job

You are not working for a regular bureaucracy. You are implementing the Comprehensive Agreement on the Bangsamoro – a peace agreement that ended decades of armed conflict. Every program you run, every policy you draft, every barangay you serve is a peace dividend. The transition period is when credibility is built or lost. If the government delivers, trust deepens. If it fails, the peace process is undermined. Your daily work is the frontline of this test.

Treat it accordingly.

Actionable Tool: “First 30 Days Planner”

Use this planner to track your onboarding progress. Review it weekly with your supervisor.

Week Tasks Status
Day 1 Oath of office, HR processing, government ID, system access ☐ Done
Day 1-3 Meet supervisor, receive position description, office tour ☐ Done
Week 1 Read MOP, study org chart, learn filing system, attend first staff meeting ☐ Done
Week 1-2 First supervisor check-in: expectations, assignments, communication style ☐ Done
Week 2 Enroll in GSIS, PhilHealth, Pag-IBIG (confirm with HR) ☐ Done
Week 2-3 Complete first CSW assignment using ADDRESS IT ☐ Done
Week 3 File first SALN (within 30 days of appointment) ☐ Done
Week 3-4 Attend government orientation seminar (if scheduled) ☐ Done
Day 30 Complete the “By Day 30” checklist in Section 14.2 ☐ Done

Supervisor review date: ___

Notes from supervisor: _________
